The Red Page
The Wonderful Wells
The World Set Free (Macmillan) is a
happy return to the fantastic and imagina-
tive romances that first made famous the
name of H. G. Wells. But this stirring
story of the future has within it the sociolo-
gical purpose of Wells’s recurrent dreams of
Utopia. And though the book may be read
either for its adventure and its delightful
incidental studies in character, or for its
sociological forecast, the unity of the dual
theme is brilliantly kept throughout. The
world is set free by the discovery of a means
for using the tremendous energy locked up
within the atom —the greatest discovery that
the future holds for mankind. The disin-
tegration of radium opens that marvellous
possibility for us. If a key should be found
to unlock the store of that energy of the
atom, the world would be changed for us
out of recognition. It is this fascinating
problem that Wells postulates as solved;
and then he is able to. indicate, with bril-
liant imagination, the extraordinary results
of this cheap and unlimited energy. But
his forecast does not depend upon the solu-
tion of a problem that may prove insoluble.
Wells contends that without the invention
of the atomic engine the conditions he por-
trays would come—in time. He merely
suggests that the coming of the millennium
will be hastened by some such invention.
“A quarter of a million years ago the
utmost man was a savage.... Over most of
the great wildernesses of earth you would
have sought him in vain; only in a few
temperate and sub-tropical river valleys
would you have found the squatting lairs of
his little herds, a male, a few females, a
child or so.. . .He fled the cave-bear over the
rocks full of iron ore and the promise of
sword and spear; he froze to death upon a
ledge of coal; he drank water muddy with
the clay that would one day make cups of
porcelain ; he chewed the ear of wild wheat
he had plucked and gazed with a dim specu-
lation in his eyes at the birds that soared
beyond his reach... .Yet he changed....”
But suppose, instead of the decay by
driblets that radium shows, there was a
decay en masse? Suppose we find presently
that we can quicken that decay?
“Every scrap of solid matter in the world
would become an available reservoir of con-
centrated force.... It would mean a change
in human conditions that I can only com-
pare to the discovery of fire, that first dis-
covery that lifted man above the brute. We
stand to-day towards radio-activity exactly
as our ancestor stood towards fire before he
had learnt to make it. He knew it then
only as a strange thing utterly beyond his
control, a flare on the crest of the volcano,
a red destruction that poured through the
forest... .Just when it is becoming appa-
rent that our ever-increasing needs cannot
be borne indefinitely by our present sources
of energy, we discover suddenly the possi-
bility of an entirely new civilisation. The
energy we need for our very existence, and
which Nature supplies us still so grudgingly,
is in reality locked up in inconceivable quan-
tities all about us. We cannot pick that
lock at present, but —we wi11.... Then that
perpetual struggle for existence, that per-
petual struggle to live on the bare surplus
of Nature’s energies will cease to be the lot
of Man. Man will sten from the pinnacle
of this civilisation to the beginning of the
next.”
It is in 1933 that Wells prophesies the
invention of the atomic engine. The in-
ventor set up atomic disintegration in a
minute particle of bismuth.... and a by-
product of the process was gold. Easily
recoverable, too. With the perfection of
the engine, power became absurdly cheap.
“In three years the frightful armored mon-
sters that had hooted and smoked and thun-
dered about the world for four awful decades
were swept away to the dealers in old metal,
and the highways thronged with light and
clean and shimmering shapes of silvered
steel”; and the problem of the helicopter
was solved. The industrial world was revo-
lutionised ; credit smashed; millions of
workers were thrown out of employment.
Humanity had been disastrously caught
unawares.
But one terrible invention brought matters
to a crisis. This was the atomic bomb.
This engine of destruction, depending for its
power upon the release of the energy of the
atom, is a brilliant fantasy of Wells’s scien-
tific mind. An atomic bomb, once launched,
burnt like a volcano for years, a monstrous
cavern of fiery energy, destroying everything
for miles around. So Wells graphically tells
of the Last War, in which the old methods
of fighting are futile against the destruc-
tion caused by these bombs. In this war
the capitals of the world are utterly de-
stroyed and remain unapproachable craters
of destruction. Canberra, not being men-
tioned, was apparently not thought worth a
bomb. All the powers went to war in a
delirium of panic, in order to use their bombs
first. By 1959 the world was slipping head-
long to anarchy. Government was at an
end. But one man brought about a confer-
ence of ex-rulers and notable men, and that
committee set stolidly to work to evolve the
regulations for a new civilisation of common-
sense. First they got control of the appar-
atus for making the atomic bombs; and
though the King of the Balkans made a des-
perate attempt to become a world-Csesar, the
adventure ended in his death.
It is a new, clean world that this commit-
tee evolve. There are no pills in it, no flies,
no old clothes Wells is always interesting
when he enumerates his New-Utopian dis-
tastes. The old capitals are beyond restora-
tion .. besides, in the new world, nobody
wants the restoration of anything old. This
latter part of the novel is of great interest.
Wells’s picture of the millennium is extraor-
dinarily inspiring, finely sane, firmly founded
on sociological demands. But he posits a
too-sudden change in the old Adam. Out of
that world-destruction something groping and
clumsy would surely with difficulty have
risen, not this vision of a scientific common-
sense Utopia. The rhythm of evolution is
slow. The great catastrophes that have
ended the civilisations of the past have flung
the race back into long periods of barbarism,
out of the chasms of which Man has had
heavily to crawl back into the light. But
Wells would have us believe that from this
universal decay a new civilisation suddenly
and gloriously flowered by the mere passing
of resolutions of a committee, supported
though they were by the control of all the
offensive force. Human nature cannot so
swiftly and so wonderfully emerge from the
chrysalis stage.
But even Wells admits that in his new
garden of Eden there remains a serpent. In
this world of science and sanity there still is
—Woman, the snake. Woman persists in
remaining woman, somewhat to Wells’s exas-
peration.
“It is you poets, with your love songs,
which turn the sweet fellowship of comrades
into this woman-centred excitement. But
there is something in women, in many women,
which responds to these provocations; they
succumb to a peculiarly self-cultivating ego-
tism. They become the subjects of their
own artistry. They develop and elaborate
themselves as scarcely any man would do. . . .
I have been reading in the old papers of the
movements to emancipate women that were
going on before the discovery of atomic force.
These things, which began with a desire to
escape from the limitations and the servitude
of sex, ended in an inflamed assertion of sex,
and women more heroines than ever. Helen
of Holloway was at last as big a nuisance in
her way as Helen of Troy, and so long as you
think of yourselves as women, instead of
thinking of yourselves as intelligent beings,
you will be in danger of —Helenism. To think
of yourselves as women is to think of your-
selves in relation to men. You have to learn
to think of yourselves—for our sakes and
your own sakes—in relation to the sun and
stars. You have to cease to be our adven-
ture, and come with us upon our adventures.”
We 11.... will y0u.... ever?
A brilliant book without a hero or heroine,
yet full of the real romance.
Sydney and the Other Woman
By DOWELL O'REILLY.
To know Sydney, you must know her by
heart, and that requires a big heart. She is
a beautiful coquette —everything she ought
not to be, and so delightful for that very
reason. She flouts our common sense, but
her appeal. to our finer senses is at once
impudent and irresistible.
Sydney is the Garden of Paradise after
the goats got in. Her moist winds are heavy
with all the odors of Araby and her camels.
An enchanted city, where black clouds of
battle-dust smother the lurid sunset, as the
whirling “southerly” stabs the “black
north-easter.” And on her harbor the light
that never was on land or sea glimmers
where opaline headlands float in morning mist
and broken sunshine.
Her faults really do not matter : faults
never do. When all her youthful indiscre-
tions are forgotten she will be beautiful as
ever —and just as indiscreet. Beauty alone
counts. Corruption is swallowed up in cor-
ruption. Tarquin stabs Tarquin to death,
while Lucrece leads Lucrece where her shame
is glory and her tears are stars. Sin
is the red rose of a perfect dawn, and horror
itself but beauty in the womb —O dew-washed
sky, and hush of night, and everlast-
ing stars —or are they tea«rs?—and rain-
bowed earth and white passion of the sea,
and secret soul of man that dwells alone
in his high place, and you—milk-white vic-
tim of Love’s eternal sacrifice, garlanded with
childlike flowers led captive by flowerlike
children, not till all your glory and loveli-
ness have passed away will the Immortals
cease to dwell with men.
Ugliness has never a chance against
beauty—even suffragette beauty. Man
knows! So he takes the myth of “The
Great Woman Movement” very philosophic-
ally, knowing that the one law that woman
cannot break with impunity is the law of
Beauty. Her place and power will for ever
depend not on “man-made law” but on the
irrevocable verdict of a full-length mirror.
She knows! The wildest of wild women
has never yet with sacrilegious hands shat-
tered her own bedroom looking-glass. On
the low plane of physical violence the suf-
fragettes’ highest ambition is to die beauti-
fully. But every carnal coquette dyes beau-
tifully, and —what is more to the purpose,
lives on to perpetuate the species —her
species. As an economic argument suicide
has no chance against peroxide. It is so
much easier to fool a man than to fight him.
To bring Asquith to his knees the suffragettes
must give him the glad eye—not a black one.
Every woman knows this in her triumphant
heart, and all she knows in her pathetic
little brain will never count for tuppence
in shaping the world’s destiny. To change
the complexion of Society she must use pow-
der —not gunpowder. And change it she
will, in her old adorable way—what though
the smoke of frenzied curling-tongs reek to
Heaven, and man-made gutters run with
rouge!
Only Beauty goes on, and all revolt
against Beauty .goes down, and out, and
under. As garden-phosohates—bone-dust
well worked in—dank evil is ever purged
back to clean earth, and nourishes the
beauty it lived to destroy.
Australian Fiction
The Australian novelist is ever with us.
Here is the latest batch of his work, all, with
one exception, unfortunately third-rate in
quality; all, in fact, of a standard that would
not, but for their Australian nativity, receive
notice on this page. The exception is the
charming,, unpretentious narrative of N. F.
Spielvogel. ,
Lindsay Russell, author of Souls in Pawn
and Smouldering Fires, has but one _ recipe
for novel-writing—a recipe for popularity of
a kind. The Years of Forgetting (Ward,
Lock) consists of the usual mixture the
wicked R.C. priest, the betrayed woman, the
illegitimate angel che-ild. Hall-Corelli
has proved the palatability of the dear old
religion-sex rechauffe. Lindsay Russell
shows considerable ingenuity in the
construction of her melodramatic plot,
but her style is sickly, her dialogue banal.
On its plane, however, it is quite equal to the
sort of work turned out by industrious
feminine exploiters of this vein of interest in
other lands.
J*
Tullie C. Wollaston, in The Spirit of the
Child (Lothian, 55.), honestly loves Aus-
tralia. Her llowery and prosy appreciations
of the boronia, the white gum, the magpie
and the black opal are interesting, but wheu
she interprets the boronia as humility, the
white gum as aspiration, the magpie as praise,
and the black opal as hope, her nature studies
become sermons. The romance she writes
is stilted and sloppy. She hangs texts upon
the eucalyptus, linds morals in the magpie
and platitudes in everythina. Most of the
book is written in a kind of prose, the rest is
written in a kind of verse. Neither is dis-
tinguished for anything but its highly moral
outlook. The publisher has shrined this
imitation gem in a gorgeous casket. The
color-photographs of black opals are brilliant
examples of three-tone printing. The Spirit of
the Child is suitable, one might say, for the
children of poor but wowser parents.
d*
The Schemers, by George A. Taylor (Build-
ing Ltd., Grosvenor-street, Sydney), re-
minds one of the sensational publications of
the late William Stead. The style is jour-
nalese ; the plot is melodrama; the 20 full-
page illustrations are by George A. Taylor.
The story revolves round the competition
for the plan of the prize design for the Can-
berra Parliament Buildings, the building of
a vast cathedral on Flagstaff Hill, the clos-
ing down of superfluous suburban churches,
and the consequent enrolment of the dis-
carded parsons in the ranks of trades-union-
ism, the exploitation of genius by a despic-
able member of parliament and a cunning
United States company promoter, and the
painting of masterpieces by a great Sydney
artist. The masterpieces are not only de-
scribed : they figure- among the illustrations
by George A. Taylor. Fortunately it is not
necessary for us to criticise these master-
pieces. The editor of the Boston Arena has
done this for us. He told George A. Tay-
lor, and George A. Taylor considerately tells
us, that “In these pictures great, solemn
truths and suggestive lessons are pictured in
an impressive manner,” and the Boston edi-
tor will be “surprised and disappointed if
this artist does not become more and more
a power for human emancipation and pro-
gress.” The good characters in G_eorge A.
Taylor’s preposterous story. enthusiastically
agree with the Boston editor. Evidently
human emancipation and progress are in for
a real good time. We wish them well.
Pleasantly, patriotically and piquantly
N. F. Spielvogel writes of unknown Victoria
in The Gumsucker at Home (George Robert-
son, C.A.) : “After my Wander Year, the
story of which is told in The Gumsucker on
the Tramp, I returned to the simple life in
Dimboola. I, contentedly, went my way,
taught school, played tennis, and read books
for four years ; then the Wanderlust again,
and the idea of exploring the Cabbage Gar-
den. A job as relieving officer of the Educa-
tion Department afforded the means. It is
a delightful picture of the diversity of Aus-
tralian life and conditions that Spielvogel
unfolds in his account of outback and little-
known settlements, each with its character-
istic and different industry, each with its own
individuality of setting and outlook. The
humor is genial, but sometimes forced ; the
love for, and appreciation of, Australia is on
every page.
A Bachelor's Wife (N.S.W. Bookstall,
Is.), by M. Forrest, begins with a forlorn
girl weaping in a bachelor’s house at night,
and ends, as one expects it to end, with the
Wedding March. This story of a penniless
girl who has the usual experiences in the
various “situations” she takes, is pleasantly
written by an Australian author who is
more herself in her poems.
The latest shilling reprints of novels are:
Lewis Rand, by Mary Johnston; and The
Ghosts of Piccadilly, by G. S. Street (Con-
stable).
With the mention of Turgeniev, Gogol,
Tolstoy and Dostoevsky we are apt to think
we have exhausted the list of Russian novel-
ists. The truth is, as the Times recently
pointed out, that these are merely the Rus-
sian classics. There is another list of diffi-
cult names to learn if we hope to keep
abreast of modern Russian literature —
authors whose works clamor to be translated
into English. There are: Ghekhof, whom we
already have in translation as a dramatist;
Kouprin, the Russian Kipling; Gorky
(already translated in part) ; Andreef; the
fantastic Sologub; the writer of
beautiful tales; Beily; Bunnin, the Russian
Hardy. Ghekhof is the most beloved of Rus-
sians; after him the most popular is Koup-
rin, author of 10 volumes of astonishingly
clever and touching stories. Gorky s talents
have been killed by exile. The Red Laugh.,
by Andreef, has been published by Bane.
Sologub is a remarkably gifted and eccentric
writer of tales. Of the names that have
appeared within the last 15 years, Artsiba-
slief is the most remarkable. His book,
Kanin, swept intelligent Russia as no other
book has swept any country. It was ulti-
mately confiscated by the Government. it
is an ugly piece of work about men and
women, and has been suggested to several
English publishers and review-editors, but
nobody dare print it. The English are not
to that extent pre-occupied with the sexual
question, and public opinion could not save
such a volume from the police. Russian
publishers are already richer than English.
Their millions of readers are being added to
every year by the diffusion of education
among the peasants. It is time the trans-
lators got busy.
W. L. George’s search for the rising novel-
ist in England has led to a quest made for
that phenomenon in the United States. A
writer in JL Y. Bookman fails to find any
hope for the United States novel of the
future. The awful incubus on the serious
young United States novelist is his heavy
didactism. The fiction-writer’s art is merged in
pedagogy. The didactic novel may be fertile
as a source of ethical inspiration. It is none
the less sterile as a work of art. The U.S.
novelist is preaching, not telling a story.
Among serious novelists Winston Churchill s
The inside of the Gup and Mrs. Whartons
The Custom of the Country both point a
moral rather than adorn a tale. And
Harrison’s U.K.'s Eyes is smothered _in
didactism. Other preachers are Herrick,
Miss Johnston, London, Sinclair, Albert
Edward, Ellen Glasgow and King. In
Harrison, Upton Sinclair and Jack Bondon
the writer finds the three men under 4U
who represent the direction taken by
the U.S. novel. Rex Beach and Stewart
Edward White are going backward. London,
who has never got away from himself, be-
comes hopelessly didactic in The Valley of
the Moon. Sinclair shows the same inability
to escape the incubus in Sylvia. _ And
Harrison’s U.K.’s Eyes is merely a sociologi-
cal tract. The land of the wooden ham in-
evitably produces the wooden novel.
The second edition of C. A. Sussmilch’s
Introduction to the Geology of N.S. TU.
(Angus and Robertson, Sydney, 7s. 6d.) is
practically a new book. It has been revised
and much enlarged, and in its present form
is complete and authoritative. The large
number of illustrations, especially the photo-
graphs of geological features taken in situ,
render the work of extreme interest to the
average reader.

There are two popular Winchester .22 Caliber
Repeating Rifles, either one of which gives
first class satisfaction for target shooting or hunting
small game. They are the Model 1903 .22 Automatic
and the Model 1906. The first, as its name implies, is
reloaded by recoil. After its 10-shot magazine is filled
and the rifle once loaded, the only effort required to
shoot it is to aim and pull the trigger. On account of the
ease and novelty of its operation, there is no end of fun
shooting this rifle. It’s the peer of all .225. The Model 1906
is a handsome, well-made, fine-shooting, smooth-wcrking gun.
It is a handy little piece which has no equal in gun value. It
has a 20-inch, round barrel, fitted with sporting front and ad-
justable rear sights. It shoots .22 Short, .22 Long and .22
Long Rifle Cartridges, which permits the use of light ammu-
nition for target work and heavier cartridges for game shoot-
ing. These rifles are Take-Downs, and come apart easily
and quickly and can be packed in a small compass.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Australian Autumn.
Me hardly know how fierce the Summer was.
Though fires have burnt the saplings red and
brown.
Swept through the bush and scorched the
withered grass,
And tumbled many a forest giant down,
For now March rains have fallen, and the
earth
Exults once more that late looked cere and
dead.
Exults with wonder of her second birth;
Life laughs, and fleecy clouds float overhead,
And Autumn comes with soft, green April
days
Pointed with dew, and misty moonlit nights;
\\ hile, by the fire, we watch the first logs
blnze.
And think we’re old, and dream of past de-
lights.
Loo perfect is the present hour, and so
We store its treasures for the days to be;
And then look back, though loath to let it
go,
Greedy for joys of future memory.
The sky is blue above the dim hilltops,
The creeks are chanting gipsy rhapsodies ;
Ploughed lands run side by side with spring-
ing crops,
The apples redden on the orchard trees.
The bracken glitters in a silver mist;
Hid in the fern-tree gully, drenched with
dew,
A spider’s web, a gleam of amethyst,
Swings, bough to bough, the mottled hazels
through.
Huge trees, by bush-fires burnt, lift blackened
boughs
Gaily embroidered with fresh, budding green;
The milkmaid calls the lazy-moving cows,
The birds round many a kitchen door are
seen.
Navvies are breaking stones along the road
To help the farmer’s cart through muddy
ways;
A team of bullocks strains with swaying load
Of mighty logs for coming Winter days.
Our little village dozes on the hill ;
And in the store the old wives never cease
To gossip over tales of good and ill;
While old men sit and smoke their pipes in
peace.
Season of respite from the fire and drought,
Season of calm, with Summer’s story told,
Seasou of beauty, when the stars are out,
The Autumn harmony of blue and gold,—
Season of joy, and sweeter than the Spring,
With old folk dreaming of the days gone by,
As we shall sit, some day, remembering
The music and the firelight, you and I !
And now the landscape waits the morning
sun,
The birds are singing by a hundred rills;
And little wanderers, shouting as they run.
Are gathering mushrooms on the grassy hills.
VICTORIA. .
LOUIS ESSON.
Lilian.
On, or near, accused the police found 10 large
firelighters, three flasks of paraffin oil, an electric
torch, a pocket-knife, a handbag containing about
141 b. of coal, each piece rolled up in paper, two
boxes of vestas, a roll of cotton-wool, a five-cham-
bered revolver loaded with blank cartridge, a map
of the Glasgow district, a bottle of perfume, several
overcoats and waterproofs, and a man’s cap.
—Suffragette item.
Airy, fairy Lilian —
Moonlight-flitting Lilian—
When they asked her what she wanted.
With her club she struck, undaunted,
At a bobby man.
She’ll not tell them what she wanted—
Naughty little Lilian !
When the owner seeks
Reasons for her act,
She, leaping wildly at him,
With her snickersnee would pat him—
Well for him it was he gat him!
Listen to her shrieks !
So innocent, so simple-cunning
Seems she —-till the oil is running!
Till the conflagrations rise !
You would say: “She's only funning.”
But you get a real surprise
When she scratches at your eyes!
When the wardress strips her dress—
Gentle little Lilian!
Everybody must confess
She is pretty thorough—Yes !
One in half a million;
For they find upon, or near, her
Things that keep on getting queerer—
Things that simply make you fear her—
Dainty little Lilian!
First a pistol; next a torch—
Three oil-cans (inside the porch)
Goal and matches, and a dagger—•
Good thing the police did bag her!
Hairpins, chewing-gum, fire-lighters—
Lip-salve of vermilion—-
Bonniest of Women’s-Righters—
Airy, fairy Lilian !
Poor old John Bull’s full of trouble !
Just now he is getting double,
Thanks to fairy Lilian!
On his right, the Arson Squad ;
On his left, the Carson Squad;
Back and front, the Parson Squad;
Care rides on John’s pillion !
For. alas ! —’tis sad to say—
Half the women of to-day
Seem to be distraught, or fey!
Whether bred in hall or skillion.
They “go on” like naughty Lilian,
Who has forfeited the benison
Once bestowed by Alfred Tennyson
In verse quite Virgilian:
Ay di mi!
The maid we see
Is ire-y
Fiery
Lilian!
N.S.W.

WHY YOU SHOULD USE
REX LUNCHEON CHEESE
What is cheese? It is strange how seldom we turn to the dictionary for
definitions of common, daily-used terms. Can you define Cheese, Mrs.
Housewife? Do you know how it is made?
Cheese is the casein, or curd, of milk, coagulated, pressed and allowed to
dry in a mould. This literally defines
“REX” Luncheon Cheese
Defines its composition, its purity. But it doesn’t define its characteristics
—its betterness. “Bex” Luncheon Cheese is the result of most careful
preparation.'
The cheeses are carefully selected by us and properly matured without being
strong—for new cheese is always indigestible. Every cheese must come up to
these requirements—any that do not are discarded.
“Rex” Luncheon Cheese is of the proper consistency and richness, and can
be spread without butter. It is therefore valuable from an economical
point, also. You should use “Rex” Luncheon Cheese for its value as a
food —its nutritive value —as well as for its cheapness and the time it saves
you. Try it to-day.

How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the
paper (and, to an extent, themselves ) by taking some
care as to methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, ad-
vertisements, etc.) should in no case be addressed to
the Editor, but to the Manager. To send them to
the Editor is to invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone by name. “The Editor, The Bulletin,” is
the proper superscription for “copy.” Delay, and
even loss, is sometimes caused by addressing to per-
sons instead of officials. The person may not be
there; the official always is.
Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be re-
fused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex-
pressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read, and, where prac-
ticable, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” col-
umn all contributions submitted, whether in the form
of Political, Social or other Articles, Verse, Short
Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Australian sub-
jects, and not exceeding two columns in length, or,
say, SOOO words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters or Newspaper Clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these
conditions are observed. When MS. is forwarded
without stamps for return it will be understood that
the copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Per-
sonal applications for return of MS. cannot receive
attention. The Bulletin desires, further, those
sending MS. to distinctly understand that it is not
responsible for the preservation or transmission of
MS.

THURSDAY, JULY 9, 1914,
ABOUT A CLEAN-SHAVED YOUNG
MAN IN A HURRY.
The word “scab” is not easily defined, but
in the way of general acceptation it implies
a person who goes back on Trades Union
principles. The present N.S.W. Govern-
ment is supported by the trades unions, and
stands for a policy which was formulated
by their representatives, so when it turns
backslider or somersaulter on its professed
policy, it is presumably some variety of a
political scab. It is difficult to see that
it can be anything else. On a recent day
Premier Holman delivered the policy of his
Government for next session, and it was so
utterly different from the policy on which
Labor first attained to office that the two
schemes wouldn’t recognise each other if they
happened to meet in the busy mart. Yet
an amazing feature of the business is that
the party which supports Holman and co.
seems to have not the slightest objection to
the political lies and treachery which now
make up its daily fare. All the promises
that the public accounts would be made to
really and honestly balance; that borrowing
would be reduced, that a general sinking
fund would be established, that loan money
would be used only for works which returned
full interest and something over, that the
foolish old Upper House would be wiped
out, that something genuine and vigorous
would be done in the way of decentralisa-
tion —all these are still proving of no more
value than an exploded paper bag. And the
boasted Arbitration policy, as a remedy for
strikes, has been practically abandoned, for
everybody strikes when he feels that way
inclined, and nobody of the crowd which
supports Holman takes any notice of
Arbitration Courts unless it suits him to do
so. Yet so long as the party now in power
enjoys such amazing and unaccountable luck
at second ballots it is difficult to see how
it can be shifted, no matter how virulently
it may turn its back on its alleged pro-
gramme.
* * * *
The Holman Government has a great pull
in the half-hearted support and the less
than half-hearted opposition of the Tory daily
papers of Sydney. Sydney is a great city,
and the people of N.S.W. put a great value
on it. It is a city owned by private land-
lords, and the unearned increment of it goes
to private landlords. In the interests of
the community it might seem worth while
to have four Sydneys instead of one, and
there are three ports, at present shut off
from commerce and settlement by the lack
of roads and railways, at which three new
Sydneys might arise. At these ports the
State might be ground landlord, and the
ground rents and the unearned increment
might provide a revenue that would go a
long way to shift the public burden. Also
the prosperity of the adjacent places might
be greatly multiplied, for many fertile dis-
tricts of the State are losing population, and
giving up wheat, and perishing in the matter
of cattle and sheep and pigs, simply because
they are so far from Sydney that trade
doesn’t pay. The Holman Government,
by way of pretending to pursue its
alleged decentralisation policy, threatens to
make a new outlet —not at Port Stephens
or Jervis Bay or Twofold Bay, where there
are real possibilities—but at the hopelesss
open roadstead of Coff’s Harbor, which is
worth about the price of an old hat. So
it throws five stones at one fowl in a manner
of speaking: (1) It avoids any unpleasant
struggle to support its policy of State owner-
ship of land; (2) it avoids doing anything
large and embarrassing in the way of settle-
ment; (3) it doesn’t annoy the rich and
influential landlords of Sydney; (4) it
doesn’t get out of the old rut, and getting out
of the old rut is a strenuous business; and
(5) it doesn’t quarrel with the important
Tory press whose chief interests lie in the big
city. So long as the Holman Government
doesn’t break in on the commercial and manu-
facturing monopoly of the one unreasonably-
bloated city, the Tory press of that city isn’t
likely to oppose it in anything like dead
earnest, even if the results of the second
ballot look somewhat improbable and the
£4 a day paid to ex-Minister Nielsen seemed
to make that one-time supporter a bloated
capitalist and a notable variety of absentee.
* * * *
The programme for next session is no
small affair. It includes the Sydney-North
Shore bridge, which has been talked about to
no purpose for more than 50 years. This
is a work to which no possible objection can
be taken, except that it is apparently to be
built with borrowed money, and the bridge
will wear out much faster than the loan.
Then there is something about an under-
ground railway system for Sydney, which is
also essential, but unhappily it is also to
be built with borrowed money, and nothing
is said about any possible or probable repay-
ment thereof. Fourteen new railways are
in course of construction, with borrowed
money, and they all point towards the city
which is already so congested that it can’t
deal in any satisfactory way with the traffic
that reaches it now. The work of
duplicating the railways which lead into
and out of the city is to be pushed
forward vigorously with borrowed money,
regardless of the fact that the exist-
ing railways have ceased to be a payable
proposition. The miserably unsatisfactory
port which leads its silted existence along-
side the private-landlord-owned town of New-
castle is to have more good loan cash spent
upon it. There are altogether about 70
propositions in the programme. Half of
them represent a razzle of borrowing—one
before which every previous razzle will pale
its' ineffective fires. The other half consist
mostly of social reforms which, if done with
a rush and charged up all at once to a very
small population, would beggar even a highly
efficient Treasurer. What the effect will be on
the present inefficient crowd, with its mar-
vellous gift of getting one “bob’s” worth of
service for 2s. 6d., can hardly be imagined.
Again, if the schemes are not mere window-
dressing—if they are to be carried out within
any reasonable space of time —they must be
thrown together in the helter-skelter fashion
which provides much profit for the legal pro-
fession and much wild astonishment for the
community. Even the greatest artist seldom
turns out 70 masterpieces in a year—or at
least he doesn’t sell 69 of them before they
are painted—and the Clean-Shaved Young
Man in a Hurry who is at the head of affairs
in Sydney, hasn’t yet been mistaken for the
greatest possible artist.
* * * *
When the Millennium arrives at full speed
the day after to-morrow —when it enters
breathless by reason of haste, and with its
collar burst because of velocity—this is
some of the luggage it is to bring along
with it. The barbarous old death penalty
is to be abolished. This at least is a good
thing: and even the most foolish and
hurried Government can hardly draft its
Bill so badly as to provide in mistake that
the criminal will be suspended by the feet
instead of not being suspended at all. There
is to be a Greater Sydney Council at last;
some more Arbitration; a Building Act;
four different Mining Bills; Police Appeal
Bill; Railway Servants Appeal Bill; Bills
dealing with explosives, motor traffic; and
the careless habit of pointing pea-rifles at
strangers. Also a new Civil Service Super-
annuation measure is to eventuate, though
whether it will or will not be prefaced by a
repayment to the older civil servants of the
money which the former Superannuation
Fund so artfully pushed them out of doesn’t
appear. The insolvency of that ancient
Fund was so persistently advertised that
many officials, believing the provision for
their old age to be almost certainly gone
after years of compulsory contribution, were
ready to compromise at almost any price.
And the price they got was permission to
leave off throwing their contributions at what
seemed to be a dead horse, and a promise that
the money they had actually paid in would
be refunded with simple interest at 3 per
cent., when they retired or died. And many
old men are still waiting to get their own
money back with simple interest at 3 per
cent. The non-payable housing schemb at
Daceyville is to be extended —whether on
payable or non-payable lines doesn’t appear.
State ironworks and State manufacture of
agricultural implements are promised, and
but for the Holman Administration’s
lamentable tendency to run everything at a
loss, nothing could be more commendable.
More education is to happen, more hospitals,
more bush nursing, more irrigation, bulk
handling of wheat, more immigration, reform
of the State Legislature, research into such
matters as cancer, consumption and syphilis,
and. many sundries. Two new salaried Min-
isters are to be appointed, making 10 in all.
And an effort is to be made at the reduc-
tion of the cost of living by eliminating the
middleman. This doesn’t, apparently, mean
the foreign importer, for the Holman crowd
is largely Freetrade, and never seems to use
any influence it may possess to damage the
importing person. Nor does it refer as yet
to the city ground landlord, who is allowed,
by reason of the great congestion policy, to
charge £7OO or £l7OO or so, for a foot front-
age of bare dirt, kerosene tins. Nor
does it seem to have any bearing on the person
like Holman, who is so fond of putting 200
miles of needless railway and any number
of needless middlemen —drivers, firemen,
station-masters," clerks, maintenance men,
makers of rails and engines and sundries —
between the producer and' the consumer.
In fact, it is hard to say exactly which
middleman is aimed at.
* * * *
Incidentally there is a great land-settle-
ment scheme, millions of acres of suitable
soil having been “discovered.” This makes
the community all the more interested to
learn the reason of a recent very costly
resumption in which two members of the
Holman party in Parliament are said to
have figured as paid agents. And there are
many other reforms and schemes and devices
which are, unfortunately, crowded out. But
over them all hangs the shadow of the
Stupendous Loan —the Gigantic' Borrow.
The public works depend directly and
entirely on the goodwill of the foreign
Coiien. The splendid revenue, which is to
cover all the things which even the Clean-
Shaved Young Man in a Hurry hasn’t the
front to charge to loans, is, to the extent
probably of one-third or one-half, the result
of the circulation of loan money. The new
loan will float, and the old one will re-float,
so long as the big Cohen can profitably
peddle out the securities to small investors.
Some day the small investor may want his
money to invest in some of Lloyd George’s
schemes. He may want it to take up his
own war-loans. He may want it to take up
the war-loans of other countries, when two
or three Great Powers are driven to throw
themselves on the money market at 6 or 7
per cent. He may strike financial dis-
aster of his own. And he may. through local
stress, lose faith in his ability to protect
his Australasian investments. For Australasia
is a very long way from Bull, and its
defences are not yet first-class, and if trouble
comes to-morrow Bull may either have to
write off his loans as a bad debt, or spend
more than the loans are worth to protect
them —or else tell the alien that he can have,
this country provided all British financial
interests are respected. There are half a
dozen possibilities which might bring down
Holman’s house of cards, and many another
house of cards, inside 24 hours. And some
of them are very possible possibilities.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Prinzip Does Monarchy a Good Turn.
By emptying his revolver into the bodies
of the Archduke Ferdinand of Austria and
his wife, the Servian student has
helped to put every monarch in Europe more
firmly on his throne, and, incidentally, to
give the Hapsbubg family a chance of hold-
ing its place in Austria-Hungary for another
generation. What the king-worshipping press
of the world calls “a thrill of horror” has
made Europe shiver since this futile murder
was committed, and there is not a sovereign
in that continent, respectable or disreputable,
who has not been able in consequence to make
a greater claim upon the people who main-
tain him in his ornamental altitude. An ex-
ception should perhaps be made for the Ger-
man princlet, William Wied, half-king of
Albania, who seems incapable of deserving
either sympathy or respect in any circum-
stances, since his specialty consists in run-
ning away from his job as soon as it looks
dangerous. The other kings, with the assist-
ance of effective cordons of police and sol-
diers, at least stand up to the risk of assassi-
nation ; and, when one of them goes down,
the “thrill of horror” aforesaid is a fine
monarchial asset for the survivors. It was
remarked by Nietzsche some time ago that
since the princes of Europe had been shot at
by Anarchists they had sat more safely on
their thrones; for the Thrill hereinbefore-
mentioned is the best antidote yet discovered
for the spirit of revolution or republicanism.
If kings were just left to themselves to
demonstrate by what right or reason the
crown should • descend from one of them to
his son, they would soon destroy all the
Divinity That Doth Hedge. Kaiser Bill’s
immense egotism would help to work out its
own consequences. In crowned persons less
presentable than Bill, monarchy tends to
die from sheer ridiculousness. No institu-
tion could live down the perpetual foolish-
ness of kings, which makes their august state-
liness of environment a comic opera; as, for
example, that merry old King of Denmark,
who when an ambassador bowed low before
him hopped from his high seat of state and
played leapfrog over the bent back; or
Alfonso of Spain, a high-spirited young
monarch with a sense of humor, exercised
in belching three times into the face of a
French envoy, each belch louder than the
one before it. If monarchs didn’t become
obsolete through their own imbecility, or the
imbecility of the system they stand for, the
destructive work would be done by their
panegyrists, whose sickly and hypocritical
praise, packed with what nine-tenths of the
“loving subjects” know to be absurd false-
hood, is nauseating enough to make republi-
cans out of men who previously acquiesced
in monarchy as a convenient enough plan of
government. Either by themselves or with
the aid of their flatterers, monarchs taken
by-and-large would soon sink so far that the
pawnshops of Europe would be full of
crowns labelled “Unredeemed Pledge.” But
when Prinzip comes along to fill a prince
and his harmless wife with lead, the emo-
tional string is strummed all through the
world, and the company of unnecessary, ex-
pensive, stupid, imbecile, epileptic or ego-
maniac persons known as kings hold a fresh
mortgage on popular toleration.
Anglicanism Remains True to Its
Traditions,
A score or more of Anglican parsons signed
a maniresto, or round robin, or cock robin
or something in Sydney last week, calling
upon the British Government , to withhold
Home Rule from Ireland “in the name* of
Christ” and Protestantism. The toilers in
the vineyard of Anglicanism had presum-
ably to hnd some justification for the Tory-
ism which has always been in them. Their
Church has ever ranged itself behind the
stout person with cash to put in the collec-
tion plate, and it has very consistently im-
bibed the latter’s political views and im-
pressed the virtue of the same on the poor,
together with the adjuration that they (the
poor) should be humble and respectful to
their betters and eagerly thankful for any
kind of a wage. Unionism happens to be
the rich man’s traditional creed all over the
Empire. To be hostile to Home Rule is as
much a matter of good form, almost, in
the circles in which the miserable, half-
starved little Anglican cleric desires to win
approval, as the habits of dressing for
dinner and not going to theatre first-nights
in bathing suits. And so, as aforesaid, the
parsons had to advance some sort of argu-
ment in its favor —just as in the past they
were impelled to contend in the name of
Christ on behalf of Individualism (the
antithesis of the Christian spirit) and the
custom of importing goods made by the in-
habitants of Roman Catholic countries. But
the “religious” argument, as applied to Home
Rule, is even thinner than the same argu-
ment as it was applied in the past to Immi-
gration Restriction legislation, the White
Australia ideal and so forth. In that tiny
portion of Ulster where political discussion
amounts really to a sort of chronic tribal
warfare the religious argument is a useful
standby. Both sides know that it means
absolutely nothing, bat it serves as a plaus-
ible excuse for the row which is the heart’s
desire of every able-bodied male. In the
eyes of untravelled Australians it may have
a certain surface plausibility, inasmuch as
practically all the local priests are Irish and
poor, which is to say adherents of the un-
fashionable party. But if in the home of
Anglicanism or in Scotland or Wales a par-
son tried to associate the Vatican with
Home Rule he would be laughed at for a
fraud or an ignoramus. The simple reason
is that, in these countries, the Catholics
being mostly extremely rich, are just so
often as they are affluent, virulently Union-
ist in their politics. The Duke of Norfolk,
the acknowledged leader of the English
‘Catholics, is about the most bigoted anti-
Home Ruler in the House of Lords. Lord
Edmund Talbot is his prototype, in this
respect, in the Commons. The Butes,
Lovats, Denbighs, Stonors, Stourtons, de
Traffortjs and practically every other
Catholic family of social prominence in the
United Kingdom stand four-square in furious
hostility to the cause which the hedge-parsons
of Sydney claim to be the cause of Rome.
Notoriously the chief test in this matter is
one of class. Most of the ablest leaders of
the Home Rule movement have been Pro-
testants —poor, or, at any rate, far from
rich Protestants. The strongest supporters
of the movement in England, Scotland and
Wales are Protestants —poor Protestants of
the Nonconformist breed. On the other
hand, in Ireland the movement is backed
chiefly by Catholics —poor Catholics.
Anglicanism does not hate Home Rule be-
cause it dreads Rome, but for the reason
that it loves “respectability.” The absurd
manifesto-signing clerics of Sydney do not
fear an auto-da-fe in Belfast, or any im-
becility of that sort, but they are very much
concerned to prove to their congregations
Votes for the Word.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Bro. O. R. Snowball, of Brighton, appeals to Vic-
torian electors to make the introduction of Bible
readings into State Schools the only question during
the forthcoming campaign. [P.S. —The candidates
who feature Bible reading are all gentlemen of pro-
nounced Conservative instincts.]
The Earth a school is, nothing more,
In which we’re called on to prepare
To live upon the Golden Shore.
That’s why the Snowballs do not care
A button for material things.
They value only harps and wings,
Yet, all unselfish, bid us plan
For Bible pleasures,
While they endure as best they can
Some carnal treasures.
Give heed to what the Brother says;
Show scorn for every godless cause ;
Care nothing for the current craze.
Nought else is worth a brace of straws,
But that the children in the schools
Shall be well trained in all the rules
Planned by the prophets, and not fail,
Each lad and donah,
When catechised about the whale
That swallowed Jonah.
Those common men, it’s understood,
Whose platform’s built of bread and cheese,
Who strive for nought but fleshly good,
Are all your deadly enemies.
Abjure them quickly. Be not fools.
Vote hard for Bibles in the schools.
So you will sei’ve the crew that clings
To notions Tory,
And gaily strives for worldly things
While you seek Glory,
VICTORIA.
EDDYSON.


that they are on the side of the financial
and social angels. Anglican parsons have
always adopted this snobbish line, and they
will continue to do so presumably till they
have driven the last disgusted democrat from
their Church for life.

The Agitator in the Top Hat.
There was a public meeting in Sydney last
week, with the wholly admirable object of
drawing attention to the sufferings of ani-
mals on stock trains. The miserable old
party question had no place in such a gather-
ing—no more than it would have in an
assemblage which was attending to the vic-
tim of a street accident, or trying to resusci-
tate the half-drowned. Thus while the chair-
man was F. E. Winchcombe, M.L.C., a prom-
inent Liberal, one of the chief speakers was
George Black, Laborite. All went well
and reasonably until it came to the turn of
the egregious Onslow, M.L.A., to speak.
Onslow is one who seeks to make up in
violence, of speech what he lacks in the mut-
ter of ideas and intelligence. He is the
mob orator of N. S. Wales Toryism, the Ben
Tillett of the affluent, the Harry Holland
of the pi’operty-owning classes. He is the
loud, blatant advocate of the idiotically im-
practicable, and the tireless assailant of the
irrevocable. In nine cases out of 10, when
Onslow gets up to speak on any public mat-
ter, his contribution is as valueless as though
a donkey had brayed in the wilderness. The
customary occurred at the King’s Hall meet-
ing last week. Onslow had no suggestions
to offer whereby the unhappy animals, whose
dreadful Calvary is the iron road to the sale-
yards, might have their agonies alleviated.
llis notion of good philanthropy and decent
behavior was to exploit the meeting for the
purposes of making an assault upon the prin-
ciples of his political opponents. The fol-
lowing is a sample from his oration:—
Since Private Enterprise had failed [as re-
gards establishing up-country freezing works to
relieve the city congestion] 'the State might step
in and make it a success at the expense of everyone in
the State. (Laughter.) Were the railways admin-
istered by a private company some officer would years
ago have been fined in the police courts, but the
Government seemed to have a free license to perpe-
trate atrocities on dumb animals. It was a scandal
and a shame that they suffered their servants to
commit these outrages.
Onslow knows perfectly well that there is
nothing of value to be gained by inflaming
the public mind against the railway men. It
is the system, and not they, who are to blame.
But he is a party man first, and a fair-
sportsman a mile or so afterwards,
lhe system of crowding everything to Syd-
ney is one which he upholds, both as a big
city property owner and as a faithful Wade-
lte - By representing that State railways
and the State’s railway employees are re-
sponsible for the infamies which occur con-
stantly in the neighborhood of Sydney and
Melbourne, he both casts dust in the eyes of
those who take him seriously, and buttresses
his burst old anti-Socialistic creed to a cer-
tam extent. He deceives his followers —if
he has any—into imagining that if they get
new railway servants there will be no need
for decentralisation, so far as averting the
torture of travelling stock is concerned ; and
he lays before the public the proposition that
otatc railways are a failure. It is dema-
goguery of the Tory sort, in its most nau-
seating form. The brightest railway staff
that the world has known could do nothing
worth mentioning to avert the inhumanity
to which last week’s meeting drew atten-
tion, .and the bellowings of 10,000 volunteer
warriors must be powerless to induce any
Australian State to retrogress so far as to
once more adopt the discredited private enter-
prise railway system of Britain and the
United States. Onslow knows these things
perfectly well,, just as he knows that his
extravagant railings against compulsory ser-
vice and the Australian navy will not have
the least effect in the way of persuading the
Commonwealth to change its established De-
fence policy. He never expects to
achieve anything. His highest aim is to
create a disturbance; to advertise himself;
and to make things as unpleasant as possible
for the Opposition, regardless of all consid-
erations. of fair play or public policy. Only
the accident of inherited wealth has kept
this congenital mob-orator from the place
where he rightly belongs—namely, a pitch in
the Domain or on the Yarra Bank.
The Half-Breed.
King Hall, the last Admiral of John
Bull’s navy in these parts, gave the Nine-
teenth Century an article on the Aus-
tralian baby-navy when he got back to
British waters. With leanings towards
Exeter Hall and the idea of “Imperial
brotherhood,” it took King Hall some
time to become a thorough-going White Aus-
tralian ; but he got there in the end:—
Professedly Australia is a Christian country, with
the highest ideals of womanhood, ethics and
morality, however imperfectly such may be carried-
out; on the other hand, although amongst the races
whom it is desired to exclude from the country
there are many individuals who ean and do set a
high standard of rectitude and morality to many
professed Christians, yet in the mass their moral
ideas, their treatment of women, their standard
of living-, and their customs generally are much
inferior to those of the white race living in Aus-
tralia ; hence the intense, passionate desire to avoid
any danger of a lowering of ideals.
The Commonweal, the paper in which
Annie Besant nowadays prattles her beau-
tiful platitudes about “universality” and
Nirvana and other spooks, tells King Hall
that his remarks “will enhance the bitter-
ness that exists in all Indian hearts and
minds on this question of color bar.” But
why feel bitterness towards Australia? Aus-
tralia isn’t responsible for the color bar.
That was invented away back in the begin-
ning of things, and Australia merely
accepted the fact. Christianity needn’t be
dragged into the matter either; the fact
that we are a professedly Christian country
has nothing to do with it. It isn’t a matter
of religious creed ; it is a matter of racial
purity—of preserving the lines which for
some reason or other Nature (call it what
you like) laid down a few million years ago.
And history shows that Nature was right.
No mixed people are of any account what-
ever. Even the Commonweal refers pity-
ingly to the half-breeds of India: “The
Eurasian population is a living monument
to Christian ethics and morality.” Very
well; take it that way. Admit that the
Christian ethics and moi. Is of Australia
are nc be-tter than the Britisher’s ethics
and morals were when the Eurasian was
started on his journey. What then? Is
India so proud of its Eurasians that it
would start a second crop of them in
another land? If the Commonweal could
have prevented the Eurasian mixed breed,
would it have done so? And if it would
have done so, how would it propose to set
about the job except in the way Australia
has taken?
The Decentralisation Sham.
“Leo Lear” : The most diabolical thing
about decentralisation is that the more it
is talked of the less it decentralises. Of
late years every Victorian politician from
15in. calibre down to pea-rifle size has
mouthed the advantages of decentralisation,
and told us how it is working at last by
reason of his Parliamentary exertions, and
how the country districts have now a chance
to expand even unto the bursting of their
belts. Well, this is how overseas shipping
has expanded from year to year in the Vic-
torian harbor of Portland. In Feb., 1902,
the deep-water pier was opened for traffic,
and during that year 13 boats hooked on
and dragged out £92,700 worth of produce.
The table will show what all the flow of
decentralisation talk has been worth to
Portland since then :
Produce
No. of Vessels. Shipped.
1903 .. .. 20 £104,478
1904 .... 8 56,620
1905 .. ~ 9 81,914
1906 .. . . 12 96,276
1907 .. .. 12 65,825
1908 .. .. 12 128,174
1909 .. .. 11 91,728
1910 .. .. 10 94,931
1911 .. .. 11 81,354
1912 .... 8 56,712
1913 .... 3 28,152
It seems to me that the only way to stop the
rot is to make it a penal offence for any
politician to take any credit (1) for believ-
ing in decentralisation, or (2) for anything
he alleges he has done for decentralisation
without producing sworn figures to support
his claim. When he is compelled to show
that he has done so much for decentralisa-
tion that the oversea trade of the principal
Victorian outport (or what should be the
principal outport) has shrunk two-thirds in
a few years, the politician may conclude
that it is safer to shut his face on that sub-
ject until he has really done something. By
reason of strange railway devices, worked
from Melbourne, it costs about three times
as much to send goods a certain distance to
be shipped at Portland as to send them the
same distance to be shipped at Melbourne.
And while that state of things lasts all the
talk about decentralisation is dust and
ashes and rags and dead peanuts and a
whited sepulchre.
The Exaltation of the Tail.
The N.S.W. Ministry has greatly exalted its
tail, which now looks as if it had been boiled
and starched and ironed, over the finances of
the year which has just closed. Yet some
people would be inclined to tie a brass bed-
stead to the tail, in order to stop its absurd
elevation. The income for the year to Juno
30, 1914, was £3,864,364 above that of the
year which ended on June 30, 1910. With all
this extra money to spend an ordinary sort
of Government might possibly have pulled
through, but as the expenditure increased by
£4,653,355, this one didn’t pull worth a row
of pins. Being badly shocked and broken
up it started to economise, not in its borrow-
ings, or its profligacy or its political drinkings,
but in its honesty. The money allotted out
of the year’s surplus for Public Works pur-
poses was cut down from £575,000 to nothing.
The money allotted in the same way for
Closer Settlement purposes was cut down
from £235,000 to nothing. The very humble
amount which used to be set aside out of
revenue for the reduction of the Public Debt
diminished from £421,034 to £5631, an alleged
saving of £415,403, but a very poor kind of
saving on the part of a Government which
had blown so big a trumpet about honest
finance, and about its cast-iron and boiler-
metal resolve to build up the sinking fund
and put every item of the Public Debt on. a
square, decent, respectable, repayable basis.
Having thus transferred over £1,200,000 of
what used to be ordinary expenditure to
loans, and left all this bill in the old cowardly
fashion for the infant to pay, the Holman
Government managed to show an alleged ex-
cess of revenue over expenditure of £373,030.
It wasn’t the sort of surplus that any decent
Ministry could have felt proud of, but it was
a trifle better than nothing. There is still a
large accumulated shortage, but that is a
matter for the future. Almost everything
is a matter for the future.
+.
The Financier publishes an annual Em-
pire number —a couple of pounds’ weight of
literature heavily subsidised by Australian
and other “Colonial” Governments. In the
last issue, just to hand, John Bull is
strongly advised to export capital:—
So obvious are the advantages that they hardly
need to be enumerated. In the first place, by lending
large sums to other lands we apply a powerful
stimulus to our industrial production. The reader
scarcely needs to be told that when capital is ex-
ported it does not go, as even to-day many ill-
informed persons suppose, in the form of golden
sovereigns. That statement is sufficiently proved by
the fact that year by year the imports of bullion and
specie into the United Kingdom exceed the exports,
or, in other words, show a favorable balance. We
could not, even if we would, lend hard cash to
would-be borrowers. Those who take capital from
us accept in discharge of the obligation the products
of our faotories and our workshops. In this way by
lending to foreign and colonial borrowers we inci-
dentally create a demand for our manufactures, and
thus serve to keep our great industries busily em-
ployed. At the same time we add substantially to
the national income, by placing upon the nations
which have borrowed from us the obligation of pay-
ing the interest on their loans. Interest, too, comes
to us in the form of goods, furnishing food for our
people and raw materials for our industries.
For home consumption the Financier was
quite right to head these remarks “The Ad-
vantages of Exporting Capital.” On the
other hand, for the foreign consumption of
a country which wants manufactures and
people the thing ought obviously to be
labelled “Disadvantages bf Importing Capi-
tal,” and the text put into plain language
would run like this: —
When you import capital you don’t get it. What
you do is to give work to foreign factories, which
supply you with goods that you ought to be making
yourselves. After that, you’ll pay no interest, in
goods of course; but as we don’t want your manufac-
tures (and as you couldn’t compete with ours in
any case, because you’ve been busy making our in-
dustries strong and your own weak), you’ll oblige
us by sending raw materials to keep our factories
going. In short, this scheme of “importing capital”
is one for kcaping the big manufacturing population
in Europe, while you remain a country with a few
million people addicted mostly to growing wool.

One night last week Prime Minister Cook
spoke at Cootamundra (N.S.W.). This is
what the Sydney Telegraph thought about
it the next morning:—
A group of Liberals, including the Prime Minister,
last night invaded the electorate of the State Premier.
Mr. Cook came specially from Melbourne to address
the meeting, and met with an enthusiastic reception.
A few days later it became necessary to shake
the Liberals up a bit, so the D.T. printed
this concerning the same meeting:—
When Mr. Cook walked into the Cootamundra Town
Hall on Monday night, not a voice was raised ; not
a hand was moved. It was a cold, wet, heavy night,
but it was a colder, heavier, more depressing audi-
ence to which the Prime Minister spoke. Hardly a
cheer, hardly a sound of approval broke the monotony
of the oratorical drip. A big audience listened
sullenly. Had the speakers been prescribing doses of
nasty medicine for each individual in the crowd, the
interest could hardly have been less enthusiastic or
more academic. It was the dullest, deadliest audi-
ence that Mr. Cook has addressed for years.
“Hardly a cheer, hardly a sound of ap-
proval” ! Still it was “an enthusiastic re-
ception”—until for the same old dishonest
party purpose it became advisable to de-
scribe it as something else.
The Tariff Commission lately rubbed up
against paint, with the usual unpleasant
consequences. Manufacturer F. J. Hodges,
of Marrickville (Sydney), asked folr lan
increase in the duty on lcalsomine. He
mentioned that the retail price of the goods
in New York was from 2s. to 2s. 6d. per
51b. packet, yet the same stuff was exported
to Sydney and sold at Is. ln order to
get a market the local manufacturer had to
sell at 20 per cent, below the inferior dumped
American article, because the lie that “Aus-
tralia can’t make it” still sticks with great
adhesiveness to the paint trade. Said he: —
I meet this sort of thing every day. Everybody’s
doing it, even the Government. (Laughter.) As
an example, I may mention that my material was
used at State Government House recently—at Cran-
brook —with satisfactory results. Yet I go into the
suburbs, where men are building duck-houses —
(laughter)—and I have to smuggle my material in
early in the morning before the arrival of the Clerk
of Works. The specifications stipulate for the
imported article. (Laughter.)
There seems to have been a large quantity
of “laughter” mixed with the proceedings,
but there was really nothing to laugh about.
The fact of an Avistralian manufacturer
being blocked out by foreign competition
isn’t a jest—it is a disgrace. And if, as
appears from the evidence, a Government
that is allegedly in office to help the Aus-
tralian worker is a party to the knocking
out by using imported paint, then the dis-
grace is too disgraceful to be mentioned in
respectable type.

“Carburetter” : The N.S.W. Navigation
Department, after protesting for 14 years
that it hadn’t power to control motor-boats
plying for hire, has launched a set of regu-
lations dealing with these craft which ought
to fill up the stomach of a long-felt want,
although considerable objection has been
raised to the manning scale. On June 30
the biggest motor-boat could have been
driven by a schoolboy. Now, a motor-boat
up to 40ft. must have a certificated driver
and coxswain, in one person; if over 40ft.,
in separate persons; while a motor-boat over
25h.p. must carry, at least, a second-class
engineer. Yet the Navigation Department’s
own law provides that a coast-trade passen-
ger ship, not exceeding 50h.p., need carry
only a third-class engineer. Further, the new
regulations allow a third-class certificate-
holder to drive a ferry steamer of unlimited
horse-power from Parramatta to Manly, or
from one end of the Clarence River to the
other, and Sydney’s ferry-boats carry 1600
passengers at times. Yet the same man
may not take a motor-boat of 25h.p. across
Port Jackson with dead sheep on board.
The authority which provides such indis-
tinction is likely to be addressed disrespect-
fully as a Williamgoat. In fact, I hereby
call it a Williamgoat to its beard.
“Umph” : An unsatisfactory position was
occasioned by Sydney Coroner Fitz-
hardinge in his verdict as to a patient
named Brown, who died in Parramatta
Hospital for the Insane on June 6, eight
days after his admission. When the medi-
cal officers examined Brown’s body it was
found that nine ribs were broken, his kid-
neys and bladder ruptured, and there were
other internal injuries. The medical evi-
dence showed that the deceased could, not
have lived more than 48 hours in his injured
state, therefore he must have received the
damage in the hospital. By an unfortunate
coincidence, Brown’s medical attendant, the
only man likely to throw any light on the
cause of the injuries, dropped dead from
heart disease before the inquest. Whatever
theory the Hospital officers and attendants
had of the accident, or occurrence, they suc-
ceeded in keeping to themselves, and after
satisfying the Coroner that Brown was care-
fully looked after and had had every atten-
tion, that official found that he had died
from broken ribs, caused no man knew how.
Now, nine ribs aren’t easily broken all at
once. A man carefully looked after might
sprain an ankle or even crack his skull
without anyone being able to know how he
did it, but for a strong man to get six ribs
on one side and three on the other fractured
in a public hospital is a curious and dis-
turbing circumstance. It was admitted that
Brown had been forcibly fed and forcibly
strapped up in a straight jacket, but whether
any of these forcible measures accounted for
the wreckage of the man’s bones did not
transpire. In fact, the amount of informa-
tion that has not transpired about this busi-
ness calls for a divining rod and seven black
trackers. Premier Holman has decreed
that the privately-controlled mental asylum
is to be abolished as being beyond super-
vision. I want to ask Holman if he is sure
he can spare any supervision from the State
asylums.
Randolph Bedford writes :
The double dissolution was an excellent thing for
Joseph Suhface Cook if only because it suppressed
the Teebdale Smith inquiry, as all Royal Commis-
sions die with a Parliament. A contract was let in
face of the known fact that cheaper track-forming
had been done by day-labor and the Castles machine
employed on the Transcontinental by the former
Government. The Castles machine did 4874 chains
of track-forming, including excavation, at a cost of
265. per chain. The replies of the Home Affairs
Department to Bamfohd’s questions as to the relative
cost of the machine and of pick-and-shovel or scoop
gangs (which are Teebdale Smith’s methods of work-
ing under contract) were: “Surface-forming has cost
approximately 4s. per chain less by the machine than
by hand labor; excavation in cutting has cost,
approximately, 60 per cent, less by the machine than
by hand labor; and excavation in side-cutting has
cost, approximately, 50 per cent, less by the machine
than by hand labor.’’ Actual figures of the work
of the machine on the Port Augusta job were 22,886
cubic yards of earthworks in five days, at a cost of
£264 9s. 6d., equal to 2 2-3 d. per yard, or 11s. 2d.
a chain. The lowest payment under the Teesdale
Smith contract is 2s. 6d. a yard ; embankment costs
4s. 6d. a yard, and if he shifts the spoil more than
two chains there is an extra 2s. 6d. a yard. Yet the
machine, which averages from 600 to 1000 cubic
yards per hour, lifting it to a height of 26ft. and
delivering it 40ft. away, was put out of commission
at the first sign of necessity for repairs, with no
apparent reason but the desire to discredit the day-
labor system that accompanied the machine. The
sacred contract system had to be reinstituted at a
cost of 2s. 6d. a yard against 2 2-3 d. a yard. Thus,
where political exigencies dictate, the very eco-
nomical Cook Government throws away half a crown
for less than threepenn’orth of work.
Great Britain, which pays the biggest naval
and military defence bill in the world, and
also the biggest in all history, finds its bur-
dens rather heavy. It is announced that
next year the income-tax on incomes derived
from property will be Is. 4d. in the £, with
a super-tax on big incomes and an extra
super-tax on bigger incomes. If only
George of Britain and William of Germany
would toss for the two thrones, and the win-
ner take both, and the loser be made an
Archduke, and the two armies and navies
be united, and the two countries agree on a
common language —if these things were done
some £50,000,000 a year might be saved in
the joint defence bill. Quite a heap of things
may be done with £50,000,000 a year.

“Pennywise” puts the case for one of Aus-
tralia’s most useful citizens : —■
Apropos of the pay of area-officers, an amend-
ment has at last been added to the existing regula-
tion, providing for an increase of £3O per annum
after three years’ service as an area-officer. As
an example of downright, unreasonable stinginess
this would be hard to equal. When universal training
began, the area-officers, .with their inadequate staffs,
rounded up the thousands of undisciplined young-
sters, pared the savagery off them, and bucked in
and put the job through in such a fashion that at
the end of the first year the business was a pro-
nounced success. In no country in the world could
it have been done better. The work entailed an
enormous amount of administration beside the stren-
uous job of training, and the Area Staff usually con-
sisted of the A.O. and his sergeant-major. If one
fell sick or died the other fellow would be left to
carry on alone for a month or so, a state of affairs
which still exists. In addition to the work, the
A.O. is responsible for hundreds of pounds’ worth
of stores. If someone’s carelessness leads to the loss
of any of these articles, the A.O. takes the punch.
He might, according to Financial and Allowance
Regulations, have to stand court-martial if careless-
ness is proved. Then this offlcer-of-all-trades must
conduct prosecutions for all units. He must always
parade with Senior Cadets, and with any other regi-
ment that wants him, from Light Horse to Infan-
try. If he is instructed to turn out with Light
Horse, as many of the A.O.’s who formerly belonged
to that arm have been in the past, he has to pay
his own horse hire. The only allowance he is
entitled to is travelling, at the rate of 12s. 6d. per
diem when away from headquarters. A small coun-
try area may contain 1500 square miles; some are
four times as large. The officer is responsible for
the registration of every 14-year-old boy in the area.
He claims Junior Cadet contingency allowances for
perhaps 60 scholars (some officers have over 100 to
look after) and audits the cash statements. If
ammunition for miniature ranges is required at any
of these schools, the area-officer has to attend to the
requisition, issue rifles and cleaning apparatus, and
help with the training of the juniors when asked to
do so. In short, he must be at everyone’s call, but ■
must keep well in the background when he isn’t
wanted. His job is one everlasting round of the
unpleasant side of military duty, made up of prosecu-
tions, voluminous reports, blame-taking and boy-
training, on the pay of a navvy. Unless he is pos-
sessed of private means, and is also an enthusiastic
soldier, an area-officer chucks the job in disgust
long before he has done the three years that precede
the new affluency. The worst feature of the thing
is that many officers who have been rated as first-
class for two years, according to the standard im-
posed by Major-General Kirkpatrick, will still have
to rub along on the old stipend till they have done
the three years, whilst second- and third-class men,
who have been in from the beginning, will get the
increase.
Betsy Prig Has Forebodings.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Take what we said in criticism of the accounts a
year ago. The loan expenditure had in the year
reached the wholly unprecedented figure of £8,587,000,
and we were rather aghast at it. —Sydney Telegraph.
Sairey, for this last year as ever was,
I’ve been a-thinkin’ deep about the Past;
And I ’ave got forebodin’s: Why? Because
I feel inside them good, old times can’t
last:
Well, just a leetle drop—an’ please don’t
pour too fast.
I looks towards you, Sairey ; an’ looks back
Wiv pride on my perfessional career;
There’s no deniging we ’ave ’ad our whack
Of profits, perkisites and tea an’ beer;
But I’m afeared that things is goin’ to
bust, my dear.
Them bragian boys is gettin’ past all bounds!
The way they borrer is a Shame, my pet:
’Ow they do spend it, too —the bold, young
’ounds !
A-runnin’ US and everyone in debt,
For things I can’t forgive, nor you, my
dear, forget.
There’s meejums, too, in all things—even
10an5....
Well, jest a teeny taste....an’ you, an’
Me
Ain’t never yet a-took to throwin’ stones
At anyone what borrered nice an’ free;
But now I’ve got the creeps about what’s
goin’ to be.
Cheer up? In course I will! But we must
send
A nurgent warnin’ to them ’orrid kids!
As long as there’s a party what’ll lend,
They’d pawn their aunt an’ gran’ma with
them Yids !
I’m fair agasht, the way they blew the
luvely quids....
’Ere’s lookin’ at yer, Sairey ! Don’t sup-
poge
Your Betsy is down’earted —Never fear!
We’re doin’ well —but I do not propoge
To let them boys usupp our lorful
sphere. . ..
Good ’ealth !. .. . an’ may our boys be raisin’
loans next year!
N.S.W.
O. C. CABOT.
