“Bunty Pulls the Strings” is flourishing at
the Royal (Sydney). Though the main-
spring of the play is misappropriation of
funds and the village policeman is always
handy, there is hardly a shadow in it, and
the audience hurries for its homeward
vehicle with a smile. The playgoer who has
another sombre show in his mind should save
bright little “Bunty” for the second course.
If ye have tears prepare to shed them at
the Sydney Criterion. “Madame X” is the
dampest play of an unusually wet season.
The meteorological officer has to go back to
“The Only Way,” produced many years a.go,
for a comparison of the unsettled and sniffy
conditions that prevailed at the Cri. on
Saturday night. Handkerchiefs may be
ordered at 10.15. Patrons who dislike wet
feet will have either to come in their goloshes
or bring their own bath-mats. The tears
streamed down the face of this critic so
blindingly that he forgot to tip the cloak-
room attendant, and consequently witnessed
the amazing spectacle of a cloak-room
attendant in tears. This critic’s grand-aunt
rushed to the ladies’ room with the desperate
desire to powder her nose, but reported, with
more tears, that 382 women had been before
her and that the powder, had run out. *
The only objection that can be taken to
“Madame X” is that the author, or the
adaptor, or the writer of the American ver-
sion, has chosen such a commonplace plot.
How frequently do we notice in our own
law courts that the woman on trial for
murder finds her husband provided with a
seat on the bench and her long-lost son the
counsel for the defence! This hackneyed
situation in real life the author, or his con-
federates, has merely transferred to the
stage. The only originality shown by the
author was his refusal to make the jury
composed of the woman’s 12 uncles. How-
ever, it provides the pathos necessary.
Jacqueline Floriot has cleared from her hus-
band and her infant child, but on the death
of her lover she returns to Floriot’s house
to see her son, who is dangerously ill.
Floriot hurls her out, and thou eh he repents
he fails to find her again. She has gone
down into the underworld, or the tube rail-
way, or the bottom of a wild-cat mine. But
20 years later she reappears as the mistress
of a genial scoundrel, a mere derelict of a
woman, drug-sodden and degraded. In order
to prevent her lover blackmailing her hus-
band she shoots the cheerful scoundrel dead.
Arrested, she refuses to speak, even to give
her name. The police are unable to find out
anything about her, and a young lawyer,
whose first case this is. is given to her for
counsel. During the trial the woman recog-
nises her husband sitting beside the judve.
and learns that her lawyer is her own son. His
eloquence results in a verdict of not guilty ;
hut the woman is dying. Before her death
she and her son acknowledge each other, and
she perishes happy.
Obviously the plav is designed to give
every opportunity for a display of the
woman’s emotional powers ; indeed, the play
is little but a framework for Jacqueline’s
emotions. And in the hands of Muriel Starr
“Madame X” becomes the most poignant
drama seen in Australia for years. In the
first scene Miss Starr plunges immediately
into a tempestuous argument with her stern
husband, flinging herself on her knees to him
and pleading passionately for forgiveness.
In the next she has become a. decrepit
horror, drinking absinthe with a fiendish
craving and greedily imbibing ether. Already
her awful life has ruined her health. In
the trial scene, dying, desperate, despairing,
she sits crumpled in the box, dominating the
situation, n figure of indelible pathos and
horror. Her scream, on discovering that her
counsel is her son, is enough to induce
hysterics in a member of the Woman’s
National Council. But in the last phase,
where she gloats upon the face of her son,
childish in her infatuation and love, Muriel
Starr touches heights of genius. In appear-
ance, attitude and dress, this actress becomes
the wreck that she is personating.
* * * *
The husband is capably presented by Roy
Redgrave. Eric Maxon, as an exquisite
Frenchman, acts with a new sincerity. E;
IV. Morrison ably fills the part of her
scoundrelly lover, his fall when shot by
Jacqueline being a masterpiece of realism.
A small part, carefully accurate in details,
is that of George Chalmers the younger, as
the hotel porter, his demeanor in the trial
scene being admirable. Frederick Ward’s
delivery of his speech in the defence suc-
ceeds, despite his lack of personality and his
trivial work in the preceding scene. Two
genial blackmailers, Claude Bantock and
George Chalmers the elder, acceptably provide
the only comedy element in the general atmo-
sphere of gloom. Miss Marjorie Soper makes
the most of a small part; and Miss Mary
Worth is content in a colorless ingenue
character. But the company does not
matter: the play is Muriel Starr.
Maud Allan arrived at Sydney Palace on
Saturday night, and found a large audience
that was sympathetic and occasionally en-
thusiastic —particularly so after the child-
ishly joyous Moment Musical which, with
Schubert’s exquisite music, was a plain
proposition and quite irresistible in its
appeal. But some of the other dances drew
merely a nominal amount of noise and left
the spectators pondering. There is nothing
deliberately dazzling about Miss Allan’s art.
It never resembles hard work, or becomes a
thunderous rattle of shoe-heels. It isn’t
Gende, or musical comedy jigging, or vaude-
ville clatter, or tango, or ballroom stuff—
none of the familiar stunts resemble it.
The leaflet which supplies a list of
titled and merely distinguished people who
“have witnessed the dances of Miss Maud
Allan” is, presumably, supplied for the
encouragement of the doubting and untitled
customer. There doesn’t seem to be any
use in it otherwise. Any dancer who lias
been before the London public for years
could hardly avoid being seen by the nobility
—who have more time on their hands than
most. The famous Salome Dance, which has
been Maud’s fortune, is carefully melodra-
matic, and, with its fine setting, gory acces-
sories and music, requires less mental effort

and chiffon costume resists the whirlwind

The first South African play produced by
Hugh Buckler at the Little Theatre (Syd-
ney) had its premiere in Monday evening.
“The Master of Angerstroom” is not particu-
larly distinguished for anything. Its con-
struction is slipshod, its situations are
mechanical. There is no lightening humor in
this melancholy drama. It is the sort of
play, too, in which “asides” never cease from
troubling and soliloquies run amuck. The
soliloquising habit so gets on the minds cf
the characters that some portions of the play
seem like a succession of one-man turns in
Maud Allan dancing to Mendelssohn?s
Spring Song.
vaudeville. Even when the “asides” are
shouted nobody on the stage notices them.
And the chief situation depends for its proba-
bility on the mere chance that one of the
characters bears the name “Adace.” The
plot tells of John Adace, the master of the
Angerstroom estate near Durban. He is
a London cashier who absconded 20 years
before. The steals ruined his employer,
Ruckmann; but he has apparently enough
left to pay his fares all over the world for
years, in his relentless search for the enemy.
Adace’s son falls in love with Bertha, the
daughter of Ruckmann. Ruckmann recog-
nises his cashier and telegraphs for a detec-
tive. When Adace, thinking himself secure,
asks Ruckmann, on behalf of his son, for
Bertha, Ruckmann denounces him and is
about to proclaim Adace’s identity to the
household when he is strikeil with (stage)
aphasia. Thus he is unable to speak or
write the name of the man the detective has
come up to arrest. The unutterably foolish
’tec cannot discover the man, though, as
Ruckmann can point, all he need do is to
round up the whole household and get the
speechless man to indicate the criminal. But
this would not suit the dramatist. He must
have his situation. So it is that Ruckmann
spells the name “Adace” by striking the
notes corresponding to the letters of that
name on the piano. Had the cashier’s name
been “Jones,” there would have been no
situation. In the end Ruckmann recovers
his speech, and, on evidence of Adace’s re-
pentance, tears up the latter’s confession of
his crime. The climax is unnecessarily de-
layed by the detective refusing to bother him-
self with arresting Adace, and resigning
from the force. There is a lot more plot,
too.
* * * *
Such a play gives small opportunity for
any but the characters of Ruckmann and
Adace. Hugh Buckler is admirable in his
many scenes of woe, terror and remorse ; and
Arthur Cornell’s presentment of the relent-
less German employer is a consistent piece
of work. Alfred Bristowe is a garrulous
doctor, whose mannerisms in the end grow
rather wearisome. Kenneth Brampton in-
fuses some warmth into the rather colorless
part of Adace’s son. The gentle ’tec is
played in a gentlemanly manner by Gerald
K. Souper. Miss Violet Paget, in an
ingenue part, has little to do but look sweet
and innocent in a succession of beautiful
gowns, and Miss Lillian Lloyd has still less.
The staging is delightful. The Little
Theatre hag reduced to a fine art the prob-
lem of getting apparent space on a small
stage. The author is said to be an ex-Aus-
tralian.
W. W. Jacob’s rather childish playlet,
“The Grey Parrot,” preceded this South
African drama. It concerns two stage
sailors and their wives. Kenneth Bramnton
makes an admirable tar of the Jacobs varietv,
but Tom Craven, as his mate, errs toward
caricature in a part that does not need to
be clowned. There is, however, an unctu-
ousness about his work that almost compen-
sates for this defect. The other characters
are well placed.

The one-act play, shunned by the Austra-
lian commercial manager, proved its power
to interest at the Stage Society’s production
at the Repertory Theatre, Sydney, on Wednes-
day last week. Three one-act plays were
presented: Barrie’s delightful impossibility,
“Rosalind,” J. Stanley Houghton’s theatric
impossibility, “The Fifth Commandment,”
and Adrian Consett Stephen’s literary impos-
sibility, “Echoes.” “Rosalind” shows a
famous actress “resting” in lodgings, where,
in the company of her landlady, the actress
comfortably relapses into her professionally-
delayed middle-age. The landlady passes
for her mother. A youthful admirer of the
stage Rosalind arrives, and is only witV&£-
ficulty convinced that the placid, middle-aged
woman is the young, sprightly geniusi of.bis
devotion. Then Rosalind is called back to
rehearsals, and quickly appears dressed. “
travel, the eternally-youthful, popular lady
again. It is a whimsical conception, deli-
cately touched with Barrie’s charm, an
humor. Mrs. R. Suttor was admirable in hei
conception of the dual personality ot Rosa-
lind, though her reading of the charactei die
not touch its depths. The youth wasinel-
fectively portrayed by Reginald Neiia.
Houghton’s play shows, rather cheaply, tac
incubus that a mother can become to a de-
voted daughter, and the incapacity, of tne
younger generation of an English family to*.
healthy revolt. Miss Gavin Brown was
well placed as the selfish, mollusc mothei.
Miss Ailsa Craig was too wooden for even
such a mother’s daughter; but the roen
parts, played by Dodd Mehan and 1 lacid
Courtney, were thoroughly satisfactory.
The society is to be congratulated
on having given A. C. Stephen’s Aus-
tralian play its chance of produc-
tion. It is a bookish play, full of literary,
but not dramatic, ideas ; the motive is vaguely
developed and overgrown with excrescences.
If thoroughly gone over with a harrow of
editorial blue-pencils, and fertilised _ with a
little humor, it would make an effective play.
But it is distinctly clever —an asset of
promise rather than of achievement. _ Evi-
dently A. C. Stephen has the stuff in him.
The performance was marred by the loud
barking of Placid Courtney, the growling of
the prompter, and the frequently inaudible
voice of A. C. Stephen.

If the suffragettes’ notion of man is any-
thing like Winifred Ward’s —the lady who
is now appearing at Sydney Tivoli —then
The Bulletin ceases to wonder why they
want the vote. Fortunately this class of
“male impersonator” is pretty rare, and it
could be at least one rarer with advantage.
Wallace Brownlow, the favorite old baritone
of comic opera, demonstrates that he hasn’t
lost all his power to charm. Eugene
Damond, a youthful violinist, is the pick of
the Tivoli’s new bunch. She does especially
well in the “Barcarole,” wherein the orches-
tra also rises to the occasion. Bellora, a
mimic; the Villions, cyclists and dancers;
and Gertrude Gebest are other interesting
additions to an improved bill.
Fresh turns continue to keep the National
Amphitheatre (Sydney) programme from
staleness (the curse of vaudeville). In this
respect the Brennan-Fuller (more particu-
larly Ben Fuller) management has made a
name for itself in a comparatively brief time,
and is giving weekly guarantees that it in-
tends to retain the reputation. The show
this week probably goes one better than any
the roof has yet sheltered.
The Imperial Salon de Luxe (Sydney)
has been booked to-night for the Civil Ambu-
lance ball, and to-morrow night the Sydney
Polo Club will have its exclusive use. On
both afternoons, however, the big tabernacle
of the tango is free to the public (with the
trifling exception of the cost of admittance),
and on Saturday the usual conditions will
prevail and pause not, nor will there be any
variableness or shadow of turning.
Next Monday, at the Repertory Theatre,
the Sydney Repertory Dramatic Society will
begin a six-nights’ production of W. R. Ken-
nedy’s remarkable “Christ” play, “The Ser-
vant in the House.” The drama, though
dealing with the same idea as “The Passing
of the Third Floor Back,” bears no resem-
blance to it in construction.

Antony and Cleopatra, who are alleged to
have suicided with such thoroughness that
they have been more or less dead ever since,
were resurrected in Sydney last Saturday
for the very manyth time. This time it was
at Spencer’s Lyceum, per cinematograph,
and they will continue to resurrect there
until further notice. There’s no particular
reason why this young and promising couple
should have suicided. This paper knows
dozens who could suicide with greater ad-
vantage ; and, without desiring to give
offence, it recommends these and others to
see how it is done. Those who are not inter-
ested in the cleanest and most painless
methods of taking their ticket may find en-
joyment in the show, which certainly puts
the art of moving-pictury up another peg.
BUNTY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Eternal Bunty, innocently-wise,
You come to cheer our lonely hearts again;
For you have always lived in some disguise,
Wee Bunty, mother-manager of men !
And as you, with your immemorial guile,
Twist round your little fingers clumsy
men,
We wriggle helplessly, then with you smile:
Who wouldn’t be henpecked by such a henV
Without you, Bunty, what would we men be
But sodden, broken wreckage, tempest-
whirled V
Then with your maiden-meek sagacity
You pull the strings—the heart-strings—
of the world.
We see the purpose underneath your mirth,
Yet meekly follow you—no more alone.
Ah, lucky we, the Weelyums of this earth,
Who have a wilful Bunty of our own !
N.s.w.
ARTHUR H. ADAMS.

Adelaide Royal is doing some quick
changes. Muriel Starr haying departed
the grey-haired Dufault tried the house
for some concerts, and found the new
building fine for singing. He is im-
mensely popular. Pretty (as Pepys said)
to see the churchy city absolutely refusing
to go home to bed until he handed
sheerly Pagan challenge of Henley’s “In-
victus.” But then (as Longfellow ought to
have said), Who has weight so clear and
strong That he cares a d —n for the words
of a song? And now the Royal is pluckily
trying to reproduce the great Drury Lane
effects of “Sealed Orders.” Ada Reeve has
just started a boom at the Tivoli ; she is
not as young as in the “French Maid” days,
but she is a far finer artist. Farr and Falk-
land are at this house too, and the amiable
person who calls himself Dusty Rhodes. At
the King’s, Sterling and Love, _ Mann and
Franks, and some clever first-in-Australia
turns. (Judgment reserved.) The finest pic-
ture in sight is the “Three Musketeers” at
West’s —Dumas carefully revised for the
popular eye.
From an Adelaide critic: —
The South Australian Society of Arts’ gallery has
been mostly occupied of late by one-brush shows.
After Mrs. Gwen Barringer (Adelaide) came charm-
ing miniatures and unpretentious landscapes by Miss
Bernice Edweil (N.S.W.) ; and now it is the turn of
Miss Thea Proctor (Sydney, with a cosmopolitan
training). She hangs the same clever, unusual works
lately shown in Sydney, with some others. The
abnormal muscles of the Genee-like dancer are treated
with merciless photographic fidelity in a variety of
poses, and the rest is mostly study of costumes old
enough or new enough to be remarkable. Her deco-
rative fans are charming, and, if she is occasionally
reminiscent, it is always of good guides. “Conder,
with better drawing,” London Times said handsomely
of some of these things when shown there. It is a
delightful change for Adelaide to see a room-full of
figure sketches, and an artist who puts landscape in
its place as a background.
Some years ago, when Leroy, Talma and
Bosco (Talma being the suffragette of the
party) introduced their legerdemain, illu-
sions and livestock under the auspices of
Rickards, the bustle and gaiety of their long
turn were as remarkable as their wizardry
effects. Now that they are back in Australia,
with other performers to help them make
the time pass, their business capacity is a
little more striking than before. The enter-
tainment staged at Melbourne King’s on
Saturday is clean, bright and lively. If
the tricks of the conjuring trio are old in
some cases, and not always astounding, the
way of putting them through is invariably
quick. Things are neatly done and oppor-
tunely timed. The matter-of-fact style of
Leroy, the boss deceiver, is well contrasted
with the humorous fooling of Bosco (whose
specialty is fresh rabbits) and the vivacity
of Miss Talma. When an apparently empty
meat safe is suddenly occupied by Leroy,
who turns out to be Talma a minute later,
the conspirators appear to take as much plea-
sure in their work as Bosco when he breaks
eggs on his forehead with a view to hatching
three fat geese in an omelette. A very
happy trinity of conjurers are L., T. and B.
At one stage of the proceedings they appear
simultaneously in different parts of the house
and palm off coins, animals and bundles of
underlinen upon members of the audience.
One feels that the production of a baby ele-
phant from an elderly gent.’s breast-pocket
is within the range of Leroy’s possibilities.
As for the final illusion, in which a live lion
is an unconcerned factor, the wonder is not
that the king of beasts disappears when
Talma enters the cage, but that he doesn’t
actually swallow the Christian martyr and
then cough her into a padlocked cabinet,
whilst Bosco cries “Hey, presto!”
* * * *
The turns, apart from the conjurers, who
carry much scenery and several assistants,
are a juggler, a pair of eccentric dancers,
and one Santo Santucci, described as the
wizard of the accordeon. The tall juggler
opens the programme with a number of
Cinquevallian feats of skill and strength, and
incidentally he makes eight lightning changes
of satin jacket, and sheds seven pairs of
knee breeches behind the scenes. Of the two
dancers —Waner and White—one is moder-
ately eccentric, and the other is extrava-
gantly slim and flexible, but as regards the
accordeon wizard the present writer can
merely testify that he didn’t appear on
Saturday. Leroy stated that the accordeon
had suffered an injury to its works and
couldn’t possibly be repaired before closing
time on Saturday. For which unfortunate
incident the audience had only the con-
jurer’s word. Possibly he had changed the
musical wizard into a canary for travelling
purposes, and was unable to remember the
password.

This is the second and last week of the
trouble that arose through the marrying of
Plaza Toro’s infant daughter to the King
of Barataria’s new-born heir. Certain
members of the new co., notably tenor
McKeown, are improving on acquaintance,
whilst others remain under surveillance
owing to their defective singing. Concern-
ing Chas. Walenor, as the Duke, there is a
grave doubt as to whether he is sumciently
conscious of his noble Spanish birth, but in
consideration of the fact that Walenor is a
quaint little person who might have walked
out of an old family picture frame, and is
more decidedly tuneful than the average
comic-opera duke, The Bulletin accepts
him without demur. Saturday’s bill is
“lolanthe,” for one week only.

At Melbourne Royal a thin fabric of
farcical comedy is tinted by the glare of
red-hot bellodrama as before, and act 11. of
“Officer 666” continues to conclude with the
tableau of Sydney Stirling, in the character
of Jaek-in-the-Box, pointing a gruesome fire-
iron at Niblo’s silent head. This is about
the only moment in the farcidrama when
Niblo’s head preserves silence. As a rule he
is either delivering repartee at random, or
talking about the girl he promptly decided
to marry because she walked into his bachelor
abode and sat silently ruminating, in a won-
derful hat, over her absurd resolve to elope
with another fellow. Thereupon Niblo re-
solves to marry that girl. It’s a way he’s
got in comedy—to resolve to marry the first
girl who blows across his path of volubility.
In “Wallingford” he referred to her as “some
girl,” but in subsequent comedies it has been
that girl who sealed his fate. Niblo will
borrow the uniform of “Officer 666” every
evening until further notice.
“Aladdin” will be cheerfully foolish for
one more week at Melbourne Princess’s. Con-
sidered as a song and dance entertainment
animated by a sincere desire to earn its liv-
ing, “Aladdin” has claims to sympathy. The
little widow (Stanley) is frequently droll.
Also the suffragette who stands 7ft. high in
her boots, and comes and goes without any
attempt at explanation, has an air of being
funny on a system of her own. Moreover,
the Emperor of China gives vent to a fine
wobbly voice, and his daughter can concede
points to the usual panto, princess. Grace
Palotta, Percy Clifton and Carrie Moore
are much in evidence. “Aladdin” will keep
the house warm until Allen Doone restores
Irish drama to its loving friends on the 18th.

The moderately welcome strangers at Mel-
bourne Tivoli this week are Ralph Erroll,
from America, who sings tenor, and the
Kracht Bros., members of the gymnastic and
acrobatic clan. For the rest it may be
remarked that Gilday and Fox are about to
take their one “turn” to another sphere of
usefulness, and Beth Tate is still figuring as
a star of magnitude. Her songs are not
impressive, nor would her voice reach the
far end of an election meeting, but Miss
Tate’s clothes and deportment meet all the
requirements of her position on the bill.
“The Pool Room,” a Yankee narrative
mostly concerned with a cocaine fiend, is
revealing the qualities of Fred Hagan and
co. to Melbourne Bijou supporters. Two
new arrivals, Livermore and his Doll, pro-
vide a novel turn. Fernandez and May are
very passable trick musicians; but the
Sherratt and Lang act is severely damaged
by the piffling business of the masculine
eiement. Josephine Gassman is now warb-
ling “You Made Me Love You” with pas-
sionate niggah intensity, and only needs a
slice of watermelon and a stolen chicken to
make her bliss complete.
“The Ruby of Destiny,” the strange his-
tory of a tremendous gem, is exciting the
interest of the jewellery trade at West’s
Palace, Melbourne. The drama is rather
long, and its action is impeded by the in-
furiated efforts of a royal lady to canoodle
with the boy of her desire. “The Fugitive”
is comparatively indifferent fiction, but “The
Water Dog,” a comic, contains a diverting
idea. Next Saturday West publishes the
Cines Co.’s version of “Antony and Cleo-
patra,” with a large Dago lady appearing as
the daughter of Ptolemy, the Flute-Player.
AT POVERTY POINT.
“Ike Pagliacci”: Camped four miles from Peak
Hill (N.S.W.) is Eroni Bros.’ circus, busy painting
carriages and feeding up the menagerie. With some
60 circus souls, they have planted an antbed of in-
dustry in the middle of the bush. The Eronis are
not Dago, but were born Perry, and in Australia.
Old Bill Perry still bosses the show, though once he
left it to be a bush publican. No agent ever suc-
ceeded in making the Eroni outfit show six days a
week in the country. Years ago they played in
Melbourne to big business, and then left a lot of
money behind by closing the season suddenly. “Too
much noise here,” said “Old Bill.” Like the St.
Leons, also travelling N.S.W., the Eronis prefer to
show where a high collar doesn’t matter. Old man
St. Leon, by the way, always shaved himself without
a mirror, but swore he would cut his throat if he
didn’t gaze steadfastly at the trunk of a tree through-
out the operation.
“H. 0.”: The revival of “The Gondoliers” suggests
that Gilbert wears better than Sullivan. Nobody has
quite come up to the latter for simple tunefulness,
but his orchestration is, at times, thin compared with
the heavily-scored work of such moderns as Lehar
and Strauss. The Savoy series contains little that is
equal in musical quality to the first act of “The
Chocolate Soldier.” Gilbert, on the other hand, seems
surprisingly up to date. In fact, many of his ex-
travagant ideas have been, or probably will be, real-
ised, as have been those of Jules Verne. A while
back, f’rinstance, the belted Earl of Warwick accepted
a tip from the Doolc of Plaza-Toro, Ltd., by floating
his ancestral hall into a public co. for show purposes.
“Harrison O.”: Gregan McMahon, of Melbourne’s
Repertory Theatre, reckons he has found the real
dramatic goods in “Whither,” a play by a Melbourne
girl, May Wilkinson, and as a guarantee of good
faith he is putting the thing on for three nights
at St. Peter’s Hall this month. Miss Wilkinson
fingers a typewriter in Dalgety’s office, and a dis-
cussion by female clerks and typists on the question
of equal pay for equal work is a feature of her
play. The lady is known as a writer of short stories
and of a prize “pome” perpetrated in connection
with the Women’s Work Exhibition a few years back.
“Harrison O.”: “An Enemy of the People,” put
on in Melbourne by the Repertory Theatre, is not a
good advt. for Ibsen’s craftsmanship. He makes the
mistake of beginning his story at the beginning,
instead of in the middle, as in “Rosmersholm,” or
just before the end, as in “John Gabriel Borkman” ;
and so we get a superfluous opening act and a last
act that is pure anti-climax. And even Pinero could
not want more doors than there are in the various
interiors. In the first, second and fifth acts the
setting is two rooms with four doors, and in the
third act one room with three doors. Ibsen generally
managed to avoid making his interiors look like gaol
corridors, but he wrote “An Enemy of the People”
in a hurry, as a sort of reply to the “compact
majority” ' that damned “Ghosts,” and careless
technique, which is very obvious when the play is
staged, was one result.
“Green ’Un”: One of Vic’s small towns had a
queer entertainment on its hands lately. With press
notices for a week in advance the “Buried Alive”
man announced that as a preliminary to his per-
formance he would drive a pair of horses blindfold
through the streets, and the drive 1 nearly provided
material for several burials, as the vehicle only
traversed about four hundred yards before it col-
lided with a cartload of bluestone spalls. The
impact was disastrous to the showman’s vehicle, and
the “committee” was scattered over the roadway.
An hour later Act 11. began. A nice grave having
been dug, and a tent erected over it, a coC'.n was
produced by the local undertaker. The earth was
filled in, and sixpence a look at the sleeping beauty
(per means of a tin tube over the face of the
“cox-pse”) was to be the rule for 56 hours, after
which the “great awakening” was billed for 10 a.m.
on Friday night in the public hall. At midnight,
however, the “watch” ran up the street calling for
help. The only help available at that hour were
the comps, of the local rag, but they proved them-
selves record body snatchers. Thereafter the corpse
“eorpsed” above ground (at intervals) till the time
appointed for the resurrection. The cause of the
premature disinterment was a faulty ventilation
scheme. It nearly killed the deceased.
If the Actors’ Association can manage it (and
nobody should be able to manage it better) memorials
of J. C. Williamson are to be raised in Sydney and
Melbourne. Monday was the anniversary of his
death; and the Actors’ Council then decided to get
to work, “in view of the length of Mr. Williamson’s
career as a manager in Australia, the enterprise he
displayed, his probity, and his many well-known
benefactions to the profession.” This paper’s bless-
ing goes with the movement.
Miss Ethel Warwick, at present with “Sealed
Orders” at Adelaide, has before the London Divorce
Court a suit against her husband, Edmund Waller,
for restitution of conjugal rights. The lady has been
told that she can have the order on condition that
she files an affidavit on her return to England. Miss
Warwick is 32 years young, and made her first
appearance on the stage in 1900 in “The Corsican
Brothers,” at the Grand Theatre, Fulham. Edmund
Waller, son of Lewis, is two years younger than
his wife. He appeared first at Birmingham, in 1904,
in “Zaza.” The lady was an art student at the
Polytechnic, and the husband an engineer, before
adopting the stage.
Samuel Way, for 38 years past Chief
Justice of S. Australia, is in sad trouble.
At 78, with the grief for the death of his
wife still fresh, the poor, lonely old judge
was faced with the sudden demand to have
an arm amputated at the shoulder. On local
medical advice he trained to Sydney. with
his sister and his associate and put himself
in surgeon McCormick’s hands* and the
operation was carried through at “The
Terraces” on Monday morning.
“The Old Soldier”
The latest variety of woman candidate is Mrs,
Emily Paul, wife of the late Commander of the
N.S.W. Cadets, Colonel Paul. She claims to be the
selected Socialist candidate for Cook, in opposition to
Catts and the Rev. Yarrington. She evidently does
not know much about Socialism, for this is her
creed: “I believe in Socialism, because some of the
conditions of our present civilisation need reform.
The Labor party are not Socialists, for they believe
in conscription, a White Australia, and warfare.”
That is certainly original. What she calls conscrip-
tion is essentially a Socialist device, because it makes
every individual in the community equal. The White
Australia idea is equally Socialistic, because it com-
pulsorily keeps the race pure. To complain that the
Labor people “believe in warfare” is as absurd as to
accuse them of believing in sickness or death. War
is one of the common evils to which the world is sub-
ject, and from which every community must guard
itself. We all have to believe in warfare whether we
like it or no, just as we believe in corns and, in-
cidentally, in women.
Another gap knocked in the administra-
tive staff of the Maoriland Shipping Co.
by that old energetic, Death ! A couple of
weeks ago J. H. N. A. Burnes, manager
at Wellington (M.L.), dropped out. This
week comes news of the demise, at Christ-
church, of Isaac Gibbs, general manager in
M.L. for the big co. Gibbs was born in
England, and migrated to Sydney in his 21st
year. Four years later he was in Canter-
bury (M.L.), where he found a resting place
upon a clerk’s stool in the show which he
has left short of a good man. He was G 3
when he came to anchor, and if none but
his enemies attended the funeral he must
have dug his own grave and got into it with-
out assistance, after carrying his remains to
the place of sepulture.
F. B. Cowling, the Congregational parson
whose name came up in the Sydney Courts
last week in regard to a case affecting the
celebration of marriages, created something
of a sensation over a decade ago by a plea
for “practical religion.” He was then pastor
of the Ocean-street Congregational Church,
and he fell foul of the elders, as might have
been expected. He had enthusiastic fol-
lowers, however, who gave him a church of
his own, and for a while his sermons filled
it. Apparently the support of the godly
didn’t last, for Cowling says he celebrated
199 marriages last year and 53 in one quar-
ter, which seems like making it a business.
In January he left the Church for
commercial pursuits, after his license to
celebrate the blessed sacrament of matri-
mony had fallen overboard. Cowling was
for some time a prominent supporter of the
Labor party; but at last election he ex-
pelled himself by running as a can-
didate after another man had been chosen
by the local league. This was at Woollahra
(Sydney), and he reaped, or gleaned, 475
votes. So he didn’t pan out anything like so
well as Quodling at Monaro. Quodling got
2880 votes. Also Hocking, at Burwood,
acquired 2728, which was almost as good as
the record of Quodling, and far, far ahead of
Cowling.
- ♦ ;
A certain small row or fuss over the Com-
monwealth Bank has done something to raise
the reputation of Manager Denison Miller.
Somebody clamored to his feet in Parlia-
ment, and wanted to know if it was true
that the Commonwealth Bank was financing
some matter or other for the Broken Hill
Proprietary Co. Instead of having the mace
and the ink-bottle and all the available
Hansard thrown at him, and his hat jumped
upon, and being disqualified from the supply
of soup in the refreshment room for three
months, the inquirer was taken seriously.
The manager was informed (though not in
these words) that a buzzing Parliamentary
ass desired to know what was going on. He
replied (though not in these words) that
the Parliamentary ass could just buzz. Then
Joseph Cook made remarks about the man-
ager being an autocrat or a Czar, or words
to that effect. Now it turns out—the trans-
action being completed—that the Common-
wealth Bank is one of the underwriters in a
big B. IT. Prop, scheme, and is likely to
make a very handsome profit out of its share
of the business. But if it was the one finan-
cial institution in Australasia which had to
reveal the details of its customers’ unfinished
business to Parliament —to explain prema-
turely what they were doing or intended to
do —it is certain that profitable business of
the sort would presently pass it by on the
other side. The bank is the property of the
nation, and a member of Parliament has no
bigger share in it, and owns no more of its
capital, and has no more right to rush into
the private concerns of its customers, than
has a tram-conductor, or an ox-conductor,
or a ship’s-steward. or the first mate of a
garbage punt. So long as its accounts can
stand the regular half-yearly audit that is:
enough. The only way to give the show a
fair chance is to suppress once and for all 1
the busybodies who would fain arise in Par-
liament to ask whether Smith has been
granted an overdraft, or if it is true, that
the institution discounted Brown’s bill at
three months for £725 6s. Id.
THE CHUCKER OUT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
You know the play in which “My Lord,”
Confronted by the wretch abhorred
Who steals the papers, and beguiles
The keeper’s daughter with his wiles,
Remarks: “Begone, before I shout
For menials to throw you out!”
His lordship calls. A servant man,
With knocking knees, comes on the scene.
He’s five-feet-ten, and weighs six stone —-
A shabby thing of skin and bone.
Then, says the “heavy” to the lout:
“James, kindly throw this fellow out!”
The villain is a bulky bloke,
Whose “chiv” is hard as British oak;
lie's equal to all sorts of crime—
Has killed a dozen in his time.
But mark the super, in the bout:
He leans on him and puts him out.
Will managements for ever more
To throw the villain from the door
Employ the same expiring gent—
Consumptive, weary starved and bent,
Who makes it plain to all about
He couldn’t put a candle out?
VICTORIA.

FALLING HAIR CAUSED BY
DANDRUFF.
HOW TO DESTROY THE DANDRUFF
GERM AND SAVE YOUR HAIR.
Thin, brittle, colorless and scraggy hair is
mute evidence of a neglected scalp; of dan-
druff, that awful scourge. There is nothing
so destructive to the hair as dandruff. It
robs the hair of its lustre, its strength and
its very life, eventually producing a fever-
ishness and itching of the scalp, which if not
remedied, causes- the hair roots to shrink,
loosen and die —then the hair falls out fast.
For the purpose of destroying the dandruff
germ you should get your chemist to pre-
pare a lotion consisting of 3 ounces Bay
Rum, 1 ounce Lavona de Composee and i
dram Menthol Crystals. Rub this thoroughly
into the scalp with the finger tips. It will
kill the dandruff germ at once and after
the first application your hair will take on
that life, lustre and luxuriance which is so
beautiful. It will become wavy and fluffy,
and have the appearance of aboundance and
incomparable gloss and softness, but what
will please you most will be after just a
few weeks’ use, when you will actually see a
lot of fine downy hair —new hair —growing
all over the scalp.*
SOCIETY
The other day a man who is “wanted” in
Newcastle (N.S.W.) stepped into Mel-
bourne’s central police office, told the un-
edifying story of his life, and asked to be
returned to the city of the dreadful harbor.
The officer in charge demurred. His visitor
argued the case for his immediate apprehen-
sion with ability and force. Still the Hop
remained adamant. The other then pointed
out that a Victorian constable was presently
journeying to Newcastle with a prisoner.
Could he (the visitor) accompany him? The
Law’s minion, growing weary or bored or
something, ordered the would-be confinee to
leave the office, or suffer the consequences.
However, the persistent one stayed on and
finally prevailed upon the harassed sleuth
to pore over the list of persons for whom
warrants were out. Then the Law found its
wits and gathered him in. In view .of the
smart work on the above lines that is con-
stantly being done by the Vic. force it is a
mystery that the Bauer and other assassins
should still remain at large.

imprisoned members, who are being punished
for what others can do without incurring
anything more unpleasant than the curse of
an embittered Bumble.

“Henry Horsecollar” : Tasmania is sitting
so hard on the head of the goose which lays
the golden egg that the animal will soon be
suffocated. It proposes to raise another
£12,000 a year by a further tax on Tatter-
sail’s sweep tickets. Now Tattersall him-
self gets 10 per cent, out of the sweep money.
The agent who dodges the Federal law in
order to get the ticket requires to be paid.
There are postages for “reply,” which con-
cerns the ticket, and “result,” which con-
cerns the silly and useless old horse. If a
speculator draws a horse he is liable to be
bailed up by the owner for a quarter or a
third or a half of the possible profits, else
the beast in the glue-pot won’t start. If he
makes a win there is a trifle of bank ex-
change for the collection of the money. Then
the Tasmanian Government taxes every
ticket. Now it proposes to heap a second
tax on every ticket, and there will soon be
so little left for the gambler that it will
hardly be worth while to gamble. Wby
doesn’t Tasmania throw the last fragment of
its alleged conscience overboard and start
a plain State lottery of, say, £lOO,OO0 —a
new lottery to start as soon as the old one
is filled up? It could altogether cut out the
horse element and consequently the horse-
owner’s “cut.” (The horse race has really
no more to do with the case than a race of
two blowflies on a window pane.) It could
pay all prizes in full, less, of course, its 10
per cent, commission. This would probably
bring it in £lO,OOO a month, which is more
than it gets now ; and yet it could give the
speculator a much better and more honest run
for his money than he gets under present
conditions. The Tasmanian Government
would be not a whit less respectable if it
ran a flutter of its own than it is when it
collects a sidelong percentage on a Private
Enterprise flutter. You can’t disguise a
whited sepulchre by giving a speculator 10
per cent, to hold a blue cotton brolly over it.
There is more satisfaction and more profit
in dispensing with the speculator, and saving
the price of bis hypocritical brolly, and ad-
mitting franklv that it is your own whited
seoulchre. Why shouldn’t anybody have a
whited sepulchre if he wants one?

A corner of Pitt-street and Martin-place,
opposite the Sydney G.P.0., has changed
hands for £122,000. The Mutual life Co.
of New York was the vendor, and the Mer-
cantile Mutual Insurance Co., of Sydney, the
buyer. In the boomtime of 1802 the then-
new Martin-place frontages were offered for
sale at auction under instructions from the
Minister for Public Works (W. J. Lyne).
The first lot offered was the corner of George-
street, where the Bank of Australasia now
stands. It went up to £l7OO per foot, ihe
reserve price was concealed in an envelope,
and when an officer of the Department opened
the envelope it was found that the price fixed
was £1749. The next lot offered was that
on the corner of Pitt-street. This was knocked
down at £1500; the next going west brought
£l2OO, and the next £lOOO. All three were
purchased for the Mutual Life of New York,
Major Rennie being then manager. Tne
lots totalled £126,000, or £4OOO more than
the land and buildings have now been sold
for. It was a glorious drunk or victory or
something. * * *
“Ringsider”: The Rev. Worrall, who,
according to the Melbourne papers, was
shocked to see John Murray at a recent
Yarraside boxing match —John being,
allegedly, a respectable citizen as well as a
passionate Liberal —would be driven stalk,
staring dippy if he put in an evening at the
Sydney Stadium. I observed there, during
tlie recent Smith-Clabby battle, C. G. Wade
and half a dozen other prominent Liberal
legislators; Fred Flowers, most righteous of
Laborites; ex-Speaker Morton, repiesenting
the Independents; Captain Verney, ex-
A.D.C., to give a vice-regal touch to the
proceedings, and about half the town-dwell-
ing members of the Union and Austialian
Clubs If the churches could only attract
the crowds that the Sydney Stadium does
every Saturday night they would not only
begin to pay a dividend; they would become
fashionable.

Ecclesiastical information from “Jeames”:
The Brompton Oratory, where the Suffragettes got
such a hot time lately, is, after the Cathedral off
Victoria-street, Westminster, the newest great Roman
Catholic church in London. It is the resort ot
Kensington Romanism, just as the Farm-street
Church, which Father Vaughan has made world-
famous, is the haunt of the Catholicism of Mayfair.
The case of the Oratory is a standing example to St.
Mary’s, Sydney. No church authorities like to have
a mortgage on their edifice if they can help it. In
this spirit the Oratorians let their church stand un-
finished —an eyesore to the frequenters of one of
London’s great causeways—for years. Then the London
County Council stepped forward and said brusquely
that the nuisance had got to end, and that soon. The
Church rallied its affluent supporters. In an in-
credibly short time the necessary funds were raked
in, and the vast dome and front were finished. So
would it be with the missing section of St. Mary’s if
the public only spoke its mind vigorously on the
matter.
It was significant, by the way, that the Oratory
congregation should have been so enraged over the
Suffragette invasion. The British R.C., unlike his
co-religionist on the European Continent, is highly
reverential. In such great churches as Notre Dame
or the cathedral at Milan one feels almost as though
one were in an arcade. Sightseers walk aimlessly
about; palpable heretics sit and laugh and talk in
jirominent places; and the worshippers accept it as
a matter of course. British Catholics have never
allowed this in their show-churches ; so decisive are
they on the point, indeed, that those Protestants who
go to High Mass or Benediction to hear the famous
Oratory and Farm-street choirs mostly do their best
to imitate the genuflections and so on of the genuine
devotees. Complete casualness reigns, on the other
hand, in the great Anglican fanes —the Abbey, St.
Paul’s, Canterbury Cathedral, etc. Why it should be
so I don’t know. Perhaps the explanation is that
an Established Church feels like a person of estab-
lished position, and is not touchy about trifles.

An indignant citizen of Paris attempted
to murder a member of the Rothschild clan
the other week, and thereby hangs a moral.
The Dr. Henri de Rothschild in question
established a “milk depot for mothers” in
a street where the indignant citizen had pre-
viously been a prosperous dairyman. Hence
the latter’s objection—he didn’t want the
milk of human kindness offered gratis to his
customers, or sold at cost price. Moral:
When Philanthropy intrudes upon the
domain of Private Enterprise, it is in the
same position as its cousin Socialism. They
are equally wicked and liable to be shot at.
“Pasquin”: Anent Senator McDougal’s
wild skirl in defence of the kilt. In (say)
Prince Charlie’s time, when the Scotch
Scotchman’s “ain fireside” was more of a
death-trap than a joy, his forced retirement

change. But what of the Australian Scotch-

camp, and had an opportunity of noting the

“B.”: The probability that,
after the temporary stage has
been got over, the Governor-
General will make Concord the
official N.S.W. home fulfils
pretty accurately the vision of
the frequently maligned old
Lieutenant-Governor Grose.
Parramatta looked rather far
away in those days for a seat
of authority, so Grose proposed
to establish a line of military
residences along the route. He
therefore allotted each officer
willing to do a bit of farming
100 acres within sight of the
river, along with 10 convicts,
victualled and clothed by the
Government, and the liberal
use of such other gangs as
the Government could spare.
That was the origin of Concord
—so called because the military
and civil ideas were blent in the
scheme; a sort of sword and
ploughshare arrangement. A
neighboring settlement more
martial than civilian received
the name of the Field of
Mars, and still another,
originally intended for
Quakers, but later trans-
ferred to free
settlers in gen-
eral, was named
Liberty Plains.
In course of
time many of
the small Concord farms
amalgamated; Yaralla embraced three of
them.

John Bridge and Cos., the Sydney wool
people, have dug up anew variety of the
spud of patriotism. At Pyrmont the co. has
a showroom floor, covering two acres, which
isn’t occupied during winter —the season
when the sheep is permitted to keep its
overcoat. Instead of letting this fine build-
ing lie idle, the co. struck the idea of off ex-in g
it to the Defence Department for use during
the long winter evenings, as a sort of off-
sider to the cadet scheme. The Department
smiled on the tentative proposal; whereupon
the Bridge people got to work, fitted up a
very complete gymnasium, extended the ser-
vice of baths, lavatories, etc., and then in-
vited the Department to step in and take
possession. The inevitable few remarks at
the opening display were made on an early
evening of this week.
The Amalgamated Society of Engineers
(Sydney branch) has sent out notices to
employers intimating that its members have
decided not to work with men who are
eligible for membership, but are not members
of the union. It is a curious position, because
it looks as if the Amalgamated Engineers
pointedly refer to some men who are mem-
bers of other unions. There are two Socie-
ties of Engineers—the Amalgamated, which
is a world-wide organisation, with branches
in Australia, and the Australasian Society,
which was formed by engineers who were
opposed to the idea of yielding the control
and funds of their organisation to a central
executive in England. Also there are at
least half a dozen unions which include men
engaged in some particular branch of engi-
neering. The Gas Employees’ Union and the
Tramway Employees’ Union also enrol en-
gineers. In some employments the members
of the English society are strong enough to
compel the enrolment of all employees in
their union, but in other places other unions
could compel members of the Amalgamated
Society to join their ranks or quit. It is
not at all unlikely that Sydney, having be-
come used to strikes arising out of a dispute
between employers and employees, over
wages, or hours, or smoke-oh, or the sack-
ing of Bill Jones, will be introduced to
another sort of strike—the sort produced by
the crime committed by Brown, who is a
loyal member of the Hencoopers’ Union, in
refusing to join the Hencoop Makers and
Finishers’ Society.
“0.K.” : Marshall Hall is in England, and
Melbourne’s musical world, outside of the
University Council, is wondering whether he
is a formal candidate for his old billet of
Ormond Professor of Music, made vacant
by the death of Peterson. Years ago the
impetuous, unbusinesslike Prof, was ill-ad-
vised in tendering his resignation, instead of
allowing the precious “scandal” over his
little book of verses to blow away like smoke.
Now his eager barrackers have rushed into
the breach with a circular to the Council
suggesting that “Mr. Marshall Hall should
be pressed to accept the responsibilities of
the Ormond Professorship.” This is absurdly
foolish. The Council, which allowed the
misguided “poet” to blow himself out of the
University because Mrs. Grundy was railing
at him can’t be expected to press him to
return. The billet is about to be advertised
as vacant in the ordinary way, and there
are one or two inevitable applicants on the
spot who have reasonable claims. If Mar-
shall Hall is to be chosen in preference, he
must make an application. If he waits to
be invited by the Council he will simply
remain out of it. His friends should advise
him to lodge an application by cable and come
to Melbourne right away on a health trip—
with the chance of being appointed to the
billet when he gets there.
* * * *
A cry of woe: —
For some time past there have been rumors of grave
industrial trouble in the Burglars’ Union (Sydney 1
branch). During the previous year, the secretary
reports, employment was steady and highly remunera-
tive. Every burglar was able to earn a living wage ;
and the conditions .of employment were so easy that
the union saw no reason to invoke the Wages Board
for a higher scale of remuneration. But, naturally,
news of this pleasing state of affairs filtered through
to the other States, and the inevitable result fol-
lowed. There has been a huge influx of unemployed
burglars, and the competition of the new-comers, who
are all free-laborers, has begun to tell. No com-
plaints are made by the secretary against Sydney
householders. Their residences are just as easy to
enter as they were last year. Nor does the executive
of the Burglars’ Union complain of any undue inter-
ference with their livelihood by the Sydney police.
These are still sufficiently busy on the enrolling of
Federal and State voters to keep them happily and
healthily engaged; and in the rare cases where a
policeman is supposed to be on his beat, he is in-
variably three suburbs away closely following a clue.
The sole trouble is that there are so many burglars
at work that there are not sufficient houses to go
round. The secretary makes a strong plea for one
house one burglar ; and in cases where two or more
burglars have decided to work on the same job, pref-
erence should be given to the members of the union.
The union officials are attempting to arrange matters
by a conference with the non-unionists, but, failing
an agreement, the Burglars’ Union_ is prepared to
picket householders’ residences against interference
with their members while engaged on their job. The
real remedy, however, seems to be the building of
more houses to cope with the extra burglary demand ;
but the secretary points out that, nending their con-
struction, real hardships will still be experienced.
The matter seems one for the Wages Board.
* * * *
Some philanthropic people in Adelaide
have been given reason to feel uncomfortable.
Nearly 10 years ago Wanda Radford, a South
Aus. infant phenomenon, was taken to set
the Thames on fire with her reciting. She
got big notices. Berlin also called her a
“wunderkind.” Then she grew bigger, and
settled down to study her arts., Now, at
just IS, she can act, and dance, and sing, but
is in poor health—“undeveloped,, wants rest,”
the German doctor says. I-ler mother wrote
out pathetic appeals to raise £IOO to give
the girl another year’s chance. Money rolled
in to a Register fund. And then her father
wrote, remarking coldly that he can support
liis own family, and asking that the sub-
scriptions given “under a misapprehension”
should be returned. Philanthropists hate
to be snubbed, so the ladies who had started
the fund handed the newspaper a bunch of
really heart-rending appeals extracted from
the mother’s letters, including direct sugges-
tions for a concerted effort by the rich people
of Adelaide. The collection is accordingly
to be sent on to Berlin, barring any part of
it that subscribers mav (fairly enough, con-
sidering all things) ask to have returned to
them.

Fares on all the Sydney Ferries Company’s services,

F ERRYMAN: “Now, then, yer got to ante-up
something extra, as owing to my increasing
girth I finds it werry 'ard to take yers
across.”
Ballarat is becoming a sort of aviators'
Waterloo. First, there was Stone who,
after a flight of about 200 yards, dropped
like a man with his name should, and broke
his collarbone. Next, our own Hawker
bashed his machine about badly while giviug
an exhibition to the locals. And, recently,
Guillaux, the Gaul, met with his share of the
luck. Three-fourths of the crowd that went
to see him hung round on any sort of old
spot as long as it was outside the paying
ground. And, to top off, some energetic
crook subsequently pickpocketed what small
takings there were. The sky-pilot prayed
for his future in reinforced cuss-speech.
EPITHALAMIUM.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The “appropriate song,” “Beloved, It is Morn,”
was sung. —Frequent wedding reports.
Beloved, it is morn: the flowery arch
Trembles to hear the strains of that sweet song,
Drowning the music of the Wedding March,
Lest lazy lovers lie a-bed too long.
Beloved, it is morn: no glimpse of moon
Misleads the cock. It is the infant day
That chanticleer has hailed —bold brat that soon
With ruthless hand will pluck the night away.
And still, to warn us from his seeking eyes
That, when the curtain of the night is torn,
Will stare upon us with a bold surprise,
The song returns: Beloved, it is morn.
Now seemly armor must our joyance don
To keep us safe against rude laughter’s scorn.
Get up, my dear, and lace it stoutly on:
Wake, Missus Smith: Beloved, it is morn !

A very Interesting discovery was
made by him in Egypt. In the course
of his work he noticed that the skulls
of many long-dead Egyptians had been
worn thin on either side. This he
attributed to the wearing of the heavy
ceremonial wigs, which are familiar
ornaments in old Egyptian paintings.—

LIMITED. SYDNEY.
PERSONAL
ITEMS
The most unusual thing about the latest
round-the-world-for-a-wager tourist who has
drifted into this Commonwealth —he claims
to be a Servian nobleman, Count of Monte-
forte —is that he is doing his ramble on a
cork leg.
“Shovel” thinks it may be his fault:—
As a youngster I was present at the reception given
on election night by Nhill supporters to W. H.
Irvine, when he defeated Minister Baker for the
Lowan seat, and so got into Victorian politics. Ex-
cited townsmen met him on arrival of the Adelaide
express, placed him in a buggy, took his hat off,
adorned his classic brow with a wreath of leaves
(presumably laurel, perhaps ivy), and made asses
of themselves by yoking into the shafts and dragging
the cart to a Victoria-street pub. Incidentally, I was
a member of a very junior local cricket club - which
languished for want of fdnds. We held an impromptu
committee meeting alongside the horse-trough, and
delegated two of
us to request a
donation. The asses,
having unyoked
themselves, crowded
into the bar parlor
and waited as
though they ex-
pected something.
I and the other
plen i p o tentiary
pushed our way in.
The future knight
was beaming and
positively glowing.
Then we tapped
him for a subscrip-
tion, and as he
handed out ss. he
suddenly seemed to
feel the cold
draughts that strike
a public man,
mostly in the trou-
sers pocket. We
went outside and
gave him three
cheers, but some-
how he has never
seemed the same
man since.
WATT, ex-Premier
of Victoria and
candidate for the
House of Repre-
sentatives.
Passed out at the great age of 96 (being a
prominent man his birth-date could be veri-
fied, which prevented him becoming a cen-
tenarian), the Earl of Wemyss, the dad of
the British House of Lords, and the last of
the really-incurable Tories. Also, he was
the only peer within this writer’s knowledge
who was ever accused of stealing a whale.
B - : When Joseph Cook takes his P.C.
he will swear, according to the ceremonial,
to advise the King to the best of his cun-
ning and discretion.” Another P.C. obliga-
tion is that he “must keep the King’s coun-
sel secret.” As lie won’t get any of it, that
will be easily done. Some years ago “P.C.”
had a larger significance. Even as late as
rhe days of W. B. Dailey, who was the first
Australian-born P.C., it: meant, if circum-
stances had sent Dailey to England, intimate
association with imperial affairs. Now it is
the little dry crust thrown by “the Crown”
to the politician who, as Joe Cook’s hymn
says, “stands shivering on the brink but
fears to venture in” where the coveted titles
are.
MR. SPEAKER
MEAGHER , of
N.S.W.
H. D. Broadhead, a Christchurch (M.L.)
young man of whom the cableman spoke re-
cently as having secured a first-class in mod-
ern and mediaeval languages at Cambridge,
has had a brilliant career. He went to the
Boys’ High School at Christchurch at 13. He
won a free place there every year that he
remained, and raked in most of the form
prizes for languages. In fact, his appetite
for languages, ancient and modern, could not
be satisfied. In 1908 he won a Junior Uni-
versity Scholarship, and passed to Canter-
bury College. Later, he won a senior M.L.
’Varsity scholarship in Latin and Greek.
He went to Fogland in 1911, and within a
few days of landing annexed an open exhibi-
tion at Trinity College, Cambridge. In
March, 1913, he bagged a senior foundation
scholarship, and three months later swept up
first-class honors in the second division of
the classical tripos. As an incidental he got
his B.xV. degree. A man with all those lan-
guages should have no difficulty in getting a
job as a police-court interpreter.
General Sir Percy Girouard, who has just
been divorced from his spouse on the ground
of incompatibility of temperament, is a
Canuck who, after an apprenticeship on
the Canadian Pacific, came under the eye
of Kitchener when that judge of men was
making his way towards Khartoum.
Girouard was in charge of the laying-down
of the line which made the march through
the desert possible, and later, in S’th Africa,
“K” made him Director of Military Bail-
ways. There the peppery temper which
finally parted him from his missus was often
on exhibition, though the lady in her evidence
was good enough to ascribe it to the climate
of East Africa, where Girouard was, of late
years, doing a job as governor. One Aus-
tralian regiment, at least, could certify to
Girouard’s temper. It was on its way to
East London (the regiment, not the temper)
to ship for home, and on the road it got a
bit off the chain, and things were lively at
the stations en route. An alarmed railway
staff-officer wired to Girouard, who was at
De Aar. When the train pulled in, the joy-
riders found themselves held up in a siding
by a strong force of regulars, with fixed
bayonets. All hands were bundled out,
rifles were taken away, and bottles were
rooted out and smashed. Then Girouard
said some things through his nose to the
officers, and sent the lot along to their desti-
nation under arrest. The transport was
ready when the crowd arrived, so matters
were allowed to drop, and all hands were
hurried on board with a blessing and a
shower of curses.
Mrs. L. E. Paul, who is going to carry the
red banner of the International Socialists
against Catts in the Cook electorate, is a
woman of outstanding ability, who has made
her mark not only in socialistic but also in
art circles. She is the wife of the colonel
who used to have command of the N.S.M.
school cadets. Of course she will bottom
the poll; but her candidature will brighten
up the struggle in that district, which is
more than can be said for many another
candidature.
Ralph Stock’s cruise in search of “copy”
for a new book or two has at least sup-
plied him with a thrill. Stock and three
others —one of them his sister —left Sydney
early in June for an extended South Sea
island tour, in a small auxiliary ketch.
Wanderlust. They came back in 24 hours
for repairs. They got through the Heads
again a week later, and next were heard of
from Lord Howe Island, after a rough trip.
Then the party made for Norfolk Island,
and that was the end of the hunt; for when
they tried to enter Emily Bay in tow of a
whaler, a couple of big seas upset the
lugger, washed it on the rocks, and in a few
minutes made splinters of it. The party got
ashore very moist but otherwise undamaged.
“ Jeames”: Lord Wernyss is dead, aged
96. A Sydney paper gives him nearly a
column of space. And yet he never did any-
thing of note. His most remarkable achieve-
ment really lay in the fact that, with every
opportunity to misbehave, he spent close
on a century without being found out in
any misdemeanor of any description what-
ever. What a wealth of righteousness, or
cunning, that represents! Why, when an
utterly unimportant old man dies in the
odor of virtue, do we lavish extravagant
praise on his deceased hairs? Obviously
because we admire his subtlety and capa-
city for acting. No one could be as utterly
innocuous, for 96 years, as Lord Wemyss
was, on his form in public. We know it,
and so, in acclaiming him, we acclaim,
really, his consummate genius as a long-
distance leg-puller.
The estate of Mrs. Townend, the wealthy
Christchurch (M.L.) citizen who passed
over recently, has been sworn for probate
purposes at £BOO,OOO, but it is expected to
finally wash up at over the million mark.
The Government will collar close on to
£200,000 in stamp duty and fees. The
deceased lady remembered everybody near
her, from her private secretary and adviser,
a comparatively young man, who gets about
£30,000, and her companion, a well-known
Christchurch woman, who gets a house and
£2OOO a year for life, down to her second
chauffeur, who gets £lOO a year for life.
Sundry parsons, numerous friends, relatives,
domestic employees also get annuities, and
several of them dwelling-houses as well. Her
four trustees get £5OOO each to start off with,
and her solicitor, who had watched over her
numerous affairs for years, gets £5OOO. Then
there are numerous bequests to charities,
mostly in Christchurch, running from £lO,OOO
down to £5OO. The residue of the estate is
to be handed to a cousin in Britain, and when
he reads the' will he’ll probably begin to
wonder if there’s going to be any residue.
“Jeames” discursively :
Joseph Chamberlain, just deceased, was one of
those rare human beings who can remain spare and
in condition without exercise of any sort. Leo XIII.,
Sir Henry Irving and Cardinal Moran were of the
same type. Joseph stepped into his little one-horse
brougham early in the morning at Princes Gate,
during the Boer war days, was driven to Dowmng-
street or the House, kept the one-horse shay waiting
in case he wanted to make a journey of over 50
yards at any time, and so home bv the same convey-
ance. Though he was an enthusiastic eater and an
inordinate smoker, he kept his boyish figure to the
end—or, at any rate, he had it when I last saw him
in 1906. His second wife was a pretty young
American woman, whom his son Austen had originally
met and admired. Loyal to his father in matters
of the heart, as in all else, the young man promptly
stepped aside when the older one indicated his
wishes. So Joseph remarried and acquired a most
devoted spouse, and —so far as the last eight or
10 wears of his life went—a highly competent nurse.
The first time T heard him speak was in the Park
at Blenheim, when the S’African war was looming.
His voice production must have been perfect, since,
though he never shouted —in fact, his tone was con-
versational to a degree—he could be heard on the
outskirts of the biggest crowd. He went down with
the populace in part, perhaps, because he spoke in
a language which the meanest intelligence could
understand,. unlike such flamboyant orators -as Glad-
stone, whom no one could comprehend at times.
Chamberlain was never guilty of a Greek or Latin
quotation, so far as I have heard, and he despised
poetry. It has been said of him that the only
classical author with whom he had any acquaintance
was Dickens. He was as unintellectual, probably, as
any great Britisher since Palmerston; and yet, by
reason of his force, he was the natural leader, at
about the time paralysis overtook him, of a party
which includes men like Balfour and the two Cecils.
A fellow-menial once told me that he was in the room
when Winston Churchill received the news that Joe
Chamberlain’s political end had arrived —that he had
been smitten by a stroke from which he could never
recover. Involuntarily Churchill said, under his
breath: “My God, if I had only known it was
coming!” Churchill had just previously declared
irrevocably for Freetrade and Liberalism. The in-
ference is that had he suspected that the seemingly
strong and youthful man, who was Conservatism’s
inevitable leader, was about to go out of the political
game he would have steered his course differently.
However, the whole story may be a lie. Twenty
years in the service of the nobility and gentry have
gone far to convince me that most of these stories are
lies.

Deputy Surveyor-General.
Mrs. Frazer, who died in Sydney last week,
aged 79, was widow of John Frazer, • a
wealthy merchant, now some years dead.
John Frazer, in the ’4o’s, was a commercial
clerk living in Fort-street. Presently lie
started a grocery in Sussex-street, and a
little later on occupied a corner store whic.l
became gruesome or historic or notorious,
inasmuch as in it was murdered a widowed
shopkeeper, Mrs. Jamison, by the well-con-
nected scoundrel, John Knatchbull. I razor
clambered up to be the head of the big firm
of John Frazer and Co., having as partners
James Watson (one-time Treasurer) and
James Ewan.

making promises
to artists.
“Pick”: At Digman’s Creek (N.S.W.),
last week, Bob O’Neil closed his account
with Time after tallying 74 years. In his
day he had been a mighty trundler amongst
the cricketing fraternity of the Braidwood
and South Coast districts. He was other-
wise notorious as the game supporter who
offered to stand shoulder to shoulder
with Austin Chapman if the latter would
tackle with his fists a hostile importin';
crowd whom he couldn’t convert with his
eloquence.
THE FOOL’S SONG.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Appalling realistic force.... The dreadful misery
with which Miss Starr clothes the character....
Madame X drifted through the dregs of life in places
to which the most pitiful wrecks of womanhood are
cast. . . .She is drunken ; she is diseased ; her mind is
half-destroyed hv drugs. . . .Miss Starr gave a horrify-
ing picture. —Extracts from a Sydney Sunday paper’s
laudatory notice of “Madame X.”
This life is far too gay—
At least, I find it so ;
Because I laugh all day,
I like a horrid show.
Grim worry and dull care,
And poverty and death —
These things are far too rare.
I laugh with ev’ry breath.
The cost of living’s so
Ridiculously cheap,
We all have cash to throw
Away, and time to sleep.
Joy is a common thing,
So sorrow we must seek ;
The actress who can wring
Some tears gets pounds a week!
And she who can portray
Disease and madness, too,
Is lauded night and day.
We are a funny crew !
Life is so sweet and bright—
So healthful, kind and sane,
We pay to get a fright,
And gorge ourselves with pain.
So this mad song I sing,
Like any straw-haired fool:
“Oh, Joy’s a common thing,
But Misery’s a jool!”
N.s.w.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
The N. S. Wales State Treasury claims a
surplus of £373,039 for the past year, as against a
deficit of £1,228,380 for the preceding year. Nomin-
ally, therefore, things have improved by £1,601,419.
But a good deal of the improvement exists only on
paper. Thus in 1912-13 £450,602 went in various
redemptions of debt, whereas in the past year only
£ 5631 was applied to that purpose —a shrinkage of
£444,971. In the same way grants-in-aid have been
reduced from £275,000 to nothing, and the Public
Works Fund, which is supposed to be fed primarily
on land-sales money, has had £23,157 less. The
balance of the “improvement” has been made up of
extra taxation, the pennies and ha’pennies added to
the tramfares, extra railway rates, etc. From
income tax alone an extra £632,045 has been
drawn (the total for the year is £1,393,841) and
stamp duties have brought in an additional
£279,917. It has taken all these cuts at sinking
funds, etc., and all these additions to direct and
indirect taxation to meet the constantly growing ex-
penditure and give a poor little £373(039 to the
good.
And, of course, that isn’t a legitipiate surplus.
The “land revenue” for the year was £1,762,876,
which differs very little from that of the previous
year. Now, in the previous year £999,363 of the
total “land revenue” was derived from land sales ; and
land-sales money isn’t revtenue. Assuming that
£950,000 came from this source in the past year,
then the alleged surplus is changed into a real
deficit of about £577,000. True, conscience-stricken
to some extent by the wholesale misappropriation of
this capital derived from the sale of the land, Parlia-
ment three or four years ago decided that certain
of the money (usually about a third of the lot)
should be passed to a Public Works Fund. That was
all very good so long as the Public Works Fund was
employed to purchase new assets. But, as a matter
of fact, instead of paying for public buildings, etc.,
out of this realised capital, such things are now
charged up to loans, and the Works Fund is mostly
used for tinkering jobs which should be charged
against revenue in any ease. The Labor Government
started off virtuously enough. But virtue is its
own reward, and this Government wanted much
better payment than that.
However, let the Government have whatever credit
it deserves for putting on enough taxes, direct and
indirect, to at least show a surplus according to the
methods of finance in vogue, whereas in the previous
year it showed a deficit even on that rotten system.
But precious little credit is deserved. In the first
place, no visible attempt was made to reduce expendi-
ture —except by garrotting or starving the sinking
funds. Irrespective of grants-in-aid and sinking fund
contributions, the expenditure went from £16,911,389
in 1912-13 to £17,948,679 in 1913-14. The actual
Government expenditure was therefore over a million
greater. This Government doesn’t know what economy
means. It is the wildest and most prodigal crowd
in Australian history. It is borrowing so madly
that the interest bill last year was £405,601 greater
than in the previous year, and 28 p.c. greater than
it was onlv four years ago ; and the money has been
poured wildly into undertakings which, with those
previously .in existence, bring in 44 p.c. more than
they did four years ago, while their working exes,
are 64 p.c. more. There can be only one outcome of
all this—an intolerable burden of taxation.
Last year the expenditure out of loans was
£8.996,540, exclusive of lOIJ’s repaid. The dreadful
record of the reckless State is shown hereunder: —
1907 ..£1,058,553 | 1911 ..£3,921,758
1908 . . 1,965.329 I 1912 . . 6,491,103
1909 .. 2,906,507 I 1913 .. 7,703,594
1910 . . 3,246,640 | 1914 . . 8,996,540
In 1907 the taxation amounted to 18s. 2d. per head,
and the surplus was so great that the taxation was
plainly at least 4s. per head too much. In 1911-12,
the year when the wild borrowing started, the taxa-
tion suddenly sprang to £1 2s. 6d. per head. In
1912-13 it was only 15s. lOd. per head; but as there
was a deficit of £1,228,000, it was obviously much
too little to keep the accounts square. In past
year the revenue from taxation was £2,437,539,
which means about £1 7s. 2d. per head. The more
the Government has borrowed the more it has thus
been necessary to wring out of the public in taxation,
in addition to increased tramway and railway fares,
etc This movement is not accidental. It is inevit-
able. A borrowing boom means waste and bad value
for the outlay; and the public has to make the short-
age good out of its own pocket.
Incidentally, there is another point. In two years
about £16,000,000 of borrowed money has been
spent by the Government. This, partly by creating
a fictitious demand for labor, has produced an utterly
fictitious prosperity, which has been reflected most
plainly in property values and rents. In 1910,
according to the excellent ICnibbs, you could buy for
18s. lOd. in Melbourne what would have cost you
19s. lid. in Sydnev. taking the average of the
necessaries of life. That was a difference of Is. Id. in
favor of Melbourne. Since then Victoria has borrowed
little while N. S. Wales has borrowed madly, and in
1913 what you could get for 21s. in Melbourne you
would have to pay 235. 7d. for in Sydney—a differ-
ence in favor of Melbourne of 2s. 7d. The borrowing
orgy does something more, then, than nroduce extra
taxation: it adds, also, to the cost of living, and is
thus an indirect as well as a direct tax.

The co. was formed in 1902 with a capital of
£250,000, half in 6 p.c. pref. shares and the other

and a weeklv paper in Melbourne. Goodwill repre-
sented £198,000 of the capital. But as the goodwill
of a newspaper is a most mercurial asset, the direc-
tors set to work at once to replace it by something
with move substance in it. Fortunately -for the
shareholders, opposition held off —almost miracu-
lously, it has sometimes seemed—and that and a long
run of years of prosperity have allowed them to get
a little way off the perilously thin ice on which they
skated at first. Here are some detailed figures:—

Like most of the others, this timber co. has been
having a great time. The mere handful of people in
N. S. Wales has spent £16,000,000 of Government
loan money during the past two years, and in the
scattering of cash with a lordly hand Allen Taylor,
Ltd., has been in a position to pick up more than a
crumb or two. So far as can be ascertained the
management has been under no delusion as to the
more or less ephemeral character of the harvest
Hence, in addition to what the directors declare to
be ample depreciation allowances,” which have been
written off profits, an insurance and general reserve
fund has been established apart from the business: it
now is £21,800 in credit, the bulk of it on deposit
or invested in Sydney City Council debentures. Fur-
ther, no large risks are taken by piling up liabilities
represented on the other side by stocks that may be
easy enough to shift just now, but mav not be so

bilities total £37,386, and apart from the insurance

consume interest and insurance money. As for stocks*
on their valuation profits depend very largely. Ob-
viously if a co. values its stocks at more than they
cost, it inflates its present profits and reduces its
prospective profits. Hitherto stocks have been de-
scribed by the directors as “valued under cost” ; this

valued,” which may be the same thing, but isn’t so
definite and satisfactory. However, that isn’t enough
to spoil the taste of an exceptionally attractive bal-
