But the 10 p.c. dividend for the half-year to Septem-
ber, ’l3, hasn’t actually been paid yet. McKenzies
are in the wholesale trade; that is to say, they deal
direct with the log-choppers, and the log-choppers
have to be financed. But it is one thing to chop down
a tree and another to get it to market. Mostly the
timber is brought down the rivers ; and the rivers of
Australia and Maoriland are erratic worms. Some-
times logs accumulate in vast quantities, waiting for
enough water to float them down, or (on the N.S.W.
North Coast particularly) pending a favorable bar
harbor. Last summer was unusually dry in Maori-
land ; hence stocks piled up there, and every avail-
able penny was wanted by this co. to finance the
timber-getters. So the 10 p.c. div., though duly
passed, wasn’t actuallv paid; and for the same reason
no dividend is declared now, though the directors hold
out hopes of an interim. They- have the power to
declare one. Logs are coming down now all right;
and the market for timber is still excellent, though
not quite as abnormally good as it was (say) 12
months ago. It is possible, therefore, that the with-
held plum will fall into shareholders’ mouths before
long.
All the same, a keen eye ought to be kept on the
growing liabilities: —
Mar., 1910 . . £45-,606 Sept., 1912 . . £72,276
Sept., 1910 .. 45,112 Mar., 1913 .. 55,836
Mar., 1911 . . 32,463 Sept., 1913 . . 70,958
Sept., 1911 . . 38,768 Mar., 1914 . . 83,444
Mar., 1912 . . 46,796
Thus in four years the co. has got about £38,000
further into debt, notwithstanding that £32,000
more capital has been gathered in. The explanation
lies not merely in the growth of advances on logs,
etc. (which is mostly a temporary matter, as ex-
plained above), but in the tremendous increase of
stocks. Compare the figures of the present and three
years ago:—
Mar., 1911. Mar., 1914.
Advances .. .. £22,794 £53,167
Stocks .. . . 54,906 119,131
Total . . . . £77,700 £172,298
If timber-getting is to cost more money presently, it
may be very good business to pile up stocks like this.
But if the demand for timber should slump, as it
will sooner or later, tnen the co. may find no advan-
tage in accumulating these big stocks, which mean-
time cost a lot of money in interest, not to mention
insurance, etc. In short, besides being a timber
merchant, the co. seems to be backing continued
high prices (which means to a great extent the main-
tenance of the construction boom) ; and it is for the
investor to say whether he reckons that this is some-
thing in favor of the co. and the stock or otherwise.
The profit shown for the past half-year is much less
than usual. The directors say: “In consequence of
the increase in wages and other exes, the profit is
smaller; but arrangements have now been entered
into which will put the business on a more profitable
footing.’’ Naturally the piling up of stocks in log
and at the mills and yards cut into profits, because
no doubt most of the £83,444 owed by the co.
carries interest; anti if the accumulation in log
meant that the co. couldn’t complete sales, the ex-
planation is sound enough. As for profits, by the way>
the table puts them at £6594; but £1460 of that
is transferred to a depreciation fund, and probably
that ought to be written oil. Mostly much more
than £1460 is struck off for depreciation; and that
suggests that the £5134 net profit claimed for the half-
year might be overstated. Everything, however,
depends on the valuation of the stock. If the large
additions have been taken in at cost price, and the
declared profit based only on sales, there should be a
good time ahead, even though the timber market
shouldn’t continue so exceptionally strong; on the
other hand, if stocks have been taken at more than
cost price it means that future profits have been
discounted. Which practice has been adopted Tub
Bulletin doesn’t know; hence it confesses that it
doesn’t know just about the most important thing
that there is to know regarding this co. What it
does know about the trade generally is that it is one
of quick, fat profits under favorable conditions, and of
long and very cold seasons when trade conditions arc
not favorable. In these latter periods the timber-
men have to pursue custom across the earth; whereas
of late the Australian demand has been so good that
a lot of them haven’t even looked as far afield as
South Africa, which was a fine market a few years
ago.

The real profits for the past year were probably
about £15,000. During the year capital Infs been
increased from £80,137 to £93,000. r llle new
shares were sold by tender, and “were disposed ot at
highly satisfactory . premiums.” No mention is made
of the size of those premiums—the highly unsatisfac-
tory method is adopted of dropping this premium
capital into profit and loss account, where it should
never appear. Apparently instalments of premiums
on shares disposed of in the previous year have also
been included. In past years revenue from entrance
fees has been usually about £2OO. Last year; “fees
and premiums” brought in £4023; so it looks as
though the profit shown in the last line is :over-
stated by about £3BOO, which would be about the
total of the premiums. Business has been pretty
brisk during the year, and the directors put this down
to the withdrawal of business competitors on account
of the stringent money market. Prevailing high
rents have also no doubt driven scores of people to
go to this and other institutions for help to become
their own landlords. And as building-society terms
are easily complied with during periods of high
wages, the co.’s figures have lately had a quick bulge.
This table shows how new advances have been
moving:—
1907 .. £52,514 | 1911 .. £89,643
1908 .. 51,245 I 1912 .. 115,455
1909 .. 64,004 ! 1913 .. 116,687
1910 .. 76,622 | 1914 . . 104,825
Thus, notwithstanding the growing demand, the co.,
in view of the very heavy and probably quite abnor-
mal cost of building, has wisely put on the brake.
As it is, the amount of advances on mortgages is
£336,553, and to finance this the co. has taken
£221,912 of deposits. Besides the mortgages the
co. has £30,080 in liquid assets and £18,604 in
Pitt-street (Sydney) office property, the latter at pre-
sent almost as good as liquid ; total £48,684. Deduct
that from the £221,912 of deposits, and you have
£336,553 of mortgages to secure £173,228 of
deposits. The other liabilities are a few pounds only
of unclaimed balances, etc. This means that the
whole of the securities would have to slump very
nearly 10s. in the £ below book value to endanger
the deposits; and though the very rottenest of the
securities might behave as badly as that, It is
almost impossible to conceive of the whole lot doing
it. While there is thus no room for depositors to
feel any uneasiness, shareholders are entitled to
reflect comfortably on a solid business connection,
and on profits which for many years past have been
so much in excess of the dividends that it would
take a severe bump to knock the latter down-from
their present perch.
NEWCASTLE-WaLLSENI) COAL Co. —As an-
nounced in a recent Bulletin, this co. proposes to
double its capital by an issue of bonus shares.
The co. is, however, advised that the business will
have to be, as it were, manoeuvred through. Though
the capital has for a ldng while stood at £lOO,OOO
it seems that about 40 years ago “the then directors
returned'to the then proprietors the sum of £60,000,
thereby reducing the amount paid up on the shares
to £4 each, and reducing the paid-up capital to
£40,000.” The directors are now advised that “no
increase of capital can be effected until the initial
authorised capital of £lOO,OOO has been restored.”
They don’t explain how it fs that the capital has
for years past stood in the balance-sheet at £lOO,OOO,
though it is alleged to have been reduced to £40,000.
Anyhow, the scheme mow is to call up £6 per share
and at the same time “declare a bonus of £6 per
share out of the surplus capital and undivided profits
of the co. ’ This seems an extraordinary proposal.
Car. a co. declare a bonus out of “sur plus capital ’ :
Xo doubt it can return capital; but when it does so
it must surely write its capital down, otherwise we
should have the remarkable spectacle of cos. mas-
querading as possessed of large capital when, as a
matter of fact, they had entirely divested themselves
of it. No doubt, having reserves of upwards of
£60,000, the co. may therefrom pay out a bonus of
£60,000 in shares, thus (in other words) capitalising
its reserves. But this will exhaust its visible reserves ;
and it proposes to issue another £lOO,OOO of bonus
share capital. The only visible way in which this
can be done is by disclosing inner reserves; that is
to say, by writing up its assets. And it is a moot
point whether a co. can write up its assets for the
purpose of a distribution of bonus shares. Some
X. S. Wales cos. have certainly been advised that it
can’t be done legally'; one was advised that it couldn’t
even restore values written off. though (as in the
case of the St. Joseph’s Building Society') it might
restore value written off in order to restore capital
written off shares. Presumably the Wallsend has
got the best of advice ; if it hasn’t—well, there are
a lot of pitfalls that way.

This co. takes the place in Victoria that the
Coastal Farmers’ Co-operative fills in N. S. Wales,
its shares being held mostly by co-operative butter
factories, whose output it markets either locally or
in Europe. On the whole, its members appear to be
much better co-operators than those of the Coastal
Farmers, not being so ravenous for cash bonuses.
When it was formed 10 years ago, its capital was
only £200; now it is £i7,979, and practically the
whole of the increase has been brought about by the
shareholders very prudently taking the greater part
of their bonuses in shares instead of cash. Last
year the capital was increased by £4510 by this
means; this year another £4OlO (out of a total
distribution of £5555) goes in the same way.
The authorised capital is £40,000. At the present
rate of progress, the whole of the capital should be
paid up in about six years. Then, assuming that
£40,000 is enough trading capital, the profit will
be available for cash distribution and the share-
holders will draw their dividend on the money
they' have put back into the business as well as the
trifle they actually paid out of pocket. Besides its
buttery; the co. runs a box factory; and though it
owns up to a loss for the year of £67, the various
butter factories and farmers, through it, have made
an estimated saving of £l6OO. The eo.’s turnover
for the year was £811,062. which compares very
favorably with £552,807 five years ago. Naturally
with so large a turnover and so small a share capital
the co. has to lean to some extent on its banker.
At the end of April, liabilities amounted to £42,702,
which includes an overdraft of £28,352. It is
astonishing that the figures are so small; nothing
but the breaking-in of the retailers to practically a
C.O.D. system could make it possible. There is
also ai grain and farm-produce department, but so
far it hasn’t reached any very large proportions.
Australian Marine Fibres, Ltd. — This
co., formed early in 1911, has made no sure pro-
gress out of the bog into which precipitation and
wild estimates landed it at the outset. The object
of the co. was to dredge up the fibrous remains of
a marine plant which grows in large crops in Spen-
cer’s Gulf (S.A.). The allegation was that this stuff
would have an enormous sale for use in the manu-
facture of blankets, serges, textile fabrics, carpets,
matting and other things. Some very rank estimates,
etc., were put forward by an English co. The pro-
moters of this Australian co., using these unreliable
figures, constructed a beautiful castle, with an annual
profit of £140,000 waiting in the cellar. On “very
conservative” figures supplied by somebody else the
promoters said that a profit of £40,450 would do to
go on with. There is no reason to suppose that the
promoters didn’t believe all this; at any rate, they
took no cash, but were satisfied with 310,000 shares
(55.). The public was offered another 240,000, but
applied for only 122,144. The estimates in the
prospectus were based on the work of three dredges ;
as a matter of fact, but one dredge has been bought,
and not only has all the capital been exhausted, but
the co. is £3147 in debt, and has given the bank a
mortgage over its assets, backed by the “joint and
several” of the directors. The prospectus alleged:
“There is no expensive development or other pre-
liminary work to be done. The fibre is easily and
cheaply raised; it is already deglutinised by the
process of nature, and after washing and drying is
practically ready for the manufacturer’s use.” In
this third year of the co.’s life the proceeds of fibre
sales amounted to only £572, and the “development
account” stands at £9494; that is to say, it has so
far cost £10,065, in addition to the outlay on leases,
dredge, jetty, etc., to put £572 worth of stuff on
the market. Nor is the development work finished
yet. The market, which three years ago was alleged
to be avid for the material, simply does not yet
exist. What little has been sold appears to have
been mostly sold locally, not for the manufacture of
fine fabrics, but for rope bagging and boiler covering.
Of course things are going to be all right, though
the piping is now in a minor key. The managing
director spent 12 months in Europe, and can merely
say that “the beneficial results of my visit are now,
I believe, about to be obtained.” It is all very
discouraging ; and yet, on the reports of wool-manu-
facturers, upholsterers, etc., there ought to be a
market. There were, among others, Middlemost
Bros. & Co., Ltd., of the Clough House Mills, Hud-
dersfield, who said they had tested the stuff in the
manufacture of tweeds, and considered it suitable
and worth 2d. per lb. : and E. J. Bickford, a Sydney
furniture man with a lot of experience, reported that
it had great advantages over hair, kapok, flock and
other usual upholstering material in that its “original
cost is less, insurance is nominal on account of its
non-inflammability, and it can be handled with per-
fect safety.” The co. started with too much “water,”
too little capital, and too terrific a screech. But
these things don’t affect the value of the fibre or the
quantity available. There appears to be no doubt on
the latter point at least. But it appears to be very
doubtful whether the present co. can last till a big
enough market has been found or made.
If J. S. Inch, the managing director of
the Co-o». Insurance Co. (Svdney), was responsible for
asking £50,000 from the Sydnev Sun for an alleged
libel, he was a very mise-uided man. If what .the
Sun said was a libel at all it was too mild to make
anyone blink, let alone scotch his whiskers, and to
ask £ 50,000 for it was merely calculated to suggest
to Judge and jury that the'plaintiff didn’t know the
value of figures. Therefore, as this was a case in
which the value of figures was everything, the co.
prejudiced its case before it went into court. Three-
fourths of the proceedings hung on the question
whether the ,co.’’s. outlay in excess of revenue at the
end of the second year could be described as an
accumulated loss’; and in the welter of words policy-
holders and others interested got more fogged pro-
bably than they .were before the case started. Hence
it is just well to reproduce some of what The
Bulletin said about the co. and its second balance-
sheet (B. 4/12/’l3) :

That doesn’t look well; but it would be almost im-
possible for the figures of any insurance co. to look
well for the first two or three years. This, at any
rate, can be said: the co., though it got much more
business in the second year, didn’t go nearly as far
to leeway in getting it. Really, of course, though a
deficit of £20,488 is shown above, the co. is more
money out of pocket than that, because the income
isn’t profit—the co. has to supply insurance for it.
The exact position can’t be ascertained till a valua-
tion has been made of the policy liabilities; in
other words, until we know what the co. ought to
have saved up at the end of the second year towards
paving the policies as they fall due. For the
prkent, all that can be said is that, if every policy-
holder forfeited his policy now, and no more claims
had to be naid, the co. would have £31;791 of its
total capital of £52,279 available to refund to
shareholders, assuming that its assets are worth 20s.
in the £. If it had to pay another co. to take
over its contracts there would be less. On the other
hand, the co. has (at great expense, no doubt) estab-
lished a certain connection and goodwill.”
The heavy outlay of an insurance office in its early
years always does cause trouble. Xo eo. is prepared
to admit that the money has gone for good ; in the
same way a firm which has spent £lO,OOO on an
advertising campaign in a year isn’t prepared to
admit that it has lost £BOOO because at the end of
that year the shrieking has brought in only £2OOO
of net revenue. Even a Judge or a barrister wouldn’t
admit that, if his training cost him £lOOO, there
was a dead loss of £ 995: at the end of the first
rear’s practice which brought him in only a fiver.
On the other hand, what can you do with the £995
but show it as a deficit? The best you can do is to
sav that possibly the outlay will bring in a harvest
by-and-bve. But there is no surety about it. The
man may die, or get tongue-tied, or get weary of
waiting for briefs and go on the land or the press
or the drunk, as many have done. And an insur-
ance co. is in the same position; after spending its
”20,000 in excess of income, it may find that all its
“business extension” expenditure isn’t drawing busi-
ness worth a cent. The most any critic can say is
tt(at the seed has been sown; but whether it will
come up thistles, or whether it will come up any-
thing at all, or whether it will ripen if it does come
up, he can’t say. The whole trouble is that the
optimistic insurance' husbandman wants him to
assume a 20-bushel harvest; and the critic simply
can’t do it and be honest. So the verdict in the
Sun case was for the defendant; and Tiie Bulletin
is wondering whether the Co-op. will next year show
the costs as a goodwill asset, too.
B. H. Block 1(1 mill is making remarkably
high recoveries from 11 p.c. lead ore. In four weeks 1044
tons of concentrates, worth 29.50 z. silver and 05.5 p.c.
lead, and loaded with 5.4 p.c. zinc, came from 8234
tons of crudes assaying B.loz. silver, 11.1 p.c. load and
11.9 p.c. zinc. This means a recovery of 74 p.c of the
lead originally in the ore. But as nearly 8 tons of
nudes are required to produce a ton of concentrates,
high recoveries don’t necessarily imply large profits.
The mine is computed to have a life of 24 years;
but' there are chances of other bunches of ore bein"
found, and these may extend that period a trifle. “

figures read well they fail to flatter, because nearly

leader. New Chum Goldfields, with £17,000, not

Goldfields. Central Blue sank to the fourth rilace
in the div. list, and will go lower unless mining
below 850 ft. becomes profitable. The sad feature,
then, at Bendigo, is that the- old deep mines have
to poke ((bout in the shallow ground for patches left
rears back. This is not nermanence, so that a
change in the fortune of the field is rot possible
tHI pluckv pebnle have a shot at neglected side-
lines. The inducement is in the reward that
went to the old hands who, by sinking from the
surface makes, on the jChurri a”d Moon lines, set
the field on its feet, thus enabling it to build up
the greater portion of the output of 19,793,0000 z.
standing as its record.

South Berry, Creswiek (Vic.), expects to
nay, a liquidation dividend of 2s. The co. attempted
to make a block of the old West Berry claim pay-
able, but the wide field of wash hardly nave wages
out of the gbld won, and occasional calls had to
be made. It took six years’ work to find this out :
but at last word has come to pull up the rails and
auction the plant. Thus passes out another way-
farer in Alee Peacock’s office. Lucky for him that
he synchronises this loss with the gain of Watt’s
salary as Premier.
*.* * *
What a record is that of the Berry lead,
where the shutting down of the South Berry means
the end of mining in Creswiek ! From the rush of
1851 in the shallow ground to 1914, £20.000.000
of gold has come out of the field. Yet Madame
Berry was the only mine to get an output of over
£1,500,000 of gold. Of that, £855,000 went as
dividends to shareholders. Next door, the Berry
Consols distributed £375,895 from £927,649 of gold
won. Higher up the gutter the Lone Hand and the
Ristori were responsible for yields worth £517,330
and £430,918 respectively, and for dividends of
£244,450 and £199,500. These four giants were
the only cos. to pay six-figure dividends. The greatest
duffer in the field was Spring Hill Leads, which,
on a called-up capital of £117,000 and an output
of over 31,0000 z., never paid a dividend. Taking a
group of 30 mines, many of which in the shallow
ground were pocket-handkerchief claims, yields
aggregated 1,595,0000 z., worth £6,532,000, ‘while
dividends were £2,430,000. On most of the gold
won royalties of from to 10 p.e. were paid to the
owners of the surface ground, who in that way bled
the cos. for nearly £400,000.

Cornish mine 3 Daylesford (Vic.), is likely
to open anotner chapter in its mercurial history, the
Government having agreed to grant a new co. £2500
if it planks down £lOOO gradually by threepenny
calls. This effort should not mean ruin or hardship
to 1). McLeod, Peter Sutherland, George Buchanan,
W. J. Iloare, R. W. Shetland and R. W. Hinkson,
the new directors. A firm of machinery merchants
bought the lease a 30-head battery from the liquida-
tor of a London co., and sold it to the new crowd
for £3750. The proposal for the fresh trial is to
sink Bonnard’s shaft, r.ow 966 ft., another 200 ft. and
crosscut both ways. The first of the Cornish cos.
won 173,0000 z. from 650,000 tons of sJdwt. stone.
The second co. crushed 46,649 tons for 25,5360 z., or
about lOdwt. per ton. These records of the shallow
levels appear to justify further exploration at a
depth. But the Cornish is not an easy mine to pro-
spect, and the Board should look round for a mine
manager with a nose like Sam Bevy’s. He could
smell gold as easily as a cat can scent blue cod.
The last English co. failed to make the mine pay
because while Bevy was sniffing round for shoots of
gold the English directors were using their noses
in locating directors’ fees and smelling rats in an
over-boosted share market. Shares bought in Bavles-
ford at 6s. were sold in thousands in London at 18s.
Meekatharra (W.A.) produced 22,G610z.
more gold in 1913 than in 1912, and the last six
months’ returns foreshadow a further increase for
1914. There is now milling power equal to 110
stamps on the field, and more stamps are being
erected on the lngliston (late Commodore South)
and Macquarie leases. The Fenian and lngliston
Consols pay big monthly divs., and both are looking
well in the lower levels, lngliston Extended, which
has been up against dyke intrusions for years, is also
said to be in good condition, while the Queen of the
Hills financed b.v Lake \ iew and Oroya—is ex-
pected to make its first distribution at any time.
The Commodore and several others are making con-
stant small profits. The development of the field,
however, is slow because most of the shows are
privately owned, and holders are equipping and open-
ing up their claims out of profits. Nowhere in the
West can be found the same amount of confidence in
the future. The value of Meekatharra’s gold produc-
tion to date is a little under £2.500,000.

Great Boulder’s 20th annual report has
come to hand, and, though not so attractive as some
of its pi cdecessors, it shows that the mill still has
a few years work ahead of it. The net value of the
gold won during 1913 was £559,830, the lowest for
several years, and the net profit was £293,800. Bivi-
dends, maintained on the 3s. per share basis,
absorbed £262,500, leaving (after allowing for other
disbursements) £lBBO to carry forward. Had the
same amount been allowed for depreciation as in
1912, the funds in hand would have been sufficient
to maintain the divs. at the rate mentioned. As
regards ore reserves, the latest estimate shows a little
shrinkage, the figures being 615,114 tons, averaging
14.57dwt., as compared with 652.916 tons, averaging
14.59dwt., at the end of the previous year. The
tonnage now in sight represents just over three years’
supply for the plant. While no fresh finds of import-
ance have been made, the ore in some of the bottom
main levels has improved so much that it is now
included in the reserves.' The quantity of ore ex-
posed- in Hamilton’s workings at the extreme north
end of the property has also been increased in the
process of stripping the lode. Although the best
geological opinion obtainable holds out little hope
that the co. will find anything of importance, it is
intended to bore to a depth of 350 ft. below the pre-
sent bottom of the mine at 2800 ft Strenuous efforts
were made b.v the management and Board during the
year to get hold of other properties in Which invest-
ment of available funds could be made ; but although
a number were examined, none were deemed good
enough to persevere with. A controlling interest has,
however, been acquired on option in an Alaskan mine,
which is declared to have good prospects. Manager
Hamilton will inspect it towards the end of this
year. The co. also has the M agd a 1 a - cum -Moonlight
show, at Stawell (Vic.) ; but, as previously hinted on
tins page, the option is likely to be turned down.

Boulder Perseverance’s latest quarterly
report shows how, after an outlay of many hundreds
of thousands of pounds in development and plant,
ore worth under 20s. per ton can r.ow be profitably
mined and treated at Ivalgoorlie. Altogether, 61,156
tons were handled during the three months for
bullion valued at £60,660, or a shade over 19s. lOd.
per ton. Working costs averaged a decimal over
17s. 2d. The report adds that more than half of the
ore during the quarter assayed £1 and over per
ton and that the average was well up to that for
the whole of the mine. Ore reserves remain practi-
cally unaltered, except that broken stone shows a
further increase.
The option-hunting Golden Ridge (W.A.)
has decided to give the Bohemian lease at Marvel
Loch a trial. Some good crushing®, probably made
up of picked stone, were obtained from 'shallow
ground in this show, and after the local paper and
other optimists had whooped about its value a Mel-
bourne syndicate came along and took an option,
but deckled, after doing a bit of work, that it did
not want the property. Since then several have
nibbled, but no one has swallowed the bait.

rise in Associated Northerns (W.A.) was due to
someone spreading a false report about free gold
being struck in a prospecting shaft on the eo.’s
Victorious mine at Ora Banda. Roberts adds that
there is nothing very dazzling about the present
apnearance of the property, and that things will

on , \ n October- Like the district in which
is situated, the show may be safelv regarded as
one which has seen its best davs.

“Stannifer”; The North Queensland
opinion is that Lode Hill has seen its best days. The
tin-bearing country is only in a narrow strip running
up a spur of the mountain. The whole mountain does
not carry payable tin, as the southern public appears
to think. Xorthev put three weeks’ easy sluicing
through at 3d. per yard; the last five weeks’ work
cost probably Gd. This explains why the first
sluicing run gave a quick clean-up of 21 tons. This
ground was very clear of rocks. The tin in that
lump of easy ground was well known to many men
long before Xorthev took the show up. Elliott and
Seining, the old proprietors, did not work it because
it was below the level of their tail race. To obtain
a deeper tail race meant either buying out the
Roaring Meg Co., so that the race could run through
this co.’s lower ground, or else cutting a new race
through a very deep ridge and continuing it for a
distance of practically two miles right around the
Roaring Meg. As both these things meant big ex-
pense, Elliott and Scirving left the lump of good
ground and worked the higher ground around it.
Xorthev came along, prospected and bought the
show, cut the race through the deep ridge
and around the Roaring Meg, and then sluiced
the slice of good ground out. The “face” at
which he is working now is beginning to be rocky,
thus sending working costs up. A long way up the
ridge, right in front of the face, there outcrops a
very big rock bar. In the opinion of experienced
men this bar has got a lot to do with the tin.
A common belief is that above this bar the granite
will carry no tin. However, Northey has bored this
ground, and he says otherwise. Of course, there is
an enormous quantity of tin-bearing ground in sight
vet, but the opinion here is that the mine is by no
means the very big proposition it is cracked up to be.
So far, clean-ups of 40 and 50 tons are talked about,
but I think if they can continue to clean up 12 to
15 tons a month they will do very well.
* * * ♦
Some few months ago a Townsville syndi-
cate spent a few thousand pounds on a tin show on
Mount Windsor, Cooktown district (Queensland).
After having 16 men employed shooting away a
rock bar for a few months, and after the expensive
process of packing the tons of iron, tools, etc., up
the mountain range, the syndicate sent a manager to
take over the show and report on it. Borehole tests
said certain ground should go 21b. to 31b. to the
yard; but all the manager could get was a few
colors. There was then a sudden collapse. Wages
men are now all off, and work is stopped.
* * * *
Mount Morgan has one of its new furnaces
busy, and a second is ready. The change-over is
affecting the output. The mine is still suffering
from staff disorganisation, and it will be miraculous
if it gets the best work possible out, of the new
plant. If things don’t go really well, nobody need
be surprised (o find a big bear appear on ihe
market.
Mount Oxide Co, held a meeting in Sydney
early this week, to authorise liquidation and the
transfer of the assets to the English co. A sad tale
was told. There wouldn’t be any liquidation divi-
dend ; instead, a call of Is. 4d. per share was neces-
sary to lint things square “Show us why!” was the
natural demand. No accounts, audited or otherwise,
were produced; so the meeting was adjourned for
about a month to supply that deficiency. From all
one hears, it will be a good month’s work.
“Exile” : Three metals promise to become
of some imnortance to Australia, provided the ring
in Great Tower-street, London, E.C., allows Aus-
tralian miners to develop them. They are osmirid-
ium, nickel and molybdenite. Near the Pieman
River (Tas.) osmiridium is found in fair quantity,
and over 70 miners have been raising the ore.
Agents for London ore-buyers Used to pay £ll per
07.., but they have suddenly dropped to £7, at which
price it does not pay to produce. Osmiridium is
very rare, and as it occurs extensively in Tasmania,
that State might easily have monopolised the world’s
supply. The jobbers on the London Metal Exchange
have apparently decreed otherwise, and osmiridium
mining has been checked in Appleland. During
the past 10 months a Melbourne syndicate has been
mining nickel at North Dundas (Tas.) with encour-
aging results. In all, 2763 tons of ore have been
mined for £13,854, or £5 a ton. Out of this divs.
of £9600 have been paid. As there are only 32
shares of £lOO each in the co., the dividend for 10
months equals 300 p.c. Molybdenite occurs
in many places in almost every State. The present
price in Australia for 90 p.c. sulphide ore containing
80 p.c. of molybdenite is £5OO a ton. Below 80
p.c. the price per unit drops, especially if copper,
arsenic, bismuth and tungsten are present. The big
inmp in value is due to a demand for the metal in
France, where in a patent and secret process it is
used to prevent deterioration in cordite. Also, it is
used for hardening steel. Molybdenite chiefly occurs
in quartz veins. If handbreaking and picking can-
not be employed, it can be crushed and concentrated,
preferably by a flotation method. Prospectors should
keep their eyes open for it. So far, the brokers in
Great Tower-street, E.C., have not “crabbed” the
price, as they have of graphite, another valuable
mineral to be found all over Australia. Some years
ago the writer tried to introduce graphite from W A.
and Tas. to the ghouls of the London Metal Ex-
change, but, although the excellence of the plumbago
was admitted, no capital could be obtained to work
the deposits because the graphite ring wanted 99 p.c.
of the product for itself, and seemed inclined to
charge fees for permitting anyone to wait in the
passage and, metaphorically, crawl on hands and
knees into the “presence.”
* * * *
Dr. Herbert Basedow, the Adelaide geo-
logist, is president of the Petroleum Association,
formed in Melbourne to protect the interests of those
interested in the new oil industry, and to spread a
knowledge of petroleum mining amongst the public.
George Meudell is chairman of the council.
A certain amount of work has been done
recently on the auriferous dykes at Terawhiti, near
Wellington (M.L.), and various assay results have
been obtained Several dykes have been already
proved to exist, all of which carry gold, but the
question is what they will average if mined on a
respectable scale. L. O. Wilkinson, recently of
Stawell (Vic.), who was one of the syndicate that
had a protracted struggle with the Big Hill ore at
Stawell, has been asked to thoi’oughly sample and
test the property, and on his report future action
(or inaction) will depend. Terawhiti, it may be
mentioned, is at the extreme south of the North
Island of Maoriland, and is the spot whence the
bodies of the victims of the wrecked Union Co. s
Penguin were taken on -packhorses to the Makara-roud
and so to Wellington. It is a picturesque, wild, windy
locality, with enough mountains and wind in a few
square miles to supply several continents. The dykes
are in the hands of Wellington people, who have put
up a good deal of solid cash for the cause, in the hope
that Terawhiti may yet become a Waihi in the south.

“Mica Street, Broken Hill, 24/1/13.
“For months vast I have been pleased to notice
nurses from all parts of Australia expressing in the
press their sentiments regarding your valuably medi-
cine. I qualified for the profession forty yea s ago,
and keen'to it because I love to h°lp the sick. In
earlier life I was ASSOCIATED WITH SOME OF
ADELAIDE'S CLEVEREST SURGEONS, and nursed
their patients. Until twelve years ago I was nursing
in S. Austrlaia, but since then I have been nursing
at Broken Ilill. I have had lengthy experience and
can endorse every word other nurses have said about
Clements Tonic. In forty years I have had the care
of hundreds of poor sick people. I have been a close
observer of the effect of medicines, and years ago I
was satisfied Clements Tonic had no equal, and still
hold that opinion.
“(Signed) NURSE MENZIES.”
In order that this medicine may be ob-
tained, all Chemists and Stores sell it all
over all Australia. When travelling or
touring to any oversea parts it would be
best to include a few large bottles as a

A
WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, July 6, 1914.
My Dear Arini, —
Polo at Kensington on Saturday brought
along the usual July muster of blue noses
and fuzzy-wuzzy coats. It is a bleak spot
for spectators on a wintry day, and some of
the motor crowd huddled in their machines
most of the afternoon. Said cars sent forth
the usual frog’s chorus “honk, honk!” when
their owners’ pals were thundering to a
goal. The Warren team made its first
appearance on the Kensington arena on the
opening day of the 1914 fight for the Lady
Dudley Cup. Narromine, captained by the
large G. C. Mack —who takes an outsize in
green diamonds on his capacious white jer-
sey—and helped by the skill and dash of
the two Webbs and 11. W. Stevenson, is
still nursing the trophy. The mighty men
from Warren made a fierce helter-skelter
for goals, and smote everything—the air, the
earth, the ball —on panting, furiously excited
ponies, while Narromine was a bit too swift,
all the time.
The Muswellbrook teams bring the usual
tribe of sporting Whites and Waites to
town. Mrs. Reg White in a huge light-
brown coat, twice the size of a small hearth-
rug, was inside the vice-regal enclosure the
other afternoon. This consists of a couple
of seats and the same amount of damp sod
under foot as the ordinary liege stamps
under his soles. The Strickland ladyship
was comfortably camped in her usual two-
horse chaise, with his Ex. hovering round
the family perch. The small Henrietta, like
her eldest sister, Treasure Mary, rode to the
fray, and hopped off a steady lookiug pony
with shaggy whiskers, in the cutest little
baby habit. Aide Wallis was in attendance,
with melancholy mien, heavily draped in a
great coat. Hon. Aide d’Apice was at the
stirrup to jump the vice-regal damsels on
and off their gee-gees. The corn-colored
locks of the eldest Miss Finn —turned up in
neat little rolls under a winter hat —were in
the same sub-royal patch of very wet grass.
On the lawn —I suppose one must call the
strip of grass between the unreserved seats
at the rail and the bare planks on which
Society books its polo roost, a lawn, for it
would be unkind to call it a bog—Mrs. Colin
Stephen’s fur coat was one of the sartorial
events, in the polar way, of Saturday’s
peltry. It was brown and striped, and
large, and voluminous, and warm enough to
make a sleeping bag for Penguin Villa at
the South Pole. Mrs. Tom Watson was
handsomely rugged in d curly black fur coat
with a marcel wave all over it. The tall
Rossitter maiden, in emerald green ; the
Norman Burdekins; the Frank Brennans;
Miss Keating, in a fine pepper-and-salt
tailor-made, warmed up with black fox ; and
pretty Marjory Smythe, Katers, Mrs. Alf
Turnbull, who is It in frocking; a lady with
a large velvet helmet or hat of the Boadicea
pattern, and stacks of Mutton lords and
ladies, shivered together at the opening polo
whack.
Miss Brookman, a good-looking English
girl now staying with the Macneill Simp-
sons, was one of the smartest black-garbed
damsels at Saturday’s polo. The coat and
skirt of the moment, when it is first un-
packed hereabouts, is a more extensive
arrangement than we dreamt of a few years
ago. The skirt has most of its trimming
behind, in flat tiers that look as if they’d
been gummed down. The coat is not
flounced, but it has a gathered appearance
at the back, and a little terrace of cloth
to top it off. The English damsel, an attract-
ive blonde, wore a tailored garment an-
swering to the above description, and it
knocked out anything of the kind that got
alongside it. The rival garment also ran,
but it hadn’t a show.
The Civil Ambulance Ball, which is one
of the tony affairs of this polo week, comes
off on Thursday, July 9. The Salon de Luxe
is the place. The Civil Ambulance Horse,
who is in reduced circumstances, is looking
for 5 per cent, of oats out of the profits of
this dance. Tickets are one guinea each.
For this sum thirsty souls can fill themselves
to the collar-stud with champagne, under the
supervision of a waiter.
The Westralian Newspaper magnate.
Wintlirop Haekett, shook the hand of George
Rex in the ancient premises of St. James’s
Palace. This levee occurred towards the
end of May. His small Majesty seems to
none the worse for this rush at his gloved
paw.
The Australian Field Artillery, a vigorous
limb of our militia gunnery department,
under Lieut.-Col. Bob Lenehan, is going to
have a party in Sargents’ rooms on July 17.
Dancing will take place on or about 8 p.m.
The A.F.A. officers’ mess secretary, N.
Alcorn Mclvell, is counting the acceptances
with a view to placing the right number of
knives and forks in position, and getting a
sufficiency of cruets. I believe that empires
have fallen, and brave men have died, and
cities have, been desolated, by reason of a
trouble which started over insufficient cruets.
Have you noticed that Mr. David Fell,
now in Britain, is still pursued by the title
“ex-M.L.A. for Lane Cove?” Every men-
tion of the Sydney man in the social news
of the day (in papers devoted to the globe-
trotting Australian), is followed by this
dubious glory—ex-M.L.A. Fancy being
described as an ex-journalist, or an ex-
draper ! And think of the man who was
mayor for one year, and was so overwhelm-
ingly popular that he was unanimously
elected ex-mayor next year!
The Lucas-Teeth are again in full swing
in their palatial humpy in London. Their
recent hop was bedizened with the diamonds
of wealthy merchants’ wives, and the
knightly orders of an assemblage that was
something Solid in the City.
The Friths, who have one of the most pic-
turesque homesteads in the Toowoomba
(Q.) district, have been motoring through
the South of England. They were booked
for the Thames Village in time to see the
Quality take its daily June airing in Rotten
Row. Speaking of the picturesque home-
stead, it may be explained that it is really
leafy. The Friths are of the tree-planting
species, as against the tree-hacking or alder-
manic or forest-demolishing species. The
arborasre anu shady avenue near their resi-
dence would drive the axe-brigade into a
frenzy of rage and enthusiasm. John Oli-
ver Frith, who has his wife and three sons
with him on the other, side, is likely to per-
sonally deal with any misguided person who
plays round his timber with a tomahawk
during his present jaunt in England.
At last the Moonlight Sonata ! After long
watching, after hearing it banged, beaten
and furiously flung on the piano, by all sizes
and shapes of visiting stars, we heard the
famous Beethoven Sonata in C sharp minor,
played by Harold Bauer. He is about the
first pianist—except Paderewski —who has
conveyed to a Sydney audience, during re-
cent years, an impression of something soft,
subtle, alluring—in fact, moonlight and not
a spring cleaning or a team of bullocks
rattling over stones —in the first movements
of the sonata, which has been done to death
by athletic persons with large and heavy
hands. Bauer’s season here is prolonged.
On Thursday evening he is to blossom in the
Concordia Hall, and at a Saturday matinee
(July 11) you can hear him bear the fruit
of melody and the foliage of harmony at our
town barn.
The British Association for the Advance-
ment of Science (isn’t this a long tail to
carry round the world —almost as long as
the British Israel Identification Associa-
tion?) is nearing this continent in three
battalions for the August conference. The
advance guard hopes to climb down the
Aseanius’ gangway at Fremantle on July
28. I think the learned Gen. Sec. Herdman,
D.Sc. (take science as read), F.R.S., is in
charge of the 60 wiseacres on the Blue
Funnel craft. The second batch will be
nicely browned when they clamber on to
Australian soil at Albany on August 5. and
the last turn-over of Science ought to be
putting on its spectacles to look at Adelaide
on August 8. There, they have a short
session of Concentrated Brain Food (for
Heaven’s sake don’t call it Fag!), and
improve the shining hour and the S. Aus-
tralian thirst for Information till about
the 12th. After that Science seizes its
umbrella and bustles off to repeat its efforts
for the benefit of Melbourne.
Sydney will fall over the Learned Inva-
sion from Britain on August 20, and fill it
up with food and listen to its orations for
about a week. In the meantime the N.S.W.
hon. treasurer, 11. G. Chapman, M.D., is
anxious to enrol as many new members at
the Royal Society’s House as he can draw
into the State Associate’s net. I under-
stand that it is quite necessary to know a
grasshopper by its Latin name or a black
beetle as something more definite than “nasty
little beast” in order to qualify for Associa-
tion membership. When you can step on a
cockroach in three languages you are really
only beginning to be eligible. Ladies who
desire to air their spring millinery at the
Science Congress can do so (to the secret
rage of other ladies who are more back-
ward. and only flee from a mouse in one
language) as members at the cost of a single
guinea. This gives them a nice little extra
dash to the front, and a perfect right to
stand up suddenly and object to the way
Sir Oliver Lodge parts his back hair.
Another of Australia’s brilliant sons,
Grafton Smith, M.D., M.A.. F.R.S., now
Professor of Anatomy at Manchester Uni.
(Eng.), came on ahead of the other scientific
Blue Socks. He can tell you the name of
every bone that ought to be in the dusty
body of Rameses the Great and Hophra and
Nimrod and such-like late lamenteds. Or
he could, if hard pressed, put his learned
forefinger on the exact place where an
Egyptian lady felt her corns long before
Israel came to the Nile country to buy
provender—in the days when it was un-
usually wet at Memphis, and you couldn’t
go out in the unbuilt city of Alexandria
without your cab being bogged.
Dr. Thacker, a Christchurch (M.L.)
enthusiast in the muddied football field (he
looks on at the big fights), has been over
here to see the recent Australian walloping
on Sydney Cricket Ground. He went back
to the Dominion with the more or less
maimed Britishers by Saturday’s boat, which
thoughtfully hung on to its hawser till the
game was over. The sporting Christchurch
medico is not a “retired gentleman” what-
ever that may be, but he’s sufficiently well
endowed with this world’s cash to be able
to take his ear away from his surgery ’phone
and follow up football to its last kick. The
British bumps and bruises will, therefore,
have friendly first aid always in sight, during
the forthcoming argument in M.L.
Soprano Lillian Mitchell, a pupil of
Andrew Black, will shortly move off this
continent for Europe, to continue the art
of vocal acrobatics in foreign tongues. A
farewell concert, at which the youthful
Lillian will sing her swan song, occurs on
July 6 in the Town Hall. Therefore it
eventuates before this reaches you, my
Arini.
Adelaide’s July contingent for Kosciusko’s
cold feet and frosty old skull include Dr.
Violet Plummer and her sister. W. G. T.
Goodman, general manager of the Holy City’s
Tramway Trust, is another S. Australian
visitor who is getting his hot-water bottle
packed up for the ski-carnival. The Ade-
laide Tramway Trust, by the way, is
a mild, non-octopus form of the combine
system. It is vested in trustees —I think
municipal. When the business was bought
from a private company, it was paid
for by a very notable cheque—about
£17,347.921,101,697 4s. 3d. Our financial
expert will know whether I’ve handed over
this trifle without inquiring for an extra
farthing or two that may lurk in the back-
ground. Dr. Shorney, from the same grape-
growing State, is another Kosciusko sport
who is putting away his stethoscope for a
July ski-run.
Captain Head, an English globe-trotter
who comes to this continent fairly often,
after periodic attacks on Maoriland’s frigid
Alns, is going to spend a few weeks
at the Kosciusko eyrie. He will put his
best foot foremost in this month’s snow
balling up there.
Solicitor Vindin, of this now rainy ham-
let, is going up aloft very soon—l mean to
Kosciusko, which is 7000-odd feet above
golosh level. Dr. Herbert Schlink, a Sydney
medico who takes snow sports seriously each
winter, is bound for the same hard freeze.
He likes to climb to the mountain top—so
that he can look over the other side, I sup-
pose. There are two sides to every case,
and also to every mountain. Dr. Oscar
Paul, another ski-enthusiast, is generally
panting for the same exertion, and the pair
go off together and admire the general re-
semblance of the scenery to a gigantic wad
of white cotton wool. Arthur Rickard and
his wife are going to try their luck in snow
boots on the thin end of the joyous aval-
anche about July 18.
Portia Geach’s exhibition of oils, water-
colors and etchings is still hanging against
the walls of Anthony Horderns Fine Art
Gallery. Most of the work is either impres-
sionist or mildly decorative. The Australian
artist has had some of her technique laid
on at the Royal Academy, but she has been
caught in the willy willy of the Paris mod-
ernist desire to throw paint in chunks on a
handy bit of canvas. Artist Portia is a
better draughtsman than colorist. Her two
most ambitious efforts, “The Procession of
the Hours,” a design for a mural decoration,
and a wall decoration design, “Mask
of Cupid,” after Spenser’s Faerie Queene,
show a vital sense of life and move-
ment in the figures. The Lion (in the path)
in the latter, however, is violently excluded
from the foregoing remark. He is a mis-
erable, anaemic-looking beast with the smile
of a beauty actor. Quite the best bit of
painting at the show is the fine study of a
Kaffir girl. Amongst the worst is “Manly
Carnival” (blobs of yellow, figures and other
things) ; “Full Moon” (more streaks of in-
flammatory orange, ferry-boats and water) ;
“Autumn” (something very hard and yellow
has hit a defenceless tree or two) ; and “Sea
Wall.” Two stout ladies are sitting on the
wall, whicli some person unknown appears
to be holding up on the other side.
The hauteur with which the frequent
official treats the humble but useful press
came to an unexnected climax in Suva a
few weeks ago. “Local society,” writes a
visitor to that sunbaked village, “is still
surprised and slightly hurt at the ‘uppish-
ness’ of mere pressmen. As you will remem-
ber, Governor Escott’s speech at a Council
of Chiefs was ignored by the, for once,
mutinous press. It was an amazing in-
stance of the Turning Worm.” The “high
official” who told the reporters that if there
happened to be any room left over when the
distinguished visitors were seated, they could
have it, has had his hair officially pulled over
the matter. The usually meek press put
away its writing pads and walked off. The
thrilling result —a Governor’s speech not
mentioned next day—has made the Govern-
ment House circle feel as if the Last Trump
might be omitted on the Day of Reckoning.
Mrs. Cecil Nevill Smith farewelled the
handsome Polish journalist, Miss Czay-
kowska, and Sydney’s Mrs. Yetta Rosen-
bloom, at the Australasian Pioneers’ Club
the other afternoon. These projected depar-
tures, as they say in the classics, are mov-
ing off this coast for Java on July 18 in the
Dutch boat. Houtman—named after some-
body who hasn’t lately been seen around.
From Java they’ll wander in a circuitous
track to the golden-haired Czaykowska’s
native Poland. In that distressful land of
defunct revolutions and long-haired musicians
you can hire domestic help at 2s. Gd. a week.
I don’t know whether the help “finds itself”
on this princely wage (some princes haven’t
been able, bv the way, to earn twopence a
week when they’re out of a billet), but you
get 20s. worth of chores for half a crown.
(N.B.—This is all right if you don’t happen
to do the chores.)
“Bunty Pulls the Strings” at the Royal
to enormous business. The crinoline area
is cleverly managed by Ella (“Bunty”)
Young, who sweetly bullies her family from
the commanding altitude of sft. nothing or
so. I notice that on her washing day she
is without that wired cage, and tucks up
her skirts with pins, and her hair into a
black velvet net, with a ferocious mesh, and
gets the family (father and brother) up to
breakfast, in quite a modern silhouette.
What a wonderful presence the crinoline
gives to even little Bunty! When Mrs.
Graham Moffatt. a fine figure of a woman at
any time, gets inside the 1860 fortification,
as the elderly Miss Dunlop, she moves across
the stage like a page in historv — extra large.
The kirk yard, with Elder Weelum Sprunt
temporarily at the collection-plate, while the
younger portion of the congregation sprint
over the stile —a small girl in a pork pie
hat. crinoline and other lingerie, is a chapter
of 1860 revelations —is worth going to see,
even if you have to splash through the wet
streets on shanks’ goloshes.
Leila Doubleday, the young Victorian
violinist who has been away for about eight
years, returns shortly to her native gum-
tree. She will tour hereabouts to show
us what she has been learning all this time
on the other side.
An Australian in the U.S. writes that
the hotel lift in the country where George
Washington didn’t tell a lie can send you
heavenwards at the speed of 650 ft. per
second. It not only can, but it docs, and it
fires you out at your 42nd floor, or whatever
it is, with the feeling (hat you are merely
a number, with no occupation and precious
little use for your feet. An Australian lift
of the wild and raging kind occasionally
bumps us upwards at the rate of about
250 ft. per moment. But it hasn’t so far to
travel as the Sheeargo streak of lift light-
ning, so the traveller arrives intact.
(N.B. —I forget whether the pace is really
per moment, pr per minute or per hour, but
that is a mere detail.)
An American millionaire bachelor (dear
creature!) has just been interviewed in
London re the advantages of being a bach-
lor. “We bachelors,” says his Dollars, “re-
pay handsomely the hospitality which is
showered on us. We are the salt of the
social earth.” This spread-eagle sentiment
has a grain or two of truth in its fiction.
The bachelor is a sought-after appendage to
any evening party, and knows his
worth. lie wanders from debutante bud
to Society matron, and steps across a ball-
room with a profound sense of his own im-
portance that is a perky challenge to the
Merely Married.
The small black Bunny toque is a mild
obsession for a week or two. It fits close
to the head, and shoots out two long rabbit’s
ears, in silk, of the same raven hue. As
queer millinery it; is only rivalled by the
same kind of pudding-basin shape which has
a long, thin dish-mop rising about 2ft. above
hair level. This sky-scraping trimming can
be made of anything that comes handy. Take
one feather duster, pluck off most of its
fluff, and gum on to its cane body a few
spangles, or a bit of red or yellow leather.
Fix firmly to the front of a velvet skull-
cap. and you get quite six guineas’ worth
of the latest style. Some day Woman will
bury her head in a large saucepan, wearing
the handle well up behind, and put half a
yard of fried bacon round the crown.
Everyone will consider it frightfully smart
—what!
The Nurses’ Ball, to provide cash towards
a building fund for the Nightingales’ resi-
dential club, comes off on August 26. The
Salon de Luxe will be the place. Mrs.
McMurray is president of a ball committee,
which means to scour our Best Suburbs for
fancy sets and other sartorial snares for the
occasion.
Yours affectionately,
VANDORIAN.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Alelbourxe, July 6, 1914.
Aly Dear Yahlie. —
The cold, grey dawn of Sunday last dis-
covered a litter of toy American flags and
cracker remnants strewn over a large area
of Spring-street. On Saturday (which, of
course, was the Glorious FourtH) the newly-
formed Australian-American Club had had
a supper party at the Grand, and, between
band selections and toasts, a good deal had
been said about “the golden bonds between
two continents” —and much more to the same
effect. U.S. Consul Alagelssen was a guest
of honor at the celebration. The Club is
intended for Americans resident in Alelbourne
and all good Australians who have made the
pilgrimage to Alecea —I mean to the Beef
Trust. The outsider who has merely bought
a peanut is not eligible for membership.
The first day of the V.R.C. Grand National
Aleeting, at Flemington on Saturday, was
watched by a heavily-furred crowd. The
few daring ones who tried to exhibit new
tailorings without the encumbering skins
suffered keenly from the Flemington wind,
and I suppose the doctor’s motor has been
waiting at a few front doors ever since.
The G.-G. looked as if the bleak weather
had chilled his slight official enthusiasm for
the sport of viceroys. He was well muffled
in a big heather-and-haggis overcoat, but
still did not look delighted. Her Ex. sup-
ported the little lace hat that is nearest rela-
tion to the bonnet. The black cloth tailor-
ings that went with the hatbon were out-
lined with white pipings. Little Doris
Blackwood looked saucy in a Napoleonic tile
without the tricolor. Airs, Leslie Stuart,
with a headgear of great price showing above
her furs, hung over the Dust Cloud animal
in the birdcage murmuring sweet nothings.
(Please understand that Mrs. Stuart was
uttering the sweet nothings; the horse had
nothing to say.) The burden of Airs.
Stuart’s song was “We’ve never got
within coo-ee of a Grand National winning-
post before.” But her sister-in-law, in
funereal black cloth, retorted “He might
have run a little nearer to-day, anyhow!”
Which seemed reasonable, seeing that Dust
Cloud had merely ambled home fourth.
Frank Aladden’s girl was facially obscured
with ’possum furs, but showed silk grey
ankles and shoes to the world. Airs. Bindley
personally conducted a Pinschof sister, who
-tarred in a black and white pierrette hat.
The sheepish legion (for names tear out the
Western District section of the directory)
were nresent to a woman in tailor-mades with
slit skirts.
The ice rink was rushed during the last
cold snap. Airs. Simon Fraser, jun. (she used
to be “Billy” Clegg), came down from her
husband’s sheep station on the Alurray just
to have another turn at her beloved pastime.
Airs. Charlie Griffiths, back from skating in
foreign places, showed some special figures;
but she couldn’t cut as much ice as the
most expert Alelbourne girls. Airs. Ambrose
Pratt, who slithers gracefully, showed off a
dainty turban that defied criticism and care-
fully refrained from falling on it.
The Glaciarium is thrilled with emotion of
various brands these chilly afternoons. A
Russian prince is occasionally seen on the
premises, and his footwork is described on
.all sides as “simply lovely.” He has a
titled aide in constant attendance, and the
way people stare at the pair of them is re-
markable. The two are bachelors, and the
skating girls are putting their best limbs
forward —though not necessarily for inspec-
tion. Several more or less beautiful maidens
have already fallen at or near the princely
feet —but it was the ice every time, not
mere sentiment. You couldn’t tell the visi-
tors from ordinary well-dressed citizens by
their clothes, which are well-brushed and
have the creases in the usual places. The
prince tried to smoke a large black cigar the
first time he appeared; but an attendant,
who may have taken it for a bomb, de-
manded the thing just when he had got it
fairly reeking, and bore it away with an ex-
planatory wave of the hand at the “No
Smoking” notice. You will see by this how
little it takes to thrill the poor females who
pay for the privilege of cutting grooves in the
floor of the frosty place.
An important military and civil dance is
trumpeted for Tuesday, July 14, in the Col-
ilingwood Town Hall. The warriors of the
Sixty-Third are determined to make it a suc-
cess, and who shall gainsay them?
The quaint old war-kettle Cerberus is at
present at Williamstown, and the officers are
scattering invitations for a dance on Friday.
.July 31. The floor of the Naval Depot
gymnasium is to be waxed for the occasion,
so the lid of the kettle will not creak under
the dancers’ flying feet.
A cable tells of a launch spree on the
Thames, and the drowning of Baronet Denis
Anson in the course of the vinous pranks.
Young Anson was in the very early twenties
and had held the title left by his uncle only
for two or three months. His claim to in-
terest people at this end lay in the fact that
Ihe was the only son of F. A. Anson, who
squatted at Peraki, near Akaroa (M.L.), in
the ’SO’s. F. A. Anson was an irascible wool-
gatherer who gave his very candid opinion to
all and sundry on each of the occa-
sions when the stork presented him with a
fine daughter, and left the longed-for heir
till last.
The other Saturday afternoon the Lachlan
Mackinnon matron invited all the Best
babies, including the Frederick Blackwood
pair, to play in her distinguished garden
with the illustrious Mackinnon youngsters.
A clown and a conjurer kept them from tear-
ing up the place by the roots and throwing it
over the fence, when they were not deeply
absorbed in the simple games of extreme
youth.
Airs. Charles Alills entertained the local
nobility at the Alexandra (Gas Co.) Club
on Tuesday afternoon, to farewell daughter
Jean Price, who sailed by the Ulysses, four
days later, to join her ex-sailor hub in East
Africa, which, so I am told, is a rather un-
healthy place. (Wait till I get my breath!
The doctor says I’ll die of one of these long
sentences some day. He said to me only
yesterday. “Avoid running up stairs and
long sentences, or you won’t live to pay my
bill I” J It was a very fine affair, decorated
with pink roses and cheered by a string band
playing light music, but vaguely overshad-
owed by the wirelessed scorn and loathing of
lurking club members who hadn’t been in-
vited, or had been overlooked, or were sus-
pected of being dead and buried. The
hostess, who looked almost vice-regal in black
taffetas, and might have been mourning for
the members who hadn’t been asked, held on
tightly to a bundle of violets. She had to
hold on to something, for the outside clubites
were distinctly depressing. I don’t quite know
how these things are arranged; but if any
man-club tried to work on the same extraor-
dinary system it would quickly burst. In
fact, I am doubtful if a club of gibbering
apes could endure the strain.
The theatrical carnival in aid of the hos-
pitals, which duly raged on uie Alelbourne
Cricket Ground, made the expected heap of
money. Amusement-seekers had to pay
their half-crown entrance fee outside the
high, bricked amphitheatre before they knew
wnat they were letting themselves in for.
The stands were certainly packed—but
mostly with discontented faces. A bleak
wind took the edge off enthusiasm and anti-
cipation—and there had to be a great deal
of anticipation on account of the scarcity of
events. Donkey races happened at long
intervals; but'the intervals were long and
a donkey race only goes a short way. Pre-
sently hope died of cold feet and interest
expired of malnutrition. Below the stands
a rabble of theatricals posed for all they
were worth. Part of the dreary afternoon
was made worse by an American voice .pro-
claiming uselessly, “Niblo will sell sweets
at two o’clock!” or “Niblo will auction a
hug at two-thirty!” And the only things
the wretched listeners wanted were hot pota-
toes and footwarmers. Grace Palotta auc-
tioned little bunches of flowers, while Essie
Perrin-Kerouse and sister-in-law May
Ivcrouse held up the stream of expectant
arrivals with appeal boxes at the turnstiles.
The only real enthusiasm seemed to be round
the doors of the underground tea rooms. The
crowd round the outer doors looked like a
bargain morning crush. Airs. Henry Corn-
ish and a large batch of Innes-Noad girls
put: up an excellent scrimmage on the out-
skirts and triumphantly disappeared into
the steamy interior. Jack Cannot wore his
bride on his arm and trotted round selling
acid drops. But even that didn’t save the
situation from an amusement point of view.
He couldn’t have done it with a dray of acid
drops.
Mary Grant Bruce, who reached literary
eminence after putting in time on tire Inky
Way here, was married last week to a name-
sake, Alajah George Bruce, whom she met
in Ireland. The lady said “I will” in a
travelling dress and grey furs, and the
united Bruces then left for a honeymoon
tour of the Cabbage Garden. A couple of
days before the wedding the bride was given
a luncheon by Melbourne women-writers,
who proved that feminine journalism is not
necessarily a last refuge for domestic in-
capables, by cooking an elaborate meal for
themselves. The bride announced that her
present to the bridegroom was to be a mop,
which she expects him to use, so that her
flow of ink shall not be interfered with.
The bridegroom looks very young to be a
major; but I suppose some catch it earlier
than others. The pair dispensed with as
much of the usual fuss as they dared,
though a crowd of relatives and friends
filled the little church while the show was
on. Airs. Bruce (sister-in-law) and Airs.
Alex. Pearson, of Berwick, hovered round,
likewise Airs. Staughton and the Bland Holts.
Addie Hine, the young Australian dancer
who did so well abroad, has renounced the
boards in favor of the domestic hearth—or
Possibly it is a radiator in a fiat. She has
been the wife of a rich young man for some
considerable time now. and has brought her
husband out to visit Australia. Their chmf-
est treasure is a very new baby who has Ids
(her?) own suite of attendants. Addie had
her twenty-first birthday supper party in
London not so long ago. The Melbourne
Br.ockhoffs, who were amongst those present,
say that it was a gallant spread.
Fan Dango-Mackay is going to England
with her husband to see how well Elsie is
doing on the London boards.
Mrs. Ambrose Pratt lately gave a fare-
well party to the Freddie Shipman lady, at
the Oriental Hotel. Mrs. Roger Thompson
and Mrs. Croker and her daughters brought
a lovely dress apiece to the party. There
were two or three steps leading down to the
reception-room, and the theatricals who at-
tended made the most of them. Some_ of the
impromptu “entrances” were really impres-
sive. Fan Dango-Mackay, muffled in splen-
did furs, tripped down with a little dancer's
run. Mrs. Beatrice Holloway-Greig descended
demurely in black cloth and smart white
spats. Mrs. Plumer gave one a fleeting im-
pression of sparkling black eyes, before the
chattering crowd obscured her. Mrs. Bland
Holt advertised her perennial youthfulness
by wearing a lovely rose-pink silk cloak, a
black picture-hat, and gold buckles on high-
heeled shoes. Mrs. Freddie said “How-
d’ye-do” and “Good-bye” under blue hat-
feathers that matched . her eyes, and the
hostess was all there in black and white
garments and a sweetly appropriate hat.
About four years ago a Hobart girl named
Lucie Atkins went away to England with
Melba to study singing. Hobart found £3T>O
by concert and subscription, and Melba did the
rest in her usual generous fashion. Now Miss
Atkins has returned as Mrs. Frank Purchas,
and she and her husband have announced
two Hobart concerts on Butt-Rumford lines.
The husband, who is an Australian and a
Melba nephew, has a fine stage presence
and is reported to have an exceptional voice.
There is a small Purchas daughter in the
background; she is four months old and. I
believe, can take A in alt, or words to that
effect, without any trouble. The Mayoress
of Hobart organised a big reception for the
returned Lucie, and also arranged a special
welcome-home morning tea in conjunction
with the original benefit committee.
Randolph Bedford’s daughter Vera sang
some of Wagner’s “Ring” music in a rich
contralto voice, at the Conservatorium con-
cert’ of last, week; and one of her own com-
positions (a setting of some Victor Daley
verses) was carolled by Elsa Waxmau.
Hugh Paterson’s clever daughter Betty also
made a triumphal dfflmt as a singer. Betty
can toss up as to whether she backs her
violin or her voice as a means to the de-
sired career. She has a touch of genius both
ways, and, moreover, is a healthy youngster
with a commendable liking for hard work.
Pretty Ivy Philips (she gets quite angry
if you put two l’s in her name), daughter of
J. C. Philips, of Adelaide, has come over
here for a long half-holiday, so to speak.
Incidentally she will give Melbourne an
opportunity to sample a contralto at a series
of Auditorium concerts. The first song-burst
is timed for the 14th.
Young Louis Foulet, who is frankly
French, and announces himself on the invita-
tion card as “Monsieur” —is exhibiting his
paintings at the Besant Hall, in the Collins-
street Centreway. The show was due to
open on Wednesday, and if it confirms the
allegations of Rumor it will be at least as
interesting as some of the shows that come
and go like moving pictures in a row at the
Athemeum Gallery.
From “Rosna,” in London: —
T. Brailsford Robertson, who is a D.Sc. of Ade-
laide University, and is married to Jennie, daughter
of Prof. Stirling, of the same Slowly City, has had
a kiddie’s book published. “The Universe and the
Mayonnaise” is an attractive-looking salad of scien-
tific facts with a dressing of infant fiction. Dr.
Robertson, who has reached the advanced age of
30, is Professor of Physiology in the University of
California, and he has already committed a number
of monographs on various aspects of biology, as well
as a learned volume on “The Phvsical Chemistry of
the Proteins,” or something dreadful like that.
Adelaide for culture again!
Alice Bryant, the Sydney-side organist, is flatting
in London with her artist brother, Charles, who
had a successful show last week. The talented girl
has pounded the cumbrous instrument at the Albert
Hall and Queen’s Hall Sunday concerts, besides giv
ing recitals here and there.
From my Adelaide correspondent:—
The open season for charity meetings has begun.
This grand old British sport was seen at its lies
this week in connection both with the Queen's
Home and the Victoria Leag'ue. Lady Galway, who
knows the game from A to Z, rode a fine race in the
former event, and her wing three-quarter play in
the Victoria League affair was much admired. Her
speeches were commendably short —a great point of
merit in these contests. Both she and the Governor
were seen in action on Saturday when, with the skill
of apparent veterans, they turned the first key in
the door of the new wing at Minda. The gate-
money must have amounted to a goodly sum.
Amongst other sporting characters present I noted
the Bishoo Wilsons, the J. C. Vercos, the Charles
Goodes, Mr. Herbert Phillipps, Miss Gladys Thomas
and the C. T. Hargraves. A musical “At Home” is
threatened for Wednesday next in the Walkerville
Hall, the proceeds of which are to go to Minda.
Take it for all in all, the place may be said to be in
luck.
Tattersall’s Races occurred on Monday—a happen-
ing which was inevitable, barring the arrival e
earthquake, invasion, the Act of God, or weatbe
sufficiently rotten to disgust a young and health.'
duck. Nothing much happened—that is to say, no
one stiffened a horse or outed a jockey, or threw a
brick at the Governor. Did you ever notice, my
dear, that there is some sort of mysterious relation
between dullness and a life of virtue which extends
even to communities?
Regardless of the Sydney small-pox scare, the fol-
lowing left us this week for the polluted area: The
Edward Hawkers, Mrs. Backhouse and Mrs. Belt, the
Arthur Rymills and Miss McMillan, and Mrs. Hillam
and Miss Barbara South. In just such a dauntless
spirit did St. Charles Borromeo go forth among the
afflicted inhabitants of Milan three, centuries ago.
Where our townsfolk have a slight bulge on St.
Charles (who, if you remember, fell a victim to his
courageous philanthropy) is in the fact that they are
all vaccinated. If any of them dies and is canon-
ised, he or she will probably be the first accredited
saint with vaccination' marks, to enter Paradise.
Which reminds me that Peter Dawson and his con
cert co. have also left us. They took with them
Miss Hilda Felstead and Miss Carlien Jurs, both of
whom are booked for the co.’s nine weeks’ tour of
the Eastern States.
A cable from England announces the death of
Mrs. F. C. Driffield, whose home was in South Aus-
tralia for most of her life. She was born in ’3l,
and came here with her father, the late Joseph
McMinn, as a child. She leaves one daughter, who
is in England, and five sons.
The will of the late Dr. Glynn has been lodged for
probate, and the bulk of the £27,000 which he left
goes to relatives, except for about £lOOO worth of
legacies to Roman Catholic institutions.
Miss Bessie Davidson left by the French mailboat
on Tuesday on a two or three months’ visit to her
people here. For the last four years she has been
in Paris studying. She is going back, via India,
in the spring.
Paul Dufault and co. were given morning tea at
the Queen Adelaide Club by Mrs. Brewster Jones
QU Thursday. Most of the guests were members of
the council” of the Adelaide Chamber Music Society,
Chief Justice Way, who has been more or less an
invalid for some time, left by Thursday’s express
for Sydney. A wire mentions that he had his arm
utated by Dl . McCormick on Monday, at the
latter’s private hospital. When last heard of he
was doing as well as could be expected,
Mr. J. W. Jones, secretary to the Commissioner
of Public Works, who has just retired under the
Septuagenarians Act, joined the Civil Service in the
g urve y Department in ’65. He is a tremendous
authority on Australian aborigines and their cus-
toms and dialects—especially the tribes of his own
State. , .. „ ~, ~
Mrs. Jack Lee and her brother, Mr. I rank Moulden,
i e ft by Friday’s express for Kosciusko. Dr. and
Mrs Burston 'and Miss Pankhurst were passengers
by the same train.
The sudden death of Michael Monaghan, Crimean
veteran in Adelaide, at 80, recalls a characteristic
trifle in .the local career of Sir Day Bosanquet, recent
South Aus. Governor. In 1856 Queen Victoria had given
tbg young soldier a s iik handkerchief of her own
embroidering for his gallantry before Sebastopol, and
when he came out to Adelaide, soon after, Govern-
ment House borrowed it to show some guests—and
lost it! For 50 years he went lamenting its loss.
taSdSTS? £ ££s “
b]m ano ther, which was formally presented to the
old hoy—just in time, for a fortnight later he died,
A simple act of kindness, but it took half a century
for anybody to think of it. When you mourn 50
years for a lost handkerchief there isn t much of
* our life left to you.

“.Esculapius”: In ancient Greece they used to
erect temples over natural springs and give to each
a titular diety. We moderns are no less appreciative
of the healthful virtues of a mineral water—only our
methods differ somewhat. For instance, up at the
famous; Helidon Spring, the water is secured just as
it bubbles up from the earth, and hermetically sealed
until the process of aeration and bottling is com-
plete. You don’t need a Greek fineness of palate to
appreciate the cool charm of Helidon Spa—it has
the same delicious refreshingness to Billjim as to the
Earl of Hopetoun. As a blender with wines and
spirits, too, it gives just that added snap and
sparkle that counts so much in the sum of pleasant-
ness. You’ll be wise to always make it a practice to
“Figaro”: Read a par. here the other week about
Nature planting the giant stinging nettle in NQ
but close beside it the tree whose leaves serve as an
antidote to its sting. This provision of Nature is
in no way unique, for, just as she curses us with
indigestion so she blesses us with Helidon Spa
After dining out you’ll have no next morning head-
ache if you take a bottle of “Helidon” before turning
in—this delicious mineral water is Nature’s gift to
those who have dined not wisely but too well. A single
glass will dissolve the undigested food and at the
same time ensure a perfect night’s rest. Quite apart
from its health aspect, “Helidon” greatly enhances
the flavor of wines and spirits—so truly the way of
wisdom is always to
SPLIT IT WITH

ABORIGIN-
ALITIES
“Moana”: One thing that Maoriland has
imported that promises to do well is the chamois.
A tew from Switzerland were liberated a year or
two ago on Mount Cook, and the other day a further
pair, a present from the Emperor of Austria, was
let loose in the same region. During the past season
these active little animals were seen fairly frequently
in the vicinity of the big hill by different parties,
and a couple of guides had a close view of a herd
of 15. The chamois invariably sticks to high, snow-
infested country, so it can’t do any harm,, which
is more than can be said for its occasionally in-
furiated relative, the red deer. This latter beast
hangs out in the more or less settled districts of the
two islands, and eats houses, fences, crops and sun-
dries.
WHEN GREEK MEETS GREEK.
