“G.V.W.’s” news from the front: I forget
how many bullets it takes to account tor a soldier in
a present-day scrap, but a chimney-stack at the
Orepuki Shale Works (M.L.) took 200 before it
gnawed the dust. Awhile ago, a streak of lightning-
collided with it, tore a portion off and left the rest
in a tottery condition. People living in the vicinity
felt uncomfortable, until a man with a .303 rifle
stalked it and started plugging away at the base.
After 200 shots he brought it down with a
crash like a month’s coal rattling down the shoot
into the bunkers of Sheol.
Lagani-Namo”: Three hundred or so
glass 11 piopperl up on sticks in the sea some 60
no es south of Port Moresby (Papuan), constitute the
ullage of Huta. The folk are noted from one
end of thus Territory to the other for their swim-
ming- The children learn to do it before they are
taught to walk, and spend most of their time in the
water. Instead of fighting with waddies on shore,
in the event of a difference of opinion, the Huta
fashion is for the disputants to come to a decision
in the water. I happened along one Sunday morn-
ing years ago in the midst of some wild excitement.
The whole village seemed to have assembled on its
rickety outrigger canoes to watch two vouths
lattling in the waves. The principals had a clear
space of about 20ft. to manoeuvre in. They did this
>.v leg action, holding the hands free for a holt.
Suddenly one flung himself at the other, and thev
went under. We could see them writhing together
m 'I 6 " 4 ' ° ne minute P9 ss ed, and just when I
thought they were down to stay one fellow drove
his knee into the other’s diaphragm. There was a
gush of bubbles, and with a final “Take that! and
that, and that!” the victor shot gasping to the
surface. Then relatives dived and brought the
other party airwards. Blood trickled from his nose
and ears, and his eyes had the gone-out look of a
defunct fish. Women took charge of operations
then, holding him topside down, applying- hot stones
to his feet, and massaging him energetically with
warmed coeoanut oil. The crowd went about its
business, and ignored this phase of the perform-
ance. After watching the unrewarded efforts of the
dusky masseuses for 15min. or so, 1 ventured the
opinion that the boy’s cake was dough, but was re-
buked with: “Huta people he savvy this fashion
belong water too much ; by and bye this-feller-boy
he orright.” And I learned later that he was. He
got into all that excitement through being caught
chewing betel-nut with the other fellow’s fiancee by
moonlight. Maybe she was worth it.
1.11.11. : I’m against wilful slaughter of
birds, as a rule, but 1 honestly admit I have no
compunction about murdering a few black magpies
or molly-hawks. These must not be confounded
with the black and white magpie of the sleek coat
and organ-toned voice. For downright cheek and
mischief I’ll back them against any bird in the
bush; and, to make matters worse, they hunt in
flocks. I put in a few acres of wheat lately at a
time when I had not seen a molly-hawk since thev
ate the last cob of my corn. The day after I sowed
one came along (he was the scout), and an army
has been infesting my pocket-handkerchief farm
ever since. They helped themselves to the grain
before it came up, and now what is up is being
further elevated by the pests, as they pull the green
sprout out to get at the milky grain. I have a dog
trained to chase them, but he will never be very
effective until I buy him an aeroplane, as they sim-
ply hop up into a handy tree. We shot about two
'° Z n n , r We mn’ I,nshot are plentiful
than before. The only consolation is that they make
a fair pot of soup—not turtle, mind you, but eatable
ana welcome.

“Kodak”: Gwendolyn, the golden-haired
boarding-house general, or Belinda or Merely Mary
Anne, had one great fault. In her daily campaigns
against dust and cockroaches she worked like a
whole company of sappers and miners; but when
meal-time approached, and it was time to retreat
from the bedrooms, she would gallop away without
tidying up the field of operations. Later on the
hirer of one of the rooms would arrive home in
the half-darkness and fall heavily over a forgotten
bucket, or be smacked murderously on the chest by
the handle of a broom which resented being trodden
on. But Gwendolyn managed to hang on to the
job till the night the chronic dyspeptic dragged him-
self upstairs as usual and cast himself on "his bed.
That instant a scarlet shriek of agony cleft the
evening stillness, and, as the congregation of board-
ers rose up with their hair on end, a voice full of
tears wailed: “ Oli-h, why did she leave the fender
on the bed?” He had fallen on it with the middle
of his indigestion, and the result was terrible.

“Te Pana” (from Tonga) : The Mormon
has his eye on this bit of coral, and the soul of Jione-
Tonga finds 3'et another pilot that promises smooth
sailing. There are a number of “elders” living under
the palms, mostly young chaps, keen as blades about,-
their work, and prepared to fight all the devils ami
bad whisky in the Friendlies. Education is a big
factor in their campaign: they get at the people
through well-managed schools, with English as' a
medium of instruction. Most of them live a la
Tonga and develop appetites for yam and cocoanut
milk. There is no salary attached to their job; and
any man who comes to Tonga and feeds on native
kai kai, and perspires, and doesn’t drink, and just
exists that Brown Brother may miss a future grilling,
is a hero, to my way of thinking. As far as I
know these gentlemen from Utah do not preach
polygamy. Neither does any other white man here-
abouts, for that matter. MGle singular is very nice,
but Mele plural—no, thank you.

“Vigilans”: “Saccharose” (B. 11/G/’l4)
is on the right track re the climate of North Aus-
tralia. It is so much bunkum to say white men
cannot work there, or that it is not suitable for
long residence. I know the North well, having spent
many years in North Queensland, Kimberley and
the Northern Territory. The people who have taken
up their residence and work thereabouts are a hardy
lot, and so are their children. In Westralia mining
is carried on by white men who have been in the
tropical north for a decade and over. In East
Kimberley the old. reside! ts are warriors as hard as
S. P. Mackay, of horse fame, was born in
West Kimberley. The Edgars, of Derby, have been
there a lifetime. In Wyndham white people live
and thrive. As regards climate, Cairns is enervat-
ing enough for anything, being level with the sea,
but it is only a day’s journey to Herberton, where
the people dress in heavy tweed suits. The climate
of the interior of Australia is the healthiest in the
world. On the tablelands anywhere the air is pure
and life-giving. There are men now living in the
interior of Australia who went there as incurables.
They suffered from chest complaints, asthma, bronchi-
tis and the like; but the pure atmosphere cured
them. The trouble is get-at-ableness. When the
transcontinental railway opens up the interior of
this continent, my tip is .you will see sanatoriums
at Alice Springs ,or thereabouts and on the Barkly
tableland and- Hall’s ('reek, AncJ the wealthy, indo-
lent croakers, sick with, ailments due to lack of any
exercise but drawing dividends, will be the most
prolific and astonished customers.

be used in the Regent Palace Hotel, now in course of erection at Piccadilly Circus:

the largest hotel, we think we may say, in Europe, having between one thousand and eleven. hundred bedrooms.
If you can possibly do it, will you let us have the piano here for the opening of the House; if not, please let us

that it is meant as a compliment to Australia, for the SI RAND 1 ALACL
HOTEL, filled from the day of its opening, has had a great many guests from

able management of the Directors of J. LYONS & CO. is sure to win the confidence
and approval of the public, for their enterprises are the most conspicuous success of modern
times in the World’s metropolis. Their one inflexible rule has always been: “Everything of
the verv best and absolutely genuine. No tips are allowed, no sccict commissions, all
above-board and in favor of the public. And so in leturn they get public favoi.

institutions. The amusing part is that we have been virulently attacked by the German

hold a mortgage over the piano-supply of Great Britain and of Austiaha in paiticulai.

“Vagabond”: It is rather as
“Q. 1.8.” says (B. 2/4/’l4), that Outback Australia
has never fallen back on one or other of the forms
of sun-dried meat, but I must confess that tlie
“biltong” it has been my luck to. sample has not
been us tempting as “(J.1.8.” describes. Any that
I’ve wrestled with has had all the toughness of
well-cured boot leather, and about ;is much taste as
you could extract from india-rubber. “Q.1.8.’5”
description of the “hard, grey crust* (tni the outside”
fits my memory of biltong, but my memory goes
deeper and carries tlie bard grey crust right down to
the middle of the strip and out the other side. Ail
the same, it has its good points, the , chief being
that it will keep indefinitely, doesn’t take any great
harm from being mixed up in the swag with loose
tobacco, tea or flour, emerges none the • worse from
a swim in tlie river, and scorns tlie ice-chest or
meat-safe as a vanity and a superfluity. Of the
various kinds of dried meats I’d recommend “pem-
mican.” It is made with dried, pounded or grated
lean meat, with dried fruit or seeds -'and currants
mixed, and the whole crammed into a clean skin or
canvas bag. Then you pour in hot rendered fat, as much
as the meat will absorb, and tie up tightly. Mix
with water, or better, flour and water, arid boil, or,
at a pinch, oat it raw.
“Reka” : .V solution of tin* problem of tlio
mysterious “bush sickness” that attacks cattle arid
sheep turned out on the land near; Rotorua is one
of the achievements of the Maorihmd; Agricultural
Department. Horses are not affected;, but sheep arid
cattle waste away with a form of pernicious a uremia
in a land of abundance. As about;'.a million acres
of land were affected, the Government ;\vas urged to
turn its agricultural eye on the matter, .and experi-
ments confirmed the theory of experts; that; this land,
heavily timbered in part and apparently”able on the
clearings to support large docks arid Kerris, lacked
some constituent necessary to keep tlie animals alive.
Iron has been traced as the missing, ingfedieiit, arid
on areas treated with a preparation of iron the
sheep and cow now feed and wax fat. . Beyond tlie
boundary the ironless cows still fade slowly away.
Calves taken off the affected soil arid hand-feil with
phosphate of iron consume tlie stuff” hungrily, which
leaves no doubt in the minds of tlie experts that
iron is the one. tiling in life they really yearn
after. The craving is so bad that ‘some covVs
would gladly eat a boiler or the cheek of a com-
mercial traveller.

“Oimai”: .Judge Murray, the Australian
Administrator in Papua, has shifted his point of view
with regard to the working out of Papua’s destiny.
'When lie first went to the country it was enjoying
the Native First policy of Administrator Barton, and
Murray found tlie whites laboring under very harsh
and. irksome restrictions. The new man abolished
many of these, and the anti-native crowd promptly,
came to the conclusion that everything was about to
come their way. But personal experience of the
native and. his would-be exploiters swung Murray off
his first course and drove him to give more protection
again to Brown Brother; whereupon many of those
who acclaimed the Governor while his attitude suited
them, proceeded to put the hoot in, arid have been
doing it ever since. Murray, like other big men, has
not been afraid to own up when lie was wrong, and
tin’s lias not commended him to the “stand-patters,”
who, like most fools, pride themselves on “consist-
ency.” To this must be added sectarian rancor,
which it is astonishing to find in a place like Papua.
He has been badly served by some of his subor-
dinates, too, and these not of tlie junior ranks. Every
Papuan official carries the Governor’s billet in his
mind’s eye, and some of them have done everything
short of actual assassination to work the job their
way. The service needs thorough organisation, and
not till Murray is allowed to get busy with an
axe among, some of his lieutenant's will the tangle of
Papuan affairs be straightened out.

“TV I’ana”: Tlie people who talk about
the employment of Pacific Island girls to solve the
Mary Jane problem should excuse their ignorance.
I’ve had maids on the paysheet in Rarotonga, Tahiti
and the Friendlies, and the collective abilities of the
crowd are not worth tuppence.
They clean the knives on their
valas, wash up in cold water, and
can’t even boil seggs. As for cooking
joints, or sweets, or stews, a bank
clerk could make a better mess
of the business. To give her one
pat on tlie hack, however, let
me mention that she can iron
creases in your white clothes.
The par excellence Island “maid - ’
is a boy. He is willing that you
should teach him to distinguish
between a chop and a blanc-
mange, or a scrubbing-brush and
a potato-peeler. Once having settled it in his dusky
head, the light way of doing a thing is harder to
tear from him than the income-tax from Sandy
McPherson.

“Zamia”: The tick, which no doubt adds
to the cost of our beef, proves very destructive on
all animals except the gohanna. The cat, if ho gets
a tick in his side, will turn double somersaults, and
if not attended to lie will succumb to the attentions
of the parasite. Ditto the dog. But the tough-
skinned gphanna stands as many ticks as can cluster
on him, and goes about his job. as if nothing was
wrong. I never yet saw a gohanna without a supply
on his body.

all the time to blowing-off point. The total saving

J'o nudge her hubby in the side,
Then in his ear a word she said—

The wife’s took bad. Say that, we want
Him quick as he can hurry down.”

The winkers over Moolv’s horns.

According to latest figures the cost of the new
gravitation yards at North Melbourne will exceed the
estimate bv £40,000 ; similar yards at Tottenham
bv £30,000, and the Caulfield line duplication works
bv £49,000. Some £3OOO of this latter sum was
incurred because the alleged engineers built platforms
on curves with too short a radius for the newest cal-
lages, though satisfactory enough for the old dog-
boxes.” Two of these platforms had to be demolished
as soon as finished and constructed with wider curves.
Presently someone will we wanting to know why the
railways—don’t pay.
The motorist has his number taken ofiener m
Melbourne nowadays than he wots of. The .“sports” of
the city, frozen off “two-Up” by the officious conduct of
,T. Hop, have started wagering odd or even on the
last numeral of each car that passes. This gumble
requires neither kip, coins nor skill. All that can be
seen bv a casual John Hop is the passing of money,
as of a man naying a debt or lending another the
price of a pint. Its simplicity is as delightful as its
security is comforting.

At Auckland (M.L.) a man who added milk to his
cans of water was recently convicted (thank the
hord!). Two samples of the mixture showed 23
and 24 per cent, of pump substance. The magis-
trate said it was theft just as much as trading off a
forged bank-note. But he didn’t prescribe the usual
treatment for forging and littering—hard labor. All
the same, he called on the thief who had utteied
forged milk to pass over £2O and Costs, and oideied
that the conviction of the said impenitent malefactor
his name, occupation, place of business, nature of
the offence and penalty—should be advertised in two
daily papers.
“Hiki”: A movement is on foot in Canterbury
(M.L.) amongst the cockies to form some sort of
an association that will do away with the middle-
man and his profits. One Ensor, chairman of the
Canterbury branch of the Farmers’ Union, a power-
ful political and commercial bod}', has sent a
message out to the world that, in his belief, all
middlemen are parasites and hairy cannibals. Some
30 vears ago another set of cowspankers and wool
cultivators thought similarly to Ensor and they
started a Farmers’ Co-operative Association. This
show has grown and developed until now its rami-
fications bulge in all directions. It has also
degenerated (in the eyes of the cocky) into an
ordinary business concern, charging the same retail
price for the goods it sells as any other store. This
the cocky can’t understand, despite the fact that
most of ' him holds shares in the concern (which
pays 15 per cent, and waters its stock). The
present generation of cocky is searching itself
for a proiect which will land the producer and
consumer into direct contact. A conference .has
taken place between the promoters of the desired
scheme and some reus, of the workers’ unions, but
nothing excepting the exposition of the obvious has
so far been produced. And that’s far less satisfying
than, say, beer and eel pie.

“Reka” : Maoriland was housing unawares for years
some celebrated stuff in the Ward-Plomer silver-plate
recently shipped to London and sold at Christie s.
The Maoriland owner, Judge Ward, inherited it from
a Ward who married into the Plomer <or
Plumer) family, and on his death last August the
metal went Londonwards to a nephew. Captain
Ward, who turned it into cash somewhat hastily.
Apart from its intrinsic value (a James I. silver-gilt
cup presented in 1620 to John Plomer brought
£4500, the highest price ever given at Christies,
the place of high prices, for'one lot), the plate was
interesting as having belonged to the Plomers made
famous by Charles Lamb. His “Blakesmoor m
H ” was the Plomer dower-house of Blakesware,
and the fierce Mrs. Battle was herself a descendant
of the Plomers. Lamb’s grandmother, Mrs. Field,
was housekeeper at Blakesware, and he, as a boy,
wandered all over the old treasure house. He docs
not mention the Plomer silver-plate, which has
gained tremendously in value since his day, but in
“Mrs. Battle” he speaks of some of the family
treasures, and in “Blakesmoor” he writes of the
tapestries and paintings of his “old great house, the
demolition of which hurt him so keenly. The plate,
which had been for 50 years in Maoriland, brought a
total of £lO,OOO at the sale.
* * * *
“Josh” : Melbourne’s gas monopoly has been calling
for applications —“accompanied by not less than three
testimonials” —for two positions in its engineering
branch. To discourage anything below the highest
proficiency, it is intimated that “candidates must
be graduates of Melbourne or other University, and
be fully qualified engineers.” The salary offered is
£l5O per annum, which is rather less than the Gas
Co.’s plumbers receive. This shows what a fine thing
it is to spend seven years or so training to be a
professional gentleman.
The original Tory outcry against the institution
of the Commonwealth Bank, on the ground that it
was a Labor Government device for injuring Private
Enterprise and driving capital out of the country,
has now changed to a reasonable criticism of its
constitution. According to the Argus of to-day the
institution needs only a board of directors to make
it safe and sound, and with this modified criticism
nobody will disagree. Under a one-man system of
management, the national concern might in the
future, with another than the present manager in
charge, be guilty of “a nolicy of inflation in sub-
servience to nolitical ends.” The manager ap-
pointed bv the Labor Ministry is safe, sound and
altogether" satisfactory, the Argus admits, but the
Tory journal foresees the possibility of a less satis-
factory appointment being made by a different Min-
istry Hence the need for a supervising board. An
inflat'ional nominee of Fusion, for instance, would he
a calamitous manager. In short, the only mistake
made by the Labor party in its good work of estab-
lishing a national bank was in omitting to provide
a safeguard against muddle in the future, under
autocrat appointed by the friends of the Argus.
“Hamer”: The eussedness of the hen has upset
many people. Towards the end of last yeal t
Sydney egg market, which had been held Up at g
prices by a careful handling of the output, suddenly
collapsed. Speculators had assisted the market
(which is mostly a place were people assemble tor tne
purpose of rooking the public) bv storing awaj
surplus fruit in the hope of a joyful resurrectioi
Christmas; but the degraded fowl, trampling al
decent principles underfoot, put in so much overtime
that when all the storage was full and speculators
were becoming scared at the size of their stock., s 1
plies kept pouring in. The market could not be he (
up any longer ; and all of a sudden the price slumpe
until it went as low as Bd. a dozen. Prices did n
begin to recover until near Easter time; but tne.i
have been high ever since. Recently, however, the
hen has been getting in more unkind work. tne
market manipulators in Sydney held on as long as
possible, but a week or two ago thev had to let go,
and newlaid eggs fell from about 2s. 3d. a dozen
(wholesale) to Is. 6d„ with a prospect of further re-
ductions. The thing was a blessing to the or dinar,'
citizen, who was able to chip his morning shell \titn-
out feeling that he had swallowed threepence And got
little benefit by it ; but to speculators in the business
it was ruinous.
* * * *
“Vigilans”: The question of protecting the Aus-
tralian banana against the subsidised importation
from Fiji is a live one. As yet, the value of the
banana plant is not known in Australia. It is grown
only for the fruit. But in some tropical countries
the banana is to millions of people what wheat is to
the European, rice to the Chinese, rye flour to the
Russian ; it is their staff of life. The banana is one
of the most striking illustrations of tropical exuber-
ance. A plant which in a southern climate would re-
quire many years to gain strength and size is, in the
hot latitudes, the product of 10 or 12 months. No
plant has so many various and useful qualities. For
instance, banana flour is becoming extensively used.
For invalids and children it is superior to arrowroot,
in consequence of its nourishing qualities. In
Jamaica the natives roast the fruit in the wood ashes
and use it as a substitute for damper. They
also boil it and use it as a potato. 1< rench
chefs fry it in batter and serve it up as an ordinary
hot vegetable. Next to the fruit itself the
value is in the fibre, which is made into rope,
and known commercially as manila ‘ hemp. it is
not hemp, but the fibre of a species of banana which
luxuriates wonderfully in the Philippines. The export
trade is large, mostly to the United States, California
and Britain. Vinegar is also obtained from it by a
very simple process. The stem is filled with an
abundant pith, containing a large percentage of starch.
Fiji is North Queensland’s great competitor m the
banana trade, but whereas Queensland is becoming a
white man’s country, Fiji is a land of cheap slavery.
A duty of 3s. per cental is required to enable the
industry to prosper in Queensland. The Daintree,
Mossman, .Mulgrave, Russell and Tully rivers are al
banana districts. The deep, black soil is unsurpassed
in Australia. The chief bone of contention is the
value of the fruit. The Fijian distributers say the
Queensland fruit is inferior, and that the demand is
greater for the nigger-grown article. This is quite
wrong; the Queensland-grown fruit is as good as
the other, and it does not suffer from scale, whereas
I know that two recent shiploads of fruit from the
islands had to be fumigated on account of scale.

“5.E.0.” : Surely it is time sanitary engineers and
town-planners gave a thought to the vacuum-air
system of town scavenging? With a system Of
powerful exhausting plants at suitable points and the
town reticulated with air pipes, the bulk of the
waste could he removed at a fraction of the present
cost. By means of intercepting furnaces the waste
already practically desiccated by being whirled along
in a current of air could be swiftly burnt and re-
duced to an innocuous dust and clinker. Consider-
ing that for wet sewage 20 to 25 tons of water have
to be brought miles to carry one ton of waste away,
the air-vacuum system, if only in
of water, would be an economy. )\ ith
street scavenging and the present system a
ously sweeping up and carting rubbish long d
tanees, the exhaust system, supplemented by
side valves, would, save cartage costs and do array
With the awful “tips.” ;

“.I p”: Landlords who fondly imagine that because
a tenant pays his rent weekly he can be kicked Out
with a week’s notice, got a rude shock in
recently. At Palmerston North a landlord, fwbM
to rebuild, handed a weekly tenant seVeh days tlbtiee
to vanish. The tenant refused In disapjKsar so
hurried]v, and demanded a month’s notice in writing.
This annoyed the landlord,- who sent a hireling to
tear down the verandah and two chimneys. At this
stage the tenant took a turn for the worse, and fired a
wr it for damages at the landlord. In the Supreme
Court the learned Judge sided with the tenant, and
pointed out to all and sundry and otheis that, in
the absence of a specific agreement a tenant is
entitled to a month’s written notice. He then
ordered the landlord to hand over £5O to the tenant
to compensate him for having to do without chim-
neys and a verandah. Thereabouts the landlotd
dropped down dead or something equally desirable.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
T.P. cot so
White and neat,
Battered soon
By Baby's feet;
Taken when
He’s just a year
For the pay-
Ments in arrear!
T.P. suit when
He goes forth
In the world
To show his worth.
If decay
His teeth should sting,
T.P. teeth
Are just the thing!
He becomes
A married man ;
Buys a house
(Instalment plan)
Costly as
The rooms are small;
T.P. furn-
iture and all!
When the fam-
ily arrives,
With the cares
Of little lives,
The expense
Of many things
More T.P.
Upon him brings.
T.P. when
His daughter weds
For the feast
The table spreads ;
While the T.
P. devils smile,
One must do
The thing in style!
T.P. for
A hundred things,
Bankruptcy
Upon him brings ;
So he seeks,
With gloomy groan,
And secures
A T.P. loan !
Full of honors,
All T.P.,
And time-payment
Dieth he!
T.P. with
Him to the tomb
Goes as T.
P. coach and plume!
From the Cra-
Dle to the grave
Man to-day
Is T.P.’s slave;
Then in Heav-
En seeketh he
His allot-
Ment, bought T.P.!
N.S.W.

Phonograph
Each year quite a large percentage of teachers
break down on account of the constant
physical and mental strain of talk, talk, talk
that forms the greater part of their work.
This strain can be practically eliminated by
installing an Edison School Phonograph,
which is niechanically the same as the well-
m
known “Concert” Phonograph. When provided with a Special
Recording Outfit the backbone of all the oral lessons of the day can
be prepared in the teacher’s spare time.
Such lessons as Dictation, Mental Arithmetic, Pattern Reading and
Singing, etc., can be prepared in such a way that the teacher is free
to deal with other details of his work. This should all tend to a
greater proficiency on the part of the pupils and-higher efficiency
on the part of the teacher.
Good Attendances on
Friday Afternoons.
Children look for excuses to stay home on Friday afternoons, but
the Self-Recording Attachment can be made a source of amusement,
and a special attraction for the children on Fridays.
Records can be made in the Schoolroom, and the wide field of educa-
tional and amusement possibilities which this feature opens will be
appreciated by every practical teacher.

HOW OUR GRANDMOTHERS
MANAGED TO LOOK YOUNG
AT SIXTY.
“It is strange.” remarked an old chemist
the other day, “that so many women nowa-
days persist in trying all sorts of injurious
patent systems and complicated treatments,
hoping to restore ruined complexions or
improve the appearance of their skins,
when they might easily do so by the
simple home remedies of their grand-
mothers. In olden times a woman
looked comparatively young at sixty, while
now most women have blemished complexions
long before they are thirty. Yet nothing
new has been invented anything like so effec-
tive as the old-time lotion used by famous
French Court beauties, such as Recamier and
Du Barry, to preserve their youthful appear-
ance. The recipe was so simple: just 2oz.
rosewater. 1 dram tincture of benzoin, and
2oz. ordinary flowers of oxzoin, which any
chemist will supply. Mix the lotion well,
then apply with a soft cloth. If you apply
it regularly it will not only keep the skin
free from wrinkles, but will quickly restore
the dullest complexion. My own daughter
is nearly fifty years old, and in this way she
keeps her face looking so young and fresh
that most people think she has not reached
the age of thirty-five.”
The above advice indicates such a simple,
harmless means to rejuvenate the appearance,
that it is published jor the benefit of lady
readers.*

GRANNY: “Goodness me, Joseph! Why did you change your pretty colored suit for that
ugly dress? It doesn't fit you, dear. Take it off, to please Granny.”
The importing complaint again! \ ic-
toria imports a United States engineer to
look after the electrification of the Mel-
bourne suburban railways. The last time
it imported a railway expert from the other
side of the Pacific it got Tait. Before that
it imported a railway genius and got
Speight. Incidentally it imported some irri-
gation geniuses and got the Chaffeys. In
betw-een it imported Coode. the harbor and
river genius. Looking at the long list of
ghastly results it should begin to feel tired.
Besides, Victoria imported Merz to draft a
scheme for the electrification of suburban
railways, and if it can’t carry out his simple
project with its own brains it had better
import a Premier.
A SAFE BBACK-GATE, POLICY.
LONDON, Saturday.—Naval policy in the Pacific
is the subject of an article in the current number
of the Round Table. “The question develops the
argument,” says the writer, “that the real danger
to Australia is Japan, and that the Commonwealth
is justified in not reposing too much trust in the
Anglo-Japanese Alliance.”
A weird cable from Rome sets forth that
business has been blocked in the Italian
Parliament through asafetida, which smells
something horrible, being poured ovet the
Opposition benches. It isn’t known whether
the Government poured the horrid substance
on the Opposition benches to stink out its
critics, or whether the Opposition poured
the awful thing on its own benches and then
left with its nose in its hand. Anyhow, it
seems a new departure in politics.
The importing disease, which is largely a
result of the foreign loan disease, is making
itself felt in N.S.W. The number of people
employed in local manufactures still grows,
but the expansion is slacking on. In
the increase as compared with o’lf.
amounted to 8913 employees. In 1912 the
increase was 6937. In 1913 it w T as oCLd.
Importation and borrowing and the size ot
the bill left to the infant accumulate very
fast, and the abundance of the local job
accumulates rather slowly.
“Gazeeka”: A feature of the opening of
the session of Maoriland’s Parliament on
June 25 was that the House had no occa-
sion to adjourn as a mark of respect to the
memory of a deceased member
or ex-member. For years past
the opening of every session has
been followed by an adjourn-
ment, and veteran members can-
not remember a recess during
which some politician has not
departed for a land where the
party system is not.
Maoriland, by reason of its
frenzied “loyalty,” once refused
to shut out any nigger belong-
ing to the British Empire, and
being an isolated sort of place
with which the nigger wasn’t
much acquainted, it was able
for a while to. pursue its “loy-
alty” without?' damage. Now
the Hindu' has discovered
Maoriland, and it has discovered
in its turn that Australia, with
its Alien Restriction Act,
wasn’t so foolish after all. The
flapdoodle about “the flag” has
fallen to a discount, and the
Massey Government proposes
to strike the babu from Bengal
and the British Chow from
Kowloon with much the same
education test which Australia has been
using for a number of years. "but 1S
to sav. the colored brother may be asked to
write 50 words in a European language and
allowed 20 minutes in which to do the deed.
Preliminary figures show that, guided by
a “non-borrowing” Labor Government which
has borrowed harder than any predecessor,
Westralia is the first proud Australian State
to fasten a public debt of £lOO on the
shoulders of every man, woman and child
in the country. With the help of the same
sort of non-borrowing Government A. S.
Wales early last year was pushed past the
£100,000,000 mark ; so both honors for dread-
ful finance now belong to the two mainland
States with Labor Governments. -At least,
they still call themselves Labor Governments
at election times.

THE RECOVER [?].
Mr. Cook and Mr. Holman addressed meetings at
Cootamundra on successive nights.
C OOTA: "Either of ’em bad enough, but fancy
mixing ’em.”
It is interesting to observe that while
Labor Premier Holman, in New South
Wales, is holding back democracy from it 3
natural victim, the nominee Chamber, Tory
Premier Massey is leading the fight against
it in Maoriland. True, he is not out to
abolish the Council, but he will do the next
best thing. His Reform Bill provides for
making the Upper House elective by propor-
tional representation, and in order to insure
the passage of the measure about a dozen
new Councillors, pledged to its support, are
to be apnointed. Labor’s autocratic leaders
in N.S.W. seem to be amongst the last of
the old Conservative defenders of Fat’s
Blockade House.
THE CORPSE REVIVERS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
On one side stand the Socialists, threatening to
capture and subordinate the whole legislative machin-
ery of the country to their class aims and propaganda.
On the other side are the Liberal forces united in
an attempt to secure the Federal Constitution and
free government from predatory hands, and restore a
free Parliament to a free people. That is the issue
of the campaign. For some time we have striven to
get things to issue in this way. Now that the way
is clear we appeal to the people to put the Socialists
in their proper place, clear of all possibility of
danger to the Commonwealth. —Joe Cook.
The night was chill. A leering moon,
With streaming drap’ries, like a ghost,
Swung ’cross the night. A whimpering loon
Mid headstones grim (a spectral host
That flecked the gloom) stood quaking, where,
Digging among the rich black loam,
A body-snatcher or a gnome
Threw out the bleaching bones to air.
“What is it?” said the digger Cook.
The frosty watcher shook his head,
Nor gave the bones another look.
“ ’Tis Capital in Flight,” he said.
Cook moved, and in another grave
He put his awful burrow down.
This time the bones were green and brown.
“’Tis Freetrader’ said the watching knave.
Cook dug the graveyard o’er and o’er,
And tossed the grinning skulls about;
And still the watcher murmured “More!”
Till Anti-Sosh was shovelled out.
They spread its rib-bones on the straw,
And with a lengthy piece of string
They threaded up the fearful thing—
And Irvine groaned at what he saw.
“It smells to Heaven. Oh!” said he,
“A mean and shameful thins; I wis
It is if we keep eompanee
With such a loathly sprite as this.”
“We must,” Joe said, “to fright the breath
From voter's free.” But Irvine-moaned.
“ ’Twill only fright those ones,” he groaned,
“Who are already scared to death.” '»

EDDYSON.
THE ACHIEVEMENT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Under the heading of “What Liberalism Has Done
the Sydney Telegrarh (3/7/’l4) prints nearly a
column of the trivial administrative matters fixed up
by Agar Wynne, or Agar Wynne’s office-boy, in the
Postal Department.
To-day my fancy spreads its wings,
My Muse is on the run
To carol all the wondrous things
The Liberals have done.
I see their armies all in line,
And singing as they tramp:
“We made a change in the design
Upon the postage stamp.”
What have you done, you mighty Libs.,
You statesmen stern and rude,
To make the babies on their bibs
Bubble with gratitude?
The answer comes: “We rest content
With this reform sublime —
No stamps to lick on letters sent
In twenties at a time.”
The pallid folk in boarding-dens,
Hash-houses with the ’phone,
May have, for only sixty pence,
Their separate names made known.
To realise : their proudest hopes
The people need not wait —
Pictorial stamps on envelopes
Will make them glad and great.
The crisis of election comes,
The noise of battle calls;
And over all the banging drums
I hear the Liberals:
“Behold the noble deeds we did
While glorious Agar reigned—•
A set of stamps for half a quid
All stamp collectors gained.”
They said they knew a way to meet
Our monstrous public debt;
Some day that job may be complete—
They haven’t done it yet;
But Liberals may walk in pride
Untouched by any fear,
With their code address in the Postal Guide
At two-and-six a year.
N.S.W.
M.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
V. Hemery’s amazing “flying mile track
record” of 31 l-ssec., which he established at Brook-
lands (England) in November, 1909, came to final woe
last week, when L. G. Hornsted, on the same course,
flew over the distance in 28.095ec., equal to 1284
miles an hour. Hornsted is the famous Benz dare-
devil driver. But fast as was his time it is not a
record for the mile, but only for the mile on a
circular track. R. Burman, the reckless Yankee
chauffeur, in April, 1911, on the straightaway course
of Davtona Beach, Florida (U.S.A.), bounded over
1760 yards in 26 2-ssec. —equal to 1414 miles per
hour. Yet although Burman’s dash was very wonder-
ful it does not compare in merit with the wild leap
of Hornsted round the banked track at Brooklands.
That was enough to make even an archangel blink
hard.
The world’s most strenuous motor-car test,
Known as “the Alpine Tour,” was battled through by
74 of the world’s most representative cars from the
14th to the 23rd of June, over 1820 miles, in eight
spasms. The course ran round about and in and
out of the Austrian Alps, over 24 precipices, or rather
passes, every one of which was over 6000 ft. in height
or drop. It was a land that had originally been
full of devils, but the devils had got caught in the
mud and frozen, and were known locally, for want of
a better name, as “roads.” The winner turned up
in a Minerva car, shod with Dunlops and fitted with
a silent Knight sleeve-valve type of engine. Eleven
of the makers had entered teams of three cars, and
the drivers were mostly picked men. This reserva-
tion is necessary, as a couple of Austrian archdukes,
Josef Ferdinand and Henry Ferdinand, took part, and
it would be rank blasphemy to call them picked men.
They seem to be more often picked off. It was a
fine, imposing array of cars at the start, among those
who also ran being the Rolls-Royce, Benz, Austro-
Daimler, Opel, Fiat, Darracq, Vauxhill, Cadillac, Over-
land, Laurin-Klement, Hotchkiss, Puch, Wolseley,
Nag (what a name!) and Armstrong-Whitworth. The
Minerva seems to be booming of late.

The third Test between the English North-
ern Union ATI I arid the. Australian team at Sydney
Cricket Ground was a Homeric battle. The manage-
ment of the English team wished to postpone the
fixture as most of its alleged best men were on the
shelf through injuries, but the Rugby League
pressed for its rights, and a cable whispered round
the world to the English to go in and win—or die.
So they went in with their alleged worst team.
(The Lord save our present team from their real
best!) Up till half-time the English XIII re-
mained intact, and it had jumped on the locals to
the tune of nine to nil. The' Australian pack was
the superior one, but the back machine of the Eng-
lish was like unto the famous Immortal Band or the
Old Guard, which dies but doesn’t drink; and the
most formidable of them was the captain, Wagstaff,
placing in the three-quarter line. His control of the
team was worth more than sixpence, and his indivi-
dual play was akin to that of Lucifer when he
kicked the football out of Heaven. The English con-
certed play was magnificent compared to that of
the Australians, who ceased to bo a team, and
degenerated into a crowd of splendid Private Enter-
prise. each Private Enterprise playing his own
game. Aid semblance of temperate order was lost.
Every man bogged in ferociously on his own potato-
patch. It was in a wild-cat struggle right in front
of Australia’s goal that things began to happen.
One Englishman had to leave with a wrenched aim,
and almost immediately after another twisted his leg
and commenced to limp off—and England (which
does not replace injured men) found itself with only
XI to XIII. But in spite of this the English con-
tinued to attack. Trampling over everything with
the large boots of confidence, and pulling everyone
down like a gorilla struggling with herbs, the Ma
Country scored, and then adding the points led with
14 to nil. The watching crowd was stricken as
near dumb as 35,009 people could be. Later
on, another man was injured, and with 10 to 13 the
English team was hustled back on to its line. A
humble but terrific dust-up occurred, in which men
were rolled over like ninepins and jersevs torn like
tissue-paper. The English fought like a Salva-
tion Army drum at its loudest to keep their goal
intact, and for a long while succeeded. Then Aus-
tralia broke through, as if it was running to catch
a train, but could not add the points. The Eng-
lish defence resumed. Australia hurled its combina-
tion to the winds, while England strove to keep some
semblance of a machine. This country, full of
undeveloped natural resources,, raged like.,the heathen,
but every time the English captain could get his
machine together he let it loose —not in the hope of
scoring, but of carrying the war away from the
English line. And the manoeuvre was successful.
It stalled off the attack till right on time, when
the last charge proved successful and one more try
was registered. But again ’the Australian boofpr
failed to add ‘the points, and then the whistle
made its melancholy sound, and England reeled
from the field, victorious by 14 points to six.
By dancing on St. George to the tune of
21 to three Glebe maintained its position at the
head of the Sydney Rugby Union competition. But
it was a runaway victory which does not leave a
long-remembered drink flavor in the mouth. Most
of the Union games on Saturday last were of the
same nature. Eastern Suburbs utterly obliterated
Randwick bv 30 to three —which was not football
but butchery. University rubbed the ear of Manly
up against the wall of the public-house, the figures
being 22 points to eight. But the most surprising
debacle of the day was the downfall of Newtown,
which failed to score a single point to Western
Suburbs’ 17. That was among the. things reckoned
impossible. The only well-contested struggle of the
day was that in which South Sydney downed Balmain
by 17 points to 13.
The chief interest in the Australian Rules
Section on Saturdav rested on the issue of the
struggle between Newtown and Svdnev, It was
watched by what was just about the record crowd
for the season. The most interested party was South
Sydney, for if Newtown could defeat the premier
team. South’s position at the head of the competition
would be almost unassailable with 40 points to 32,
and onlv two matches to be played before semi-
finals. It was a mighty close struggle, too. First
quarter saw Sydney ahead with nine to three, but
half-time saw a different state of affairs, with New-
town leading with 18 points to 16. Then Newton
lost one of its best men, and the golden hope began
to wither. But the team played all it knew, and at
three-quarter time was still on top with 32 points
to 17. This promising state put fresh hopes and
powers into the Newtown side, and it dashed into
the fray, and amidst tremendous excitement ran out
with 32 points to 26. In addition to enhancing the
position of South Sydney this unexpected upheaval
also made more rosy the chances of Newtown reach-
ing the semi-final stage, to enter which a club must
win to one of the first four places at the top of
the competition. Central-Western shares the second
rank with Sydney. Newtown has 28, Paddington
24, and North and East eight each.
N.S.W. Rugby League’s pockets are bulg-
ing to bursting point, the visit of the English having
proved a veritable treasure trove. Five games in
Sydney yielded over £12,000, representing the gilt
of 186,000 barrackers —-an awful thought. The in-
vading force takes 65 per cent, of the gross, or
£7BOO. Truly the invaders’ bread is buttered on
both sides and his sandwich consists of two slices
of meat and no bread in between.

Saturday’s Melbourne lioofball left both
League and Ass. ‘competitions in an interesting, if
unaccountable, state. At present ’Roy, South, Carl-

between each. They follow ’Wood, Geelong and St.
Kilda, and a single victory will put any one of the
three ahead of Essendon. So the games are strenu-

up to within two competition points—half a game—
of ’Roy, by scoring 10 points more, which result was
mainly due to the Red and White’s accurate kicking
—-64 points to ’Roy’s 54 points being the tally.
St. Kilda and Essendon likewise put up a tall
scuffle, which the faster and better organised ’Dons
would have won but for their untidy goal shooting,
their ultimate 54 points being only enough to draw
against the Saints’ 54 points. ’Wood, as in so many
previous seasons, left itself with a hard contract later
on by fooling away easy games. It went to Rich-
mond and held a fair grip of affairs up to the last
quarter. Then it fooled around, while Richmond
rushed up 20 points and found it couldn’t go fast
enough to overtake the deficit in the time specified,
being beaten by 65 to 49. The situation of ’Wood,
also St. Kilda and Geelong, now is that they must
win five of the next six games to reach the finals,
and it isn’t possible for all to do it. Geelong outed
Melbourne by 68 points to 22 ; and Carlton pa-ssed
the Students their appointed trouncing—B7 to 56.

North leads with 40 competition points, ’Scray owns

and Brighton 28 each. So the quaint suburb of

“H.C.C.” gives a brother pressman a send-
off: Australian rules football in Sydney lost an en-
thusiastic press champion by tile death of “Billy”
Williams. In the Star for several seasons, and
subsequently in the Sun, the Australian code was
kept as well and truly to the front as was possible by
the little man with the youthful figure, big head,
piercing dark eyes, short, quick steps, and inevitable
stick ever upon his left forearm. It was not a case
of devotion to the duty book, but of genuine affec-
tion for the game. He was an enthusiast/ He knew
football thoroughly, and next to his beloved Austra-
lian code he placed- “Soccer.” He knew the strength
to a side of every first-grade club in Sydney, and
acted on East Sydney’s management and selection
committees. Visiting teams from other States did
him reverence and “brothered” him for years.
When Newtown (Sydney) was entertaining St. Kilda
i'Welti.) the visiting team rose to its feet and gave
him “three Sunday-school cheers” (inaudible), “three
lady-like cheers” (a mincing whisper), “three St.
Kilda cheers” (roof-raisers), and “three Australian
cheers” (pandemonium reinforced). , His sterling ser-
vices deserve some -recognition by the Australasian
Football Council. f

The results of the All-England (or what
are generally- regarded as the world’s) tennis cham-
pionships make the Australasian team’s chances for
the Davis Cup look rosy. All four of the Australians
got as far as the semi-finals in the doubles against
the pick of the world’s players (Americans excluded),
but neither Dunlop nor £)oust got any farther.
Brookes and Wilding were left in to argue the final
with the two Lowes, and the Australian pair won.
They are now booked to meet Roper Barrett and
Dixon in the challenge round. In the singles. Dun-
lop and Doust dropped out before the semi-finals, but
Brookes reached the final, where he beat Kroitzheim,
the German champion, after five sets. In the chal-
lenge round he met Wilding, tire holder, and relieved
him of the title after three straight sets, wherein he
gave, according to English papers, “the greatest
exposition of tennis to date.” Summed up this means
that out of six places in the finals Australians filled
four, and two of them got into both challenge
rounds. It’s a pity the American cracks didn t play
—it would have made it easier to pick the Davis Cup
winners. But on their present form, and after the
heap of practice lately, Brookes and Co. ought to just
about relieve Uncle Sam of the trophy. the lust
preliminary Cup round (England v. Belgium) began
on Tuesday.

“0.K.” : The big steeplechase at Flemiug-
ton, next Saturday, which will be worth about ±.1500
to the winner, promises to bring youth and age into
interesting competition. Icon and Anachrion are
four-year-olds of the Grafton breed, with the family
aptitude for jumping that Grafnell and Grafnax
showed of yore. Vanguard, Leah lvleshna, Guncap,
The Miser, Zephuron, Coradjill and others are more
practised ’chasers in the aged division. And all ot
them seem fresh and willing for the fray just at
present. Experienced followers of the game hold
mostly to the doctrine of “an old dog for a hard
road,” the G. NV Steeplechase being a very hard
road in the matter of obstacles. But the young
horses, although not chucked into the handicap,
should be always in the front rank for two miles
bar accidents, and will stay there to the bitter end
if their jumping muscles are sufficiently developed.
On the other hand, the staying ability of some of the
old stagers is beyond doubt. Leah lvleshna and Gun-
cap are previous winners, “aged” now, but not too
old. Coradjill finished third last year. V ith
Guncap in particular, the questions are: Did he beat
anything as gay and sprightly as Icon and Anachrion
two years ago, when he carried lOst. 61b. to victory
in record time, and has he lost any pace since t-hen ?
With only 71b. more to carry, he is better handi-
capped on form than either Leah Kleslina and Corad-
jill, for the latter was merely placed, and the extra
91b. bestowed on Leah for her victory brings her
weight to 12st., which is rather a crusher for a
rather small mare. Besides, that lovely lady jumper
appears not so thoroughly wound up as the son of
Carbinier, though she is looking well. In fact
Guncap, of ail the veterans, is the most dangerous on
paper. When he won the National he was giving a
stone to United Kingdom, who lately ranked as one
of the best, but has gone off for some unaccountable
reason—an internal injury, perhaps. Therefore the
students of steeplechasing regard Guncap firstly, and
Coradjill secondly, as the most likely representatives
of experienced age versus go-ahead youth, Icon and
Anachrion being bovs in comparison.

This paper’s suggestion that Sir Prim and
Beccassine, among the lightweights, were likelv to
stay in front of Obi and Clontaft from start to finish
of the G. N. Hurdle Race was nearly half a mile
short of the full distance, as far as Clontaft was
concerned. For about 24 miles Beccassine, Bill
Brandon and Sir Prim made all the running, which
was fast. Then the two youngest members of the
field were done with, but the aged Bill Brandon (9st.
41b.) kept plugging away in the lead until Clontaft
came along, full of ginger, and settled the argument
in two strides. Clontaft (list. 41b.) won by five
lengths, whilst the eccentric Lord Grey put in a run
which beat Bill Brandon for second place. Thus
Erie A. Connolly, the bold backer and reputed owner
of Clontaft, as well as of Lord Grey, took another
big rise out of the “books.” Obi’s conspicuously bad
running was said to be explained by a twisted plate,
whereas two other previous winners of the National —•
viz., Arquebuse and Wingarara—had no visible excuse
to offer. 1 r the pacemakers and the gee that
finally rushed to the front, nothing ever looked
dangerous bar Dust Cloud, who was utterly pumped
opt at the finish.

If the “noble sports” of Britain take only
the feeblest interest in professional sculling it isn’t
that they get no reminders of the
poorness of the game in its pro-
fessional aspect. Barry and his
friends have always been clamoring
for financial support, and now the
champion is making an apneal for
a subscription on Jim Paddon’s
account. Paddon having sprained
his wrist, Barry has agreed to put
back their race for a fortnight, from
August 24 to September 7, suggest-
ing at the same time that the
“public” will pay the challenger's expenses. If
no cash is forthcoming from the world in general,
Barry will allow the Australian more than a half-
share of the steamboat receipts, which are the nearest
approach to gate-money that scullers can hope for.
One of these days the blessed public will remember
that boat nulling is the one big athletic sport in
which no black man has ever taken the shine out of
the whites, and then, perhaps, the patrons of Chris-
tian sporting endeavor will do something for sculling.
There has been no Johnson, Deerfoot or Kan ji of
the river, thus far. It needs more than a black
man’s pluck and determination to make a champipn
oarsman.
Sydney baseball, in finishing another com-
petition round on Saturday, provided plentv of
excitement for those who find the other sidelights
of winter revelry too aesthetic. The growth of “gate”
is as comforting as hot toddy on a cold night to
those who control the show ; and attendances of over
5000 prove forcibly that the game is flourishing
like a bay tree. The sparkle of the round certainly
belonged to Lane Cove —which has not won a game
this season—and its bulldog fight against Leich-
hardt was inspiring. Half-way through the game
(five innings) the score was four all; but in the
other four biffs Leichhardt scored two runs, while
Lane Cove could onlv steal one. It was the kind
of shave which chills the marrow and builds up a
hatful of respect for the other bloke. Petersham,
in its own backyard, accounted for Mosman-N. Syd-
ney by eight runs to, three; while in the other
matches. Waverlev chewed Burwood and picked its
teeth with the bones by 13 to 7, and Paddington
felled University by six runs to one. The round left
Waverley in the lead with nine wins. Petersham and
Leichhardt being on their coat-tails with eight each,
while Paddington (five) and University (four) are
the only other clubs whose chances are better than
Buckley’s of getting into the semi-final.
The latest in strikes in Sydney is a
disturbance in the charmed circle of pony-racing,
where owners have decided not to race at certain
courses until the boodle is fattened considerably.
The bloated clubs are adamantine, and threaten to
bring ponies from Victoria, and other parts and
territories adjacent. But the Owners’ Association,
which controls something like 700 prads, has
declined to be impressed bv the threat, and has
hardened its heart and stiffened its neck. Some
people say its units have stiffened more than their
necks in their time. However, the owners have to
carry a nosebag, and the clubs have not, and the
weight, of that utensil mav prove the decisive factor.
The sudden and somewhat dramatic over-
throw of Colin Bell, the Moree (Australia) heavy-
weight, by the English beauty pugilist, Bombardier
Wells, was not at all unexpected in this country.
Bell had proved in Australia to be a phenomenally
unreliable, in-and-out fighter. He shaped exceptionally
well since his arrival in Europe, and a sudden and
astounding change of form was about due if he
kept his old sudden habits about him. Of his skill
there was never any doubt whatever, and his hitting
power was admitted by all who had been hit by him.
What Australians had always grievously suspected was
the want of stamina to keep his tail starched and
pokerwise and erect during the long course of 20
rounds. Of the actual fight there is little to be
said. The first round consisted of the usual ex-
changes, alarms and excursions, in which Bell seems
to have thrown out the majority of the punishment,
and to have ruffled his blue-eyed, golden-haired beauty
opponent badly. Bell is not beautiful in excess, or
lovely beyond comparison, and his countenance when
seen in battle array is reminiscent of an over-fried
egg or pork crackling. When the second round
started Bell rushed to close quarters and devoted him-
self to the Englishman’s body. Wells, apparently,
was receiving considerable punishment in a clinch
when he took a sudden dislike to Bell, and broke
from his embrace or caress, and the Australian
dropped his guard. Then the planet burst, or some-
thing, and the next thing Bell knew was that he
had outraged man and all the gods by trying to
hoist himself to his feet per medium of the immac-
ulate pants of referee Eugene Corri, Esq.
Next day Bell’s manager tried to fix up another
match, as Bell was not satisfied, and declared that
the right swing that put him out was a mere fluke.
Also a doctor arose and hurled the Saratoga trunk of
contumely on the management of the affair. He
declared that Bell was unfit to go into the ring
owing to an affection of the nose which made it
possible to breathe through the mouth only. Also,
he was suffering from neuritis of the ear, and to
deaden the agony and make it possible for Bell to
fight he had injected cocaine. This rather throws
clods and slush on the remarks above in re inability
to keep up the tail —but this paper never lies.
Neither does it use bad language, so it will not
express its opinion of the management which allowed
12,0f)0 people to pay their good brass at the gates
of the Olympia to see a man fight under the
influence of cocaine. On second thoughts, it might
be a good idea for boxers to get cocaine injected into
the point of the jaw. The experiment is worth
trying.
Over 15,000 people crowded into Sydney
Stadium on Saturday night, to watch the long-looked-
for battle between Jimmy Clabby and Eddie
McGoorty. Clabby wore dinky trunks of most
emerald green, and what part of McGoorty was not
naked was encased in brilliant red. At 2 p.m.
the stales had declared Clabby to be list, ljlb.,
while McGoorty kicked the beam at list. 61b. But;
in the ring McGoorty looked nearly two stone heavier
and bigger and stronger. Also, he looked like one
of Canova’s boxers. Clabby looked anything else
on earth than a pugilist, and the contest struck one
as being one-sided. But it wasn’t. Had Clabby
shown the same form against Jeff Smith he must
surely have won. Probably it was Smith’s superior
speed that prevented him showing that form.
Where Clabby gets his force from is hard to
imagine, but certainly he has hitting power, and
again and again he stopped McGoorty’s rushes with a
straight left. The only blood drawn during the
combat was what oozed from McGoorty’s mouth after
Clabby’s left, swung from afar, in the fifth round,
had fallen on the gateway of beer, potatoes, beef, cab-
bage, tea and other viands. Otherwise it was a
well-conducted scrap without signs of damage, and
what McGoorty smiles with wasn’t badly wrecked
after all. McGoorty smashed hard all the time, it
comes natural to him ; but Clabby put steam behind
his wallops only occasionally. Clabby was as tricky
as a bag of cats and other females, and though he
evaded some awful blows, he stopped a good many
and demonstrated to a startled audience that he has
an appetite for punishment. He took some terrific
swings apparently unruffled, but it was obvious that
the number he could assimilate must be very limited,
lie bounded round the ring like a rubber ball, and
made McGoorty miss by anything up to- 1000yds.
And then the scene would change and it would be
McGoorty slithering this way and that to avoid an
attack, and hunted for his life, and then lie would
make Clabby miss by a mile. Curiously enough,
it was the smaller man who scored most in the close
fighting, where he uppercut and looped the loop
to McGoorty’s mouth and chin in a surprising plan-
ner. Clabby would race round the landscape to lure
McGoorty into a rushing pursuit, with a view to
suddenly swinging round and meeting the rush with
a counter that would have disarranged an elephant.
But McGoorty was too old a bird to be caught more
tli'an once bv that chaff.
Clabby started brilliantly and gave a splendid
exhibition of skill over sunerior force—or what
looked like superior force. But as the battle went
on it became apparent he was feeling the strain. In
the sixth round the solid-fighting McGoorty all but
got him with a left hook followed by a right chop ;
but Clabby came in off the ropes and uppercut so
viciously 'that McGoorty clinched. The superior
strength of McGoorty enabled him to press his at-
tacks and to sustain his speed. In the seventh
Clabbv made a splendid effort, and charmed the
house with his gallant display, but when he went to
his corner this recording angel is under the impres-
sion that McGoorty was ahead on points. In the
eighth and last round McGoorty came out and at-
tacked with splendid vim. Clabby retreated before
the fury of his onset, and in a wild rush in pursuit
McGoorty landed somewhat low. Clabbv protested,
but was'ordered to box on. McGoorty became in a
hurry. He seemed to have an appointment. He
evidently felt that Clabby’s strength was waning.
He hit from every angle and sent Clabby against the
ropes. There was a sort of whirlpool, and McGoorty,
leaving his jaw uncovered,, swung his left for the
solar plexus.' Clabby fortified himself with Ins
right while he swung the left for McGoorty s chin.
But the blow was not turned aside. It was sent
low. and Clabbv’s swing never landed. With a
muffled remark Clabby staggered back, fell on th»
floor and rolled over. That he had been fouled
was bevond nuestion, but it was purely accidental.
Next moment McGoorty found himself disqualified.
He was struck off the rolls. Clabby was lifted up
and helped from the ring. McGoorty was broken-
hearted. He rushed to pick his opponent up, lint
the referee waved him back. He tried to explain
to the audience, but the audience barked at bun
And then he bowed his head and followed Clabby
from the ring, a pathetic figure. His luck was
out.

TOILET TALKS.
SIMPLICITY is the order of the day as
regards beauty culture. Many women
regularly visit the specialist to have their
wrinkles ironed out with the help of hot
applications, cold creams and massage.
Most of us realise that there comes a time
when no amount of persuasion of this sort
will avail. This process of “wrinkle remov-
ing” is wrong in principle. Both
the hot water and the massag-
ing tend to expand and loosen
the skin, besides softening the
muscular foundation. The very
opposite result should be aimed
at. The tissue should be strength-
ened and the skin tightened to
smooth out the wrinkles. For
this purpose ordinary parsidium
jelly, obtainable at all chemists,
may be used with beneficial
effects. Get about one shilling’s-
worth and apply like cold cream.
The face will quickly look years
younger.
IT is astonishing the number of women
whose faces show a more or less pro-
nounced growth of superfluous hair, and it
is much to be regretted that through ignor-
ance, or wrong treatment, the majority of
them have made matters considerably worse
by causing the hairs to grow more quickly
and stronger than before. It only requires
a little thought to realise the folly of it all.
Removing the surface growth for a few days
may give temporary relief, but in order to
obtain permanent results the hair roots
must be killed. Caustic depilatories are
worse than useless, and on no account
m
should the hairs be pulled out. Apart from
the electric needle, which is a costly and
painful process, any chemist will tell you
that the most reliable home treatment is
pure powdered pheminol. This substance
has a devitalising effect upon the hair roots,
in addition to which it removes all trace of
the objectionable hair in a few seconds. The
recommended treatment gradu-
ally, but surely, dissolves out the
hair follicles, which is the only
method of permanently ridding
oneself of this embarrassing
disfigurement.
HOW often do you see one
of the old-fashioned pow-
der pots on your friend’s dress-
ing table? Not very frequently,
I am sure. They are fast being
converted to a more useful pur-
pose. The truth of the matter
is, women are at last beginning
to realise that powder is the
“root of all evil” as far as their
complexions are concerned, at any rate, and
are wisely discarding the powder puff
for that ordinary, innocent-looking sub-
stance known as cleminite. What they
do is to obtain about an ounce from
their chemist and dissolve it in four table-
spoonfuls of water. This gives a lotion
which immediately imparts a delightful
bloomy appearance to the skin; removes that
horrid greasy appearance, and renders pow-
dering quite unnecessary. The result lasts
all day long under the most trying condi-
tions, and what is far more important, im-
proves the complexion instead of ruining it. !"•>

About Some Fine Legislative Points.
“T.F.M.” on the ethics of barracking:—
Apropos the cry of the Argus and other Fat parties
that it is disgraceful of Labor barrackers to drag the
umpire into the “dirty puddle” made by Joe Cook
and Irvine, it is interesting to recall a case or two.
Some 50 years back, when the Victorian House of
Fat Landlords rejected an Appropriation Bill, and
Governor Darling, in the complications that ensued,
religiously followed the advice of his constitutional
advisers (of whom one George Higinbotham was the
chief), the Fats, who hated Higinbotham, didn’t hesi-
tate to drag the umpire into the turmoil. On the
contrary, the rage of those whom Higinbotham termed
“the wealthy lower orders” was only satiated when
they had made a total wreck of Darling’s official
career. Indeed, some of them were not satisfied even
then, for after they had succeeded in ruining Darling,
they vented their spleen on his wife. Yet it can be
affirmed f 1) that Higinbotham and his colleagues had
a substantial majority of the Assembly at their back ;
(2) that public opinion was overwhelmingly in their
favor.
And when the Fat Landlords again rejected an
Appropriation Bill, sent up to them by Graham
Berry in 1878, the then Governor, George Bowen,
was vilified in the same vitriolic way by the same
faction for the same reason. Because he followed
the advice of his constitutional advisers, who had
overwhelming support in the Legislature and the coun-
try, the machinations of “the wealthy lower orders,”
if not quite so successful as in Darling’s case, did at
least succeed in procuring the transportation of
Bowen to the obscurity of Mauritius. Yet in this
case, also, can it be maintained that no ruling given
by Bowen, as umpire, endangered the Constitution in
the slightest degree.
On another aspect of the matter. On June 24,
1880, the Service Ministry was defeated in the
Assembly on a Constitutional Reform Bill. Next
day Parliament was prorogued for a week, and a
formal request for a dissolution was sent by Service
to the Governor. The request was granted, and
two days later (June 29) the respective “reasons”
of Service and the Governor (Normanby) were, by
the latter’s direction, published in a Gazette Ex-
traordinary “for general information.” Twelve
months later,. Graham Berry being then in power,
a hostile motion was carried against his Government
at the instance of O’Loghlen. On this occasion
when Berry asked for a dissolution, Normanby
refused it, but again he gave his reasons the fullest
publicity. In his official reply to Berry he said,
inter alia, that “if the principle were once admitted
that a Minister had a right to a dissolution whenever
he saw fit to advise one, a vital blow would be
struck at the power, and independence of Parlia-
ment. The Minister would then become the master
of Parliament, and the knowledge that a vote against
the Government might terminate its existence would
act as a constant drag upon the independence of
Parliament, and the exercise of that supervision over
the actions of the Government which it is its duty
and right to exercise.”
Normanby was reputed to be a crass Tory; but
even a crass Tory had more respect for the rights
of Parliament and the people than the “Liberal”
Ministers of to-dav who. while they yell “We trust
the people,” take good care to keep the people in the
dark.
Melbourne’s Harbor Trust is up against
trouble once more. The Naval Board has
intimated that unless better accommodation
is provided in Port Phillip, the future visits
of the flagship Australia—which at present
has to anchor some distance down the bay
—will be adversely affected. The Trust’s
only resource is to ask the naval authorities
how much dirt it will have to dig up to
provide a suitable berth for the flagship.
Melbourne’s struggles to scratch away
enough dirt to keep the sea-traffic away
from other Victorian ports where Nature
has done all the necessary scratching
becomes pathetic.
“Croson’s” Tale of Slumber: —
Two years ago the Melbourne Harbor Trust asked
the ViC. Railway Commissioners their intentions as
to railway facilities for the then proposed new pier
in Victoria Dock. The Commissioners acknowledged
the inquiry, and fell asleep. The Trust also slept, but
awoke in February last and asked the railway bosses
to get busy, as the pier was under way. The Railway
Department regretted its oversight of the matter, and
would attend to it at once —and fell asleep some
more. Railways Minister Maekinnon has been prodded
and has promised to “call for a report,” but appar-
ently he hasn’t yelled loud enough, as nothing gets
any further ahead. In any case, Victoria’s Railway
Minister is a joke or a mere figurehead, as the Com-
missioners do as they think, and concern themselves
about no Minister or Government.
From the Oldest Inhabitant:—
Some old-Sydney buildings in what is now Dalley-
street, and formerly was Queen’s-place, are to go to
the hammer, and thence no doubt to the knacker.
These old-timers were built in the ’3o’s, and are to-
day as they were then, not a brick or stone being
altered. They form the quaintest bit of old Sydney
now left. The first time they were offered for sale
was at Sam Lyons’s auction rooms as far back as
March 2, 1835. They were described as “12 new
brick dwelling houses, counting houses and spacious
vaults.” The houses then were side-on to the Tank
Stream, “over which the new line of Pitt-street is
proposed to be carried to the intended quay behind
Macquarie-place.” The property brought £5OO a
year in those days. Four of these old cottages front
Dalley-street and eight others form Queen’s Court.
In one of them Henry Parkes lived while he was
working at Russell’s foundry, adjoining, at ss. per
day.
“McNorth”: We have our own way of
doing things in the North. Before the en-
forcement of the Early Closing Act Thurs-
day Island’s main thoroughfare was more or
less illuminated by the lights in the Chow-
storekeepers’ windows. It wasn’t much in
the way of illumination, but it was better
than nothing; and when the children of
Asia were told to close their shops and turn
off their smells at 6 p.m. instead of any old
hour, nervous citizens abroad at night avoided
the footpaths and their lurking shadows and
walked in the centre of the road. Then the
local council got busy and erected a com-
plete electric-light outfit. Arc-lamps were
installed in the highway, the dark places
were flooded with light, large Well,
no, they didn’t illuminate the town by elec-
tricity exactly, but at a council meeting the
motion was carried that representation be
made io have certain provisions of the Early
Closing Act rescinded as far as Thursday
Island is concerned. The idea was to exempt
the storekeepers—mostly Chows —from early-
closing. and thereby gain a cheap light; for
the streets from their windows! Unfortu-
nately for this economical device, Home
Secretary Appel leaped on it with both feet.
THE MAJORITY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The proposal of the Maoriland Government to re-
duce the effective vote for national prohibition from
three-fifths of the total poll to 55 per cent, of it will
probably be hailed with satisfaction by the prohibi-
tionists as a good long first step toward a bare
majority , and bv the liquor party with dubious feel-
ings.—Sydney Telegraph.
