The South African Cricket Association
accepted without comment the Australian XI chosen
by the Board of Control for the next set of tri-
angular matches. It was a team not altogether un-
expected, although there was a howl that it was not
first-class—said howl let loose by a few pessimists
who got a nightmare over the dropping of some
alleged ‘‘hig dogs.” The S’African Association, which
is supplying the £2OO guaranteed each player, has
the right to reject the team if it is not up to the
Association’s taste. And, apparently, it was with the
view of bringing about such an objection that the
riot was raised. But the S’African body, very sen-
sibly, did not bite, and apparently reckoned that
the Australian selection committee knew its work
and its men. The team will depart for Afric's sunny
fountains and golden sands in October (not Novem-
ber, as previously arranged), and it will wander round
the Dark Continent for oh months.
* * *
The Australian Rules Footballers in Syd-
ney are burnishing up the house for the biggest
kick-up in the game the Ma Capital has ever known.
Inter-State teams from Queensland, Victoria, Tas-
mania, South Australia and even faraway Westralia
will blow along in quest of the All-Australian Foot-
ball Championship during August, and great will be
the dust raised.
* * * *
One of the final kicks of the Cook
Government is a grant of £lOOO to assist a handful
of athletes to hie unto Berlin to take part in the
next Olympiad. Considering how badly money is
required to provide new rifle ranges in Australia, on
which potential defenders may qualify for our Arma-
geddon, this looks like ridiculous waste. Out on
the Randwick (Sydney) Range on Saturday there
was some fine shooting; but there was not nearly
enough space to accommodate all the would-be
shooters. Sergeant Holdaway banged 32 consecutive
bull's-eyes and T. Rowland amassed 22. Over a
dozen put up “possibles,” and when one thinks of the
damage 1000 men like these might do to an invading
force, it seems a pity to spend money on Olympiads
abroad.
* * * *
At one of John Wren’s mixed race-meet-
ings at Ascot (Melb.) the other day occurred a
rather staggering instance of autocratic management.
Grape Vine was backed very heavily for the Third
Division Handicap, but when the “good tiling” was
lining up for the start it sprang into the barrier and
fell dead. According to orthodox rules of racing the
gee was in the starter’s hands (the barrier being
down), and all bets should have held good. At
Randwick or Flemington the dead horse would have
counted as one left at the post. Grape Vine was
practically a starter who went the long, long journey
on his own. His backers would have lost their money
at any meeting where V.R.C. rules operate. But
Wren evidently didn’t wish Grape Vine’s backers to
lose their money, or the books who had been fielding
heavily against other nags to lose their money. So
he ordered all the surviving candidates to return to
the weighing enclosure, whilst the books were
ordered to return all the money they had received
and start betting afresh. When the delayed race
came to pass an outsider got home, which, of course,
was satisfactory to all parties. The books won, and
the punters who had got longer odds about Grape
Vine’s nearest rivals in the betting than they got
when Grape Vine was out of the market had nothing
to complain of.
* * * *
Maoriland toeball is howling for more
referees —three to control each match. Also, toeball
wants the tw r o touch-line umpires, instead of being any
old woman, to be officials appointed by the union and,
with the referee, to constitute a glorified Rugby
trinity. This is sound sense. Present scribe saw
two important matches in Dunedin (M.L.) recently,
one of which was won by a try that was never scored,
and another lost because a brilliant and perfectly
fair score was ignored by the referee. In neither
case was the worried official to blame, the movement
being entirely unexpected, and the result of a sud-
den flash of brilliance and speed, which his Honor
had no earthly chance of getting up to see. Both
touch line judges, however, were in a position to
know, and did know, the exact truth. So, why
shouldn’t these two men be deputed to watch scor-
ing, etc., and give their opinions when necessity
arises, and so help the referee who, especially in the
Borrowing Isles, generally has his hands more than
full dealing with illegal tactics? It would tend to
do away with a lot of bad mistakes, and would inci-
dentally tend towards a cleaner game. A clever
trickster, working the blind side of the scrum, can
get in his deadly work unseen by one referee, but he
would have a hot time if there were three to dodge.
* * * *
The Football League in Adelaide, having
got its benevolent despot of a president, Cleland,
K.C., back from London, is talking seriously to
players who are anything but the perfect gentleman
on the field. Disqualifications have been handed out
for “having attempted to strike another player,” for
“having disputed decisions of the umpire,” and
“having indulged in unnecessarily rough play” (“in-
dulged” is a good word). One hardened villain was
reported for “having sneered at a steward” appointed
to watch for anything undesirable. But he made
haste to apologise, so he was not needlessly beheaded
in the backyard.
Maoriland Amateur Athletic Association
is jumping with all its feet on the open-order system
of selecting trophies. Recently it was asked to part
up for a set of false teeth, a quantity of nails and
a hammer, and a razor hone, these goods having been
selected as prizes by three different winners who •
had been handed open orders and urged to go forth
and spend the cash as their fancy dictated. The
Association is now reminding all and sundry that
false teeth and packets of nails aren’t trophies within
the- meaning of the Act, or words to that effect.
That Tourist Trophy Contest in the Isle
of Man on June 11 must have been indeed strenuous.
The world's best cars were there to the number
of 23 —and only six finished. Talk about elimina-
tion of the unfit! ! ! Many of the leading makers
entered teams of three cars—and only one team
survived. That was the Minerva, and the units
finished second, third and fifth. All the other teams
were burst before 150 miles had been covered. These
three Minerva machines wore Dunlop tyres, and were
fitted with the Knight Sleeve Valve engines, and the
result proves fairly conclusively that for sustained
strenuous work the engine with the sleeve valve is
the thing. Australian military authorities please
note.
The stake of the Maoriland Trotting Cup,
to be competed for at the Metropolitan Club’s big
meeting at Christchurch in November, has been
raised from 2000 to 2500 sovs. The first horse will
get 1500 sovs., and the remaining 1000 sovs. are to
be cut amongst the next three. The Metropolitan
Club, the most prominent light-harness horse con-
cern in M.L., is booked to scatter no less than
£10,040 in stakes spread over the three days of the
big November meeting. For the size of the
figure the commission collected on the money
passing through the tote is mainly responsible.
The abolition of the raucous bookmaker may put
the horseowner on a bad wicket so far as getting
early bets are concerned; on the other hand, the
arrival of the totalisator puts solid thousands in his
pocket in stake-money when he wins.
The pastime of looping the loop isn’t so
prevalent amongst airmen as some people imagine.
Out of the thousands of human birds that climb
skyward daily in all parts of the planet, recent
statistics show that only 43 have so far done the
trick. France claims 26 of them, Fogland five, Uncle
Samland one, Russia two, Italy two, Peru one, Para-
guay one. and there are five whose addresses seem
to have been lost. Each of the performers risked
his neck in a French machine, which is some advt.
for the aeroplane builders of a country with a sinister
reputation as regards necks.

FREE ELECTRICITY.
Once installed, THE AUTOMATIC WIND MOTOR can be run
practically for nothing: it is driven by the wind and requires
NO FUEL whatever to generate electricity. There is no
Smell or Dirt, NO RISK from Fire, Shock or Explosion.
The power generated will safely light your home in the most modern, convenient and healthy
manner.
The heating of dwelling rooms is thereby reduced to a minimum, and the soiling and air-
vitiating effects of gas, oil and other illuminants is obviated, and, further, the fire risk is nil.
Electric fans can be driven to render hot summer days more bearable* and free the home
from flies, mosquitoes and other winged pests.
The domestic problem is solved by the furnishing of cheap power for vacuum cleaners,
electric irons, washing machines, etc.
A small freezing machine, capable of turning out ten or, in the larger sizes, a, proportion-
ately greater number of pounds of ice per hour, can be driven. This feature in itself s iou <
commend the plant to those who are forced to reside in the hotter districts of Australia, and, in
combination with the furnishing of electric light, will do much to give the country dweller some
of those comforts only at present enjoyed by the city man.
The larger plants can be utilised for driving shearing-machines, chaff-cutters, churns,
pumps and other machines where moderate powers are intermittently required.
Many pumping problems otherwise impossible of solution by the ordinary windmill pump
can be solved by the Automatic Wind Motor, since it can be placed in the most exposed position,
and the power generated transmitted by wires to a pump driven by an electric motor.
We shall be pleased at all times to advise prospective clients as to the size of plant best
suited to their individual requirements, or to explain any doubtful points.

IN
BANANA LAND
There’s none of us can dodge Death all the
time and every time. You outpoint him
badly for 19 rounds, and then he shoots out
an unsuspected left and wins on a clean
knock-out in the twentieth. The other day
in Brisbane a young cyclist was caught by
an electric car, which was half over him
before the motor-man could heave-to. Some-
body crawled under and discovered the lad
under the life-guard, with the pedal of his
machine across his throat, and slowly choking
the life out of him. They pulled him out,
and after the ambulance men had taken him
to the hospital, it was found that he had
escaped from what seemed almost certain
death twice over with no injuries to speak
of. But old battler Death, outpointed so
badly, didn’t lose his block : he just waited
until .the young man was full of confidence,
and then laid him- down and out with
tetanus. The trouble made an entrance
through the gravel-rash on his hands —an
injury so slight that nobody but Death had
noticed it.
‘‘Cleveland”: The hilarity of the loaded
goods-truck that ran amok in N.S.W. (B.
18/6/T4) reminds me of an incident on the
Cairns-Mareeba line (Q.) last year. A
guard’s van which was left standing at Tolga
remembered an appointment to spend the
week-end at Cairns, and started off. As
the van melted into the gloom, Tolga dragged
itself from its beer and cheered the guard,
■who was trying to make up a lOsec. handi-
cap on a down grade; but as no mortal man
could stand the strain, he retired after
500yds., and tore back to the station at
Tolga, where he tried to call up Mareeba
and advise it to bustle its sleeping stock off
the line. In reply he got 84 tons of silence.
Meanwhile, the van careered joyously on
through the night, and passed Mareeba with
a roar like an unpopular decision at a Mel-
bourne football match. The gods that pre-
side over the destinies of railway men worked
overtime that night, for, though Mareeba is
a junction station, ail the points were open,
and no damage was done. At Bibhoora a
steep grade and a sharp curve pulled rip the
runaway, which had done the 26 miles m
20 minutes, or at the rate of 78 miles per
hour. This tops the speed performance .of
Bananaland’s rolling stock. More exciting
still was the chase of a truck with four tons
of dynamite aboard, which broke away from
Stony Creek, on the eyebrows of the Kuranda
range (Cairns-Kuranda line), and followed
a passenger train. This was some years
ago. The passenger train kept ahead, and
the truck nosed its way into Freshwater
station, at the foot of the range, with one of
its wheels jammed and a penitent air about
it generally, while the railway, men offered
tribute to their joss. Taking it altogether,
they seem to have a very good joss in North
Queensland.
“Shark” : Virgil Power, lately summoned
to a Higher Court —not to be confused with
Charles Powers, who was summoned merely
to the High Court —was the last of the
Queensland Judges who wasn’t more or less
a Rechabrte. His capacity was surpassed
only ;by his recuperation. Many a time
friends, seeing how things were going, sus-
pected that there would be no court
morrow; but they always suspected wrong.
His law never seemed to suffer from Ins
conviviality. Only’ about four times in 15.
years were his judgments passed out, and
those times he was probably right and the
Court of Appeal wrong.
A wail from the North:—
Many Cardwell residents came up here with Jimmy
Tyson in the old Talisman between 30 and 40 years
ago, and settled on the Murray and Tally Rivers.
Jimmy had promised a sugar mill; but the black-
labor question impaired his memory, and the settlers
were left stranded. Gamely a few cockies hung to
the land, and still drag produce per dray to an
ancient, one.legged jetty, which is now propped up
with saplings, and is in the last stage of decay.
This jetty was built by Thompson, a private sawmill
owner. Government has done practically nothing for
THE ADAPTABLE PIE.
NED: “’ Struth , Jam, there was a mouse in the larst one I bought, from yeh."
JUMBO: “ Well, what a bargain! A firs'-clars mouse-trap fer a tanner."
the place. When the North Coast railway was mooted
we were given' to. understand that the first sections
would be built at the most payable points. Cardwell
threw up its hat, making sure at last of a jetty and
25 miles of line out to the rivers, which drain
thousands of acres of the richest scrub soil in
North Queensland, now roamed over by savages, but
capable of carrying a population as large as that of
Brisbane. But no line came, and it seems our sec-
tion is to be the last completed. Townsville is 120
miles south, and Cairns 120 miles north, and the line
has been started at 'the two ends, nearest the cham-
pagne. South of Cardwell lies the deep water of
Oyster Point, a beautiful natural harbor well-shel-
tered hy Hinchinbrook Island, and miles in extent.
Behind Cardwell, clothing the foothills, are forests
of ironbark suitable for sleepers ; 200,000 were taken
from here a few years since. Yet the axe only tickled
the fringe of the timber belt. Bloodwood and gum for
piles and girders are plentiful, while on the crest of
the range there are miles of pine, with patches of
cedar, maple, silky-oak, bean, etc. These valuable
timbers are at present beyond the reach of the lone
bullocky. Apart from the wealth in the timber the
soil is a bank on which 100,000 people, growing
sugar, butter and fruit, could draw prosperity. _ But
without either rail or shipping facilities there is no
prosperity even for the mere score or two of settlers
who are' left. Meantime, while the land cries out
an invitation to our northern friends to come south
and make everything yellow, and while Hinchinbrook
offers them safe anchorage for their ships, Tolmie,
the State’s Minister for Lands, weeps because he
can’t find country for settlement without buying out
the big Western sheep men. But even if he could put.
the new men on that Western land, they would be
only starting to get things ready, when here, in the
prolific North, they might be reaping their first
harvest.
Though he claims a great success, Digby
Denham says that to raise a loan in London
is not exactly wine and roses. This is true.
It’s whine and Moses,
The other day, at an agricultural show,
a Airs. Burgess was handed a special • blue
ribbon while competing for the prize for the
best astride horsewoman on the ground. She
was a brisk young settler of 74 years, and
the show was at Emerald, where the awful
climate comes from.
“Fergus”: Queensland buskmen are sub-
scribing to a fund to help Jim Ivenniff, who
comes out of gaol this. year. The opinion of
very many off them is that Jim shouldn’t
have- been there. The evidence of an abo.,
Sam Johnson, hanged Pat Ivenniff and sent
Jim to gaol for 15 years. Sam alleged that
he was a witness of the murder of Doyle
and- Dalke; but the bushman can’t believe
that the Ivenniffs, if they killed two men,
would have let a third look on end then ride
off to hang them. Besides, bushmen have
met too many Sam Johnsons to hang any
man on one Sam’s say-so..
“Rangan”; Brisbane still throws away
thousands of pounds annually in its vain
attempt to scrape sufficient mud off the bot-
tom of the creek to allow big ships to reach
the town wharves. In the last 10 years it
has poured no less than £813,000 into its mud
banks, and the return for that period showed
only £493,000. Since 1903 receipts have
grown from £25,000 to £77,000, and expendi-
ture from £33,000 to £139,000. Six dredges,
the Remora, the Hercules, the Hydra, the
Platypus, the Casuarina and the Mary-
borough, are continually scraping at the mqd.
— : and of these six Brisbane pays for only
one, the Remora. The other five, and, also,,
most off the £320,000 debit, is, charged up
against thq. general taxpayer, y/ho, iji all
probability has q- cr,eek offmis own to, dig out,.
There might be tracq% off sefise in this expendi-
ture if the improyemcqt \yqre permanent,, but
the.river is qontinqajly, drafting sand and mud-
down to take the place, off: the stuff removed;
and then the work goes., on, endlessly. More-
over, an occasional flood will drag a, mudf
bank from some out-of-thq-way spot, deposit
it fair in the middle of the stream, and pass
on without leaving, an address where a, claim
for compensation or, a, summons for assault
and; ungentlemanly conduct can be left, Ijt
is all very foolish and very futile and a. tre-
mendous waste of things too numerous and'
sanguinary to mention.
The Forest Thing.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I was a thing of the forest time.
And you were a thing of the sea ;
And out -of the flood you tried to climb —
Out of the weed and sand and slime,
And the shingled beach, to me.
I was a thing of leaf and bud,
And the sap in the thrilling bole;
And you were an eyeless spawn of mud,
Bubble-breather and brine for blood,
And a blind, impotent soul.
I swung on a branch on a clear, blue sky,
You rolled in a seaweed snare;
I flirted a squib at a butterfly,
Mocked the wind as it rustled by,
And drank of the sun-sweet air.
You sighed in the dark on your poachy floor,
You sobbed on the shell’s sharp rim.
And every ebb from the light-steeped shore,
Would fling you back with a spiteful roar,
To your caverns green and dim.
I drew my life from the grass and mould,
Where the oak and bracken grew ;
And you were but drift where the breakers
rolled—-
Now your hair is pale as the seaweed’s fold,
And your eyes still keep the blue.
My hair is ruddy as good red earth,
And my eyes are warm and dark,
As the bracken cowl ere the frond has birth,
And the bee and the moth have weighed its
worth,
Or the musky cedar’s bark.
Then keen to your tide, O wooer mine !• i
While I cling to my natal tree,
For your lips have a tang of the cold, cold
brine.
And my blood is red as the earth’s red wine,
And I cannot hide the sea !
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, July 4, 1914.
My Dear Tiiargomindaii, —
The seven new appointments to the Legis-
lative Council have been made, and the sus-
pense is over. Brisbane supplies two out
of the seven representatives —"Ted” O’Shea,
sport and solicitor; and Eugen Hirschfeld,
German Consul. The other five are A. J.
Stevenson, ardent Freemason and Liberal;
Curtis, of Rockhampton, auctioneer and old
Sydney Grammar School boy; Campbell,
farmer, of Toowoomba; Ilodel, of Towns-
ville, whose family had newspapers in Thurs-
day Island and Australia North; and Dunn,
of Maryborough; who practically was reared
at the printer’s case, and comes of a very
old newspaper family.
Two weddings happened this week. On
Monday afternoon Gladys Hobday, younger
daughter of deceased artist J. M. Hobday,
married C. A. Powell. Brother P. S. Hob-
day, and sister Gus, who both can do some-
thing uncommon with brush and pencil,
accompanied their Gladys up to the tethering
post. She wore pale blue harness, over-
topped with a pale blue veil caught with a
wreath of pink roses and forget-me-nots. It
was uncommon.
The other wedding, was a matter between
John Macdonald's pretty daughter Isabel
and Maldwyn Davies, the strapping six-foot
son of Mr. and Mrs. W. Davies, of Auchen-
flower. This wedding caused quite a flutter
in flapperdom, since the bride is barely 29
and the groom one year older. Mary Mac-
donald was bridesmaid and Eric Wardrop
next best man. Parson Mer ring ton performed
the necessary ecclesiastical odd jobs.
Mabel Woolcock, the handsome soprano,
who has been singing in Maoriland, has-
signed another sort of contract, and shortly
will become Mrs. Bernard. The male party
to the agreement has been for many years ini
the Bank of Australasia.
Lena Hammond, Gladys Frost and Mar-
jorie Stuart gave a musical recital at
Paling’s Claviola Rooms on Tuesday. The
room was crowded inside, and outside the
tail of the audience spread like the narrative
of a peacock.
The Yacht Club Ball on Wednesday might
was a noble effort and grandiloquent result,
bar the fact that the men were outnumbered!
by about 30 shemales. The decorations,
however, were evenly balanced, and were in
gold and white art muslin. Fluffy bunches,
of yellow wattle were set effectively on the-
supper table. The music was good, the sup-
per was far from being bones and ashes,_ and'
the floor engineer had brought slipperiness ;
to a fine art. There were three buds—
Dorothy Earwaker, who looked very hand-
some ; Miss I’igott, of Toowoomba; and Mis»
Day Bird.
The girls from the Residential College-
had a dance on Friday night, and on the-
same date the first of the Corinda Private-
Assemblies was held.
Maibry Campbell, assisted hy. a number off
well-known local performers, gave a benefit
entertainment for the Hospital on Tuesday
night. It was unfortunate that the public
didn’t support her better, for the show was;
as good as its object.
Yours- affectionately,
AUSTRALIAN JANE.
Thus Minister Tolmie :
The policy of the Liberal Government is: People-,
land, communication and railways.
If the Liberal policy be “People,” how comes-
it that Queensland, which has had so many
Liberal Governments, gets such a miserable-
dribble of immigrants every year? The fact
is both Liberals and Laborites in Queens-
land are frightened of a strenuous immigra-
tion policy: the Liberals because they ard
afraid of swamping the Southern constitu-
encies with Labor voters; the Laborites be-
cause they dread the boot of those of their
followers who only see in immigrants fresh)
competitors in the Labor market.
“Rangan”: The three substantial increases;
in the fares and freights on coastal steamers;
in the past year needs some explaining away
but; at present Rockhampton is trying to- get)
the shipping companies to- explain the fol-
lowing (-abridged) table off freights;;—
Brisbane to'
Rockhampton-to,. same towns;.
Mackay .. . ... 29/6 per ton. 25/-per ton.-
Townsville- ... ... SO/- „ 26/- „
Cooktown . . ... 40/- 35/6
Thursday Island, . . 39/- „ 30./- ~
Brisbane has always had a pull on the-
northern towns by cheap, long-haulage rates*
on, the railways, but then the railways belong:
to, the Government, and the Government is;
a. Brisbane institution.. It costs 17s. 6d;
per ton to, bring goods the 4.00 miles from)
Brisbane to Rockhampton by steamer, yet
with this 400 miles thrown in it is cheaper
to. carry goods from Brisbane to northern-
townsthan from Rockhampton up. So appar-
ently the sea is also a Brisbane institution;.
This seems as good a time as usual for Cen-
tral Queensland to start an insurrection.

A Melbourne paper, speaking with some
pride of a local pickpocket, says: “His
talent for purloining tie- and breast-pins has
won him high distinction among the crooks
of the Commonwealth, who recognise him as
an absolute genius.” Why not? Why may
not genius be an enormous capacity for tak-
ing pins?
The Ship that Never Returned has been
reporting itself somewhat frequently of late.
On June 6 the steamer August (Valparaiso
to Sydney) found the American four-master
El Jarasio deserted and waterlogged. Boats
and papers were gone, and there was nothing
to show when the crew left, or how or why.
A full cargo of timber kept the dismasted
hulk just awash. The August set alight to
what was left, as it was lumbering the
track of Australia-South America vessels.
On February 4 the Ventura (’Frisco to
East London) boarded the corpse of the
Dalgonar. This, the biggest British wind-
jammer afloat, had been abandoned last
year while tramping from Callao to Taital.
Captain and crew were picked up by the
French ship Loire. The Ventura’s skipper
reckons the derelict could easily be rescued
from its lonely pilgrimage. A tale hangs to
the schooner Americana, which vanished
somewhere between ’Frisco and Sydney
about a year ago. Soon after the vessel
took the road a seaman, Louis Buttner, got
his arm caught in a windlass. The yarn
goes that the limb was amputated with a
meat saw. Buttner was put ashore at
Eureka, and lat&r sued the Ameridana’s
charterers for 40.000 dollars; but as the
vessel and all hands were missing his suit
failed, for want of witnesses. Lately the
Americana’s mate turned up in ’Frisco, and
said he had seen Buttner’s smash. His luck
was in for once—he had left the schooner
at Knappton, so there was one less to respond
when D. Jones stepped aboard to call the
roll.
Marshall Lyle addresses the jury:—
I ask permission to reply to the attack upon the
principle of municipalisation (B. 18/6/’l4), in regard
to the rumored intention of the Melbourne City
Council to close the municipal Turkish baths. The
writer of the paragraph claims that these baths
should be closed, because (1) they do not pay; (2)
they are only availed of by a few, and not furnished
for the general public; (3) they are stuffy and ill-
ventilated ; (4) the supporters are either Hebrews
(mostly) or penurious monopolists. The third and
fourth contentions are notoriously untrue. The
Melb. municipal baths are exceptionally well ven-
tilated. As for the users of the baths, they consist
of all classes, but mostly non-Hebrews and persons
hostile to monopoly, such as Labor supporters,
Federal and State Parliamentary Labor members
(| _ can name six Labor leaders who are regular
visitors). As regards the statement that the city
Turkish baths do not pay, and therefore should be
closed, I deny that that is a good reason to close
any municipal sanitary undertaking. There are
various municipal services that do not return a cash
profit, nor even balance their expenses (such as
ordinary sanitary arrangements, public lending
library, and town organ), but are they to be abol-
ished therefore? It is wrong to state, also, that
the city Turkish baths are not furnished for the
public. They are very comfortably furnished.
Likewise, it is inaccurate to accuse the Turkish bath
of being a luxury. Maybe it is to some people, but
to a great many these baths are ordered by medical
men for health reasons. And in this respect public
baths of every kind, hot or cold, are as worthy of
support out of the rates and taxes as hospitals or
asylums. Not the limitation, but the extension, of
the functions of the State and the municipalities, in
regard to the public health, is urgently required to-
day.

The Jobless Multitude.
Mention begins to be made, in a casual
sort of way, of the okl-, old, very old sub-
ject of “the unemployed.” It isn’t a large
problem yet, still it was recently stated
that certain unions, with a total member-
ship of 262,000, had 15,500 jobless persons
on their books. Nearly one-third of these
were said to be on strike, while over two-
thirds suffered from plain joblessness. A
certain amount of wailing arose over this
circumstance, yet it is difficult to see where
any legitimate wail comes in. Despite the
boom and the fuss and the city congestion
and the inflated price of land, the jobless
condition seems, especially in N.S.W.,
wholly natural and reasonable. In the
year 1904 Australia imported goods to the
value of £37,000,000, which was £37,000,000
worth of work which its people didn’t have.
The amount was considerable, yet Australia
made some pretence of paying its way in
1904. In 1913 the Commonwealth imported
goods to the value of £79,700,000 and that
was £79,700,000 worth of work which its
people didn’t have. With an increase of
£42,700.000 per annum in the amount of
work which the people didn’t have it was
quite certain that the unemployed problem
would turn up sooner or later. The goods
which were sent abroad to balance the im-
ports and to cover, in addition, interest on
this country’s public and private foreign lia-
bilities, grew, between 1904 and 1913, from
£57,500,000 to £78,500.000 ; so, as far as
trade was concerned, the work which the
Commonwealth did increased by £21,000,000,
while the work that it didn’t, or that it neg-
lected to do, increased by £42,000,000. In
fact, the work that it did for export was so
far short of the amount that it should have
done, if it wanted to pay for its foreign pur-
chases and to meet the interest on its for-
eign debts, that it had to borrow some
£17,000,000 to square the account. That
is to say, it handed down a new bill for
£17,000,000 for posterity to tackle in the
future, rather than have £17.000,000 worth
of honest work and honest wages in the im-
mediate present. A certain element of
decay must show itself in. a community
that does such things. If a country bor-
rows £17,000,000 in a year to pay for lack of
employment it is liable to get the article.
Also, it deserves to get it. The boom in
unemployment is probably just starting.
Presently, with any sort of luck, it should
become a great: boom. The old man will
lose his tailoring or bootmaking job, and go
around in imported pants and footwear, so
that the infant may, later on, have to pay
for his disreputable father’s outfit. It may
be rough on the infant, but meantime the
old man is busy trying to make the bit of
work which he hasn’t sold to the rich im-
porter. go round, by cutting down hours and
pushing up wages. And every time he
pushes up wages without a corresponding
push up in the tariff he increases the
chances of the rich importer, and adds to
the amount of employment which isn’t here,
though it ought to be. Comparing the
year 1913 with 1912, the increase of popu-
lation was 26,000 less. The total stock of
sheep, cattle and pigs diminished. The
exports diminished.; The imports increased.
The amount added to the public debt was
the biggest in Australia’s record. With all
these diseases coming on the country at once
lack of employment becomes inevitable. The
country is paying for the joy of losing its
billet, or at least it is leaving the bill for
loss of billet to its young son, and its baby
daughter. So it naturally loses its billet.
„ « T
“0.K.” reports some hard sense:—
Von Goes, Consul-General for Sweden in these
parts, nurses two subjects next his heart —one of
them commerce between the two countries, the other
the importation of immigrants to Australia. Whereas
this country’s direct imports from Sweden amount
to £750.000 a year, its direct exports thereto are
inconsiderable. Sweden uses Australian wool and
frozen meat, but it gets them by way of Britain, and
thus both consumer and supplier are taxed by a
superfluous middleman. The Swedish Consul’s
other pet subject is of more general concern. Speak-
ing as one Who doesn’t favor the emigration of any
able-bodied citizens from Sweden (which needs all
the workers it can raise at present), he suggests
that no Australian Government has hitherto begun
to go the right way to procure a large flow of immi-
grants from those countries that have workers to
spare. If Australia wants new arrivals they should
be welcomed as desirable strangers. It is sheer folly
for a nation which spends a lot of money in advertis-
ing for immigrants abroad to treat" the immi-
grant, when he does arrive, in such a manner as
to rob itself of his subsequent gratuitous advertis-
ing. Not what the Agent-General in London says
about Australia, but what the recent immigrant has
to say, is the thing that matters. The desire of the
would-be immigrant is either quickened or deadened
by the reports of his fellow-countrymen who have
actually tried the new land, and one encouraging
report from “a man from our village” is worth two
tons of literature scattered by perfect strangers from
a London office. Australia thus far has made no
attempt to win the friendship of the desirable
stranger who comes with an idea of stopping for
good. Undesirable strangers of the Johnny class
have kindness and consideration thrust upon them ;
strangers of no social “standing”-—assisted immi-
grants and others —are coldly regarded as new-
chums, not to say “blanky newchums,” and made
to feel rather like lone intruders in a hostile
country. And because the public is unsympathetic
to home-sick new arrivals the Government which
imports them is more called upon to make a show
of paternal interest in their welfare. The value of
“first impressions” is commonly admitted to be
great; yet in this important national business no
Government makes it its business to see that the
first impressions are favorable.
Tiie Prohibition crowd in Maoriland has
drawn the sword, and cast away the scab-
hard, and has thrown its hat into the ring,
and burned its boats, and let loose the pup of
war. Hitherto the law has required a three-
fifths vote (60 people out of 100)
to abolish drink in any district, and the
same lot to re-establish drink. The idea was,
of course, that if a majority of one could blot
out beer, and at next election a majority of
one could blot beer in again, the bars would
keep opening and shutting like a devil burst-
ing through a trapdoor in a pantomime and
no one would know where he were. But
now the Prohibitionists propose to repair the
law so that 55 votes out of 100 will rub beer
off the map, not only in isolated districts, but
over the whole archipelago, and they seem
strong enough to do it. So presently, in all
probability, the loan drunk will be the only
bust left to Maoriland, and then the tourist
will sidle off and the distinguished
visitor will stay away, and when the
next contingent comes back blithered from
the Boer war it will be nut in gaol instead of
being received with food and applause.

A Celestial Adventure.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
rnHE painters left the ladder there,
A ladder long and thin.
It seemed like a celestial stair
That led away from sin,
A pleasant pathway to the sky
That any passing wight might try.
My soul was lifted up with beer—
Cold beer that truth derides,
And gives a vision large and clear
Wliere never shadow hides;
But everything that is not true
Is outlined faithfully in blue.
Between the ladder and the wet
That late had splashed my soul,
It seemed an easy thing to get
Up to the heavenly goal.
Where ancient Abraham reclines
And one star in his whiskers shines.
With courage high I set my boot
Hard on the lowest rung.
Intent upon that higher loot
That sainted bards have sung.
If I could climb up through the air
A crown and harp should be my share.
The moon would light my upward way,
A pleasant moon was she;
About her little stars at play
Winked as they glanced at me ;
I fancied that I soon might meet
Their owners in the golden street.
lip, up, from rung to rung I went,
A mile or two it seemed.
Beneath my weight the ladder bent,
The chill airs round me streamed —
Oh, it was good to climb and rise
By that steep pathway to the skies.
Perhaps it was my weight of sin,
Perhaps my load of joy,
But Heaven seemed harder now to win
Than when I was a boy.
Though high and holy my desire
I felt my legs begin to tire.
The spider Robert Bruce observed,
That tried and tried again
A kingly brain to effort nerved,
As Mrs. Cook made plain ;
I thought about Eliza’s tale
When first my knees began to fail.
“Up, up the insect went once more !”
I struggled bravely on,
A window on the golden shore
Above my forehead shone,
And then the Blessed Damosel
Looked out and everything was well.
She leaned across the bar of Heaven,
Three lilies in her hand.
The hair-stars that she wore were seven,
Marking her special brand.
I looked aloft and said, “My dear,
The night is fine, mine is a beer.”
It may be that she drew the draught
From the deep wells of light,
But as she looked on me she laught
And everything in sight
With sheer enjoyment twisted round
To hear that rich melodious sound.
From the steep pathway of the sky
She drew me gently in.
The room was carpeted and dry,
And free from trace of sin.
Three angels sat at supper there
Looking quite nice in evening wear.
Some portions of a heavenly cray
Were singing on their plates,
They catch the crimson things they say
Near to the Pearly Gates.
While shallows of the jasper lake
Are full of oysters all may take.
These things may really not occur
Exactly as I think,
But it was good to sit by her
Who gives the angels drink,
To watch the pictures in the room
And know my soul had ’scaped its doom.
The Blessed Damozel and I
_Were soon the best of friends;
We sat on cushions of the sky
Just where the moonlight ends;
With rapture not devoid of haste
I put my arm around her waist.
Some flights of seraphs came and went
With giggles on the stair.
But all my happy soul was pent
’Neath balmy wafts of hair;
The Blessed Damozel had lips
Like red geranium petal tips.
She lent her lips for me to use
When I should feel inclined ;
Tall cherubs passed in threes and twos
But did not seem to mind
Dur clear souls spooning in the night
Beneath a pink electric light.
I might have there remained in bliss
A thousand years or so
Exchanging happy kiss for kiss
Till it was time to go.
But some large person winged and stout
Shook his wide plumes and blew me out.
The wind was like a cyclone blast,
As Lucifer I fell,
I feared that I might bump at last
Upon the lid of Hell;
Rut kindly fate on my behoof
Fixed matters with a laundry roof.
She set me gently on the ridge,
And when the morning broke
I was as one who forms a bridge
Across a gulf of smoke.
I found my seat distinctly hot
Upon that cheerless chimney pot.
The painter came and took me down —
A kindly soul and brave.
Who volunteered for half-a-crown
My fallen soul to save—-
And on my homeward way at seven
I looked aloft and sighed for Heaven.
. M.S.W.
PAT O’MAORI.
Charity.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
UNDER the new-leaved oaks, tracing
their lucent green against the elec-
tric lights besieged by whirling moths,
Companies A, B, C and D went through their
drill. Compulsory service was made glad
and voluntary by the spirit of emulation and
c-amaiaderie that grew in the touch of shoul-
der to shoulder, the common aim of regular
marching and the sound of tramping feet.
Everything was young—the leaves and the
boy soldiers and the moon and the night.
Everything was strong and decisive —the out-
lines of the Moreton Bay ligtrees, the search-
ing lights, the stride of the companies.
And Westall and his girl were young and
strong and handsome, and respectful or
themselves and of each other. And the regi-
ment’s baud, playing farther east in the
Domain, filled the overflowing of the night
with beauty and romance.
Westall and his girl, seated on a bench
under a tree, felt all that courageous life
about them, lie had told her that he had
that day been "raised” another 450 a year,
and she listened to the thrilling exploit with
the admiration the degenerate Desdemona
gave to the stories of the alien Moor. And
then she told him of the number of words
she had typed that day at Is. (Id. a thou-
sand, and he regarded the achievement as
epic.
"Good girl.” he said. “We’ll soon be able
to be married.”
“Oh, go on!”
‘Wes, we will.”
“Oh, not for a long time. We’re so young.”
“No fear. I’m 24 and you’re 20. And I
was born when my father was 23.”
“You mustn’t say that.”
“Well, I won’t—but you know. Suppose
we were married. I needn’t come and walk
about waiting for your mother to let you
out, and you needn’t be too frightened of
your father to ask me to your place. We’d
have a place of our own then, wouldn’t we?”
“Yes, I suppose.”
“And I'd look after you and love you till
it was time to die.”
“You’re a dear boy.”
“And you’re all the angels that were ever
kidnapped out of Heaven. And I’d go round
introducing you, ‘My wife, the angel, came
down in the last shower.’ ”
“Silly boy!”
lie put his arm around her and kissed her
without a word. The jackal of the park
who walked in the shadow saw the innocent
action, and took heart at the thought of trade
and finance being toward.
“Form fours —form twos —slope arms—
right about—march !”
The companies of Australia’s hope moved
off the grass and marched eastward down
the reverberant paths of asphalt. The dis-
tant band began to wail —
Bluebell, my heart is breaking;
I’ve come to say good-bye.
Westall and his girl followed to the great
avenue of Moreton Bay figs that line the
carriage road, and in the darkest places of
the grass plots before the carriage road is
reached he clasped her waist, and her head
found his shoulder, so that they walked with
difficulty. But they were not conscious of
walking at all. The sense of touch was subli-
mated till it became almost ethereal. The
soft carpet of the grass was buoyant as air,
and the air bore them up as if it were salt-
water. They murmured plans to one an-
other, and the jackal, skulking in the shadow
of the trees, chuckled to himself cruelly as if
he were a starved hunter and they the care-
less birds entering the net.
A distant word or two of command to the
retiring companies. The band wailed its
pathos to march-time :
Good-bye, my bluebell,
Farewell to you.
“Let me go, Jack! Take me home! It’s
late!”
“We’re walking that way all the time,
sweetheart. A kiss—just one more.”
“No, no.”
“Yes —one.”
“There then.”
Mid campfires gleaming,
And shot and shell,
I will be dreaming
Of my own Blue Bell.
“You’ve crushed my hat, Jack. Let’s walk
quicker.”
But they walked still slowly, and the jackal
in the shadow chuckled again.
“Bli’me,” he said. “He looks a toff. Nothin’
under a quid for me.”
The captains and the shouting were lost in
the night as the lovers walked towards the
Woolloomooloo gate. By a bench under a
great eucalypt, growing bravely from a rift in
a tremendous outcrop of sandstone, Westall
halted, begging for a minute, only another
minute, before the tramcar and her father’s
house claimed her. They sat down quite
calmly and contentedly, and very near to-
gether. And the jackal, as one who sees the
reward of Enterprise and Industry within
reach, crept behind them in the cover of the
great tree, and settled himself within five feet
of the bench, waiting to dash upon the stage
in the role of outraged Virtue and rinar the
curtain down with a demand for damages.
Below lay the gleaming sea with the masses
of figtree. foliage between; Woolloomooloo
ugliness was softened by the night; the fairy
lights of the distant steamers shafted the
water tremulously; the South Head threw
wavering bands from its sectors, and star-
shine was over all.
“We’ll be happy,” said Westall. “We'll
have a house —all our own —in time.”
“Oh, dear ! That will be nice.”
“I’ll make the plans myself. We’ll build
it with a little quadrangle, so that every
room can have a door on both sides.”
“Won’t the draught be too strong, Jack?”
“No. Fresh air never killed anybody. And
I’ll plant flametrees and flowering gums and
jacarandas and poincianas—you ought to see
them in Brisbane —and a bougainvillaea on
the north end, and magnolias in the quad-
rangle.”
“That will be lovely, dear. How do you
think of it all?”
“Oh, it just comes to me when I think
of what I want to make home like for you,
I’d like to steal the Garden of Eden, but, as

Do you realise that Pains in the Back, Limbs or Joints, Weak Heart, Irritability,

Kidney disease comes on quietly and may take you unawares, for often
its symptoms are put down to other causes—a serious mistake in many cases.
Karly symptoms are pain in the back and groin, bladder irritation, stiff or
aching limbs, scarcity or excess of urine, sediment, night sweats—chilly, moist
or swollen extremities —drowsiness, blurred sight, dizziness and morbid depression.
For when diseased the kidneys fail to purify the blood, so that uric acid
and waste matter colled! in the system, causing rheumatism, stone, dropsy,
gravel, sciatica, lumbago and other complications.
- Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills are a tonic for the kidneys, which they
heal and strengthen, enabling these all-important organs to rid the system of
poisonous deposits that are setting up disease throughout the body.
Keep the kidneys well and they will keep you well, but you must get
the right medicine.

SYDNEY WOMAN’S TESTIMONY.

me to use Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills, and I procured some. The first few bottles gave me
relief, and 12 bottles completed my cure. I am now quite free of the dropsy and backache, and the
secretions are regulated. Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills have cured me completely and permanently,
for I have been quite well for two and a half years now. I cannot sufficiently praise Doan’s'Back-

“The bloke’s balmy,” said tlie impatient

There’s nothing more like guilt faced with

bly.
The girl felt as if she were dying of shame.
Wild rage tilled Westall. In another in-
stant he became cold and decided, and
walked on—paltering, sparring for time.
“I—l haven’t got much,” he said, and
the jackal believed that he had won.
‘Dub up,” he said, walking with Westall,
who edged him into the light. With ltis
left arm supporting The Beloved he put the
right into his pocket. The jackal smirked
and lifted his head to praise his own cun-
ning and in a flash the hand was withdrawn
from the pocket. It was a handful, not of
money, but of pain. The fist landed on the
point of the prowler’s jaw. He had no time
to shriek. His heels flew up and his head
crashed upon the sandstone, and he went
out.
“Run, dear; run,” said Westall, and the
girl found new strength and ran.
They slackened their pace by the Wool-
loomooloo gate, and the soft-hearted Westall
had time to think.
“I must put you in a cab,” he said.
“Why? Oh, why?”
“His head crashed terribly. I may have
killed him.”
“The brute.”
“But to kill a man, that’s terrible.”
He trembled at the thought of it. The
stern man of action became again the
dreamer of a house with a quadrangle set
with magnolias and poetry on a Hat roof,
and purple bougainvillaea growing against a
copper-green dome.
“I might halve killed him,” he said. ”1
must go back.”
She could not gainsay him then. He found
a cab and said good-night, bought a flask of
appropriately bad brandy for the man he
had killed, and climbed the asphalt to the
Domain.
He lifted the jackal, whose breath smelled
as if he had been murdered not so recently
as memory would swear to. Then as Westall
poured a little of the acrid spirit between
his lips he was joined by two large women
with a perambulator—fairly divided between
a sleeping child arid the family laundry—a
tubby old man. and a tall, thin, solemnly-
drunken young man who was eating trotters
from a paper bag. A street accident is the
grand opera of such, and they asked for the
plot anti the cast all loudly and all to-
gether.
“I found him here a little while ago, and
I went for brandy,” said Westall.
The jackal groaned, and Westall con-
tinued hurriedly: “If yoif don’t mind holding
him and giving him the brandy I’ll go for the
ambulance.”
“Righto,” said the gentleman with the
trotters. “Gimme the barrel.”
The jackal groaned again, and Westall,
hurriedly handing the spirit and giving up
his place to the self-important and solemn
drunk, walked off’ slowly enough to prevent
suspicion being excited and quickly enough
to keep up his character as the compassion-
ate stranger in search of help.
And then he heard the jackal cough, and
he moved more quickly into the shadow.
There he ran.
“Have a swig, mate,” said the man with
the trotters.
The jackal groaned, drank as ordered,
coughed and cursed them all. Then he sat
up and groaned again, and tenderly rubbed
his injured head.
“Who did it, mate?”
“Oo found me?”
One of the fat women became shrill and
eloquent.
“A beautiful man,” she said. “A Chris-
tian —that’s what he is. He found y’ lyin’
there dead an’ wont away and bought brandy
to bring yer to.”
“Oo did it, mate?”
“A big bloke. Oh, me ’ead! About as
big as two p’leecemen. Says to me lie’s
goin’ to have a flat roof on a round ’ouse
made o’ copper, ’its me on the ’ead with a
mallet and steals two pound. Oh, me ’ead !”
“Nev’mind. The young feller will bring
the ambyerlance.”
“Oh, will ’e? Oh. me ’ead!”
“Yes —an’ the p’leece ’ll come an’ make
yer comfortable.”
“Eh, what? I don’t wan’ to be made
comf’t’ble. I’ll get ’ome now—gimme tli’
brandy. Oh, me ’ead ! If I c’d only see the
cow that ’it me! Oh, th’ world’s ’ard on a
poor bloke!”
“But what about the beautiful man what
brought y’ th’ brandy?” said the fat woman.
“A good S’maritun he was.”
"Hol'd bless ’im,” said the jackal after
drinking from the flask and tenderly caress-
ing the bump on the back of his skull as
he staggered down the path. “Gord bless
’im....but I’d like t’ see th’ cow that hit
me.”
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.
Imitation.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
HORI paid his billiard score and re-
, . . ~ •*. ,1
moved Ins sporting suit a d
person it encased into the darkness,
“Great coves the Maories,” commented
the shabby little man. who, for the past ten
, , , , , , • ..... *-„i,in cr Hio
minutes, had been absoiled »
flies run races on the bread and cheese at the
far end of the bar. “Beggars for imitating
the pakeha. Drinks the same tipple; cuts
his corns with a razor; drives a motor-car,
, . ... „ , ■. 0.,+
and stops his teeth with go (. > " K ‘
terers of the white man, except in the art ot
breeding fleas and blowing down the girl’s
neck. Styles hereditary in these cases.”
lie sizzled some soda-water into the glass
of vellow stuff, bit at it, and wiped his lips.
,: T . . . . . „
I once put m an hour or two at a native
settlement and had the time of my life.
Brown-eyed girls, bare legs and dried shark
—a great combination. Kind of harbor in a
gale of wind it was to me. I’d been out on
the road with a side-show—‘Brofessor
Slidem’s Lot’s Wife Illusion Co.’ I played
the pillar of salt in the fraud, and it was
astonishing the effect the star performer
had on the rest of the company. Dry! We
drank by day, by night we drank, and the
boss at the pubs we visited worked overtime
breaking down the whisky.”
The barmaid, hearing the last word of the
sentence, poured out two more potions, and
the siphon sizzled again.
“Poor business we played to; but for a
montli wo jogged along, and chased away
the animal that prowled about the back of
the tent. Then the salt, and the thirst, and
the Scotch cookies conspired to defeat us.
They won easily, and we emulated the sons
of the desert. A week later, somewhere be-
tween two towns about a thousand, miles
apart, I overhauled the treasury. A silvered
penny, an imitation ten-dollar bill—one of
those old “Dollar Princess” advertisements
—and ten pence in coin of the realm, did not
spell bed and breakfast and a beer. There
was nothing left me but the hope that the
pubs at the next town might be uneducated,
So I ‘padded’ it on and walked and walked
until my throat was lonely—so lonely that I
commeneed to think of a nice, clean way of
dying. Then, on rounding a turn in the
road, I spotted a Maori village. The sight
did the Coroner out of a guinea. Whew!”
I took the outburst of air as a hint, and
the yellow stuff was poured out once again,
He still had traces of salt about him.
“It was not a very attractive place,” he
went on, after saying “sluek.” “A few fowls
pecked at a heap of rotten kumeras; some
dogs snoozed in the shade of a tottering ti-
tree fence, and a score of sharks, hanging
from a pole shrouded with big. blue flies!
most literally hummed. Of a long chap, who
reclined near the fish, I inquired the distance
to the nearest town.
"‘I dunno,’ he replied, squinting up at
the sun. ‘(food ’prse t’ree o’clock ; poor fel-
ler moren five hour, I tink. You got te
sikerette?’
“I hadn’t—unfortunately; but he didn’t
regret the fact a bit. He was a decent kind
of Maori, and escorted me inside the fence
and pointed out his whare. and the . spot
where Henare killed a pig at the last tangi.
Over by the meeting-house a few perspiring
individuals, fat, hot and greasy, blew down
some battered brass instruments and produced
a bewildering mixture of noises. A tattooed
old chap was standing beside a slab of rock
hung from an overhead staging. He appeared
to have his hands full of coins and bank
notes. This was interesting, and I asked
Hemi the why and the wherefore of the dis-
play. ‘Oh. that feller build te new church.
They gettim to lay te stone, eh. Put in te
utu —te money, te shirrin’, te polin', te halfer
karown —everyting underneath. All te same
te pakeha, eh?’
“I was not too sure about that —and I felt
that brummy dollar-bill in my pocket. True,
tbe white man, when laying a foundation
stone, was in the habit of placing bank notes,
coins of the realm and cheques—yes, that
was it —cheques. Er —did the old man have
a cheque to place beneath the stone? The
pakeha always saw to that. ‘My word, you
stand te game, ehoa,’ commented Hemi.
‘First time I hear ’bout te cheque. Taihoa,
I ask te ole feller.’
“He jabbered a string of noises at the
tattooed party, and the crowd seemed to get
interested. The band ceased its wailings
and butted in with questions. Soon half a
dozen persons were in the midst of a boiling
argument. Hemi sorted out tilings a bit for
my benefit, and explained that the natives
were desirous of doing exactly as the pakeha
in the matter of laying foundation stones.
They had plenty of money, lint, unfortu-
nately, none of the people had a cheque in
their possession. ‘Plenty hard luck to us, I
tink.’ he concluded.
“Exactly 1 So 1 respectfully requested
that I might, be allowed to offer the use of
such an article. It represented only a trivial
ten pounds, but might serve their purpose
well enough. Furthermore, on account of
their hospitality, I would accept nine
pounds in cash, for the cheque, thereby
donating a sovereign to the general fund.
Hemi talked to the crowd, and I shivered a
bit in the hope that they would bite. The
jabbering went on for a long time, and at
last the old fellow asked to see the cheque.
I handed over the ‘Dollar Princess’ adver-
tisement, pointing to the pale green ‘Ten’
thereon, which settled the business. He
passed me nine good golden sovereigns, and
I stayed just long enough to see the bit of
paper buried beneath half a ton of rock, and
hear the band play. Yes. thanks ! In a
medium glass this time. I’m thirsty.”
